## R&D #45 survey (started 2026-05-23)

8 fresh angles, none in shipped list (#6-44 — including just-shipped `disk_io_latency` / `slab_audit` / `watchdog_inventory` / `net_proto_counters` from cycle #44), modules tree (160 files), or parked bench (27.2/27.5/27.6/27.8 + 28.2/28.3/28.6/28.8 + 29.2/29.4/29.5/29.6 + 30.4/30.6/30.7/30.8 + 31.5/31.6/31.7/31.8 + 32.2/32.6/32.7/32.8 + 33.3/33.5/33.7/33.8 + 34.5/34.6/34.7/34.8 + 35.5/35.6/35.7/35.8 + 36.5/36.6/36.7/36.8 + 37.5/37.6/37.7/37.8 + 38.5/38.6/38.7/38.8 + 39.5/39.6/39.7/39.8 + 40.5/40.6/40.7/40.8 + 41.5/41.6/41.7/41.8 + 42.5/42.6/42.7/42.8 + 43.5/43.6/43.7/43.8 + 44.5/44.6/44.7/44.8). Stdlib + jsonschema only, single-GPU desktops / homelab focus (RTX 3090 reference), GUI-controllable from Svelte 5 settings.

| # | Feature | Effort | Fit | Why it matters / first module |
|---|---------|--------|-----|-------|
| 45.1 | **`/proc/net/nf_conntrack` + `/proc/sys/net/netfilter/nf_conntrack_{count,max,buckets,tcp_timeout_*,udp_timeout*,generic_timeout}` + `/proc/net/stat/nf_conntrack` netfilter-connection-tracker saturation + per-protocol timeout + insert-drop auditor — distinct from shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` (sysctls only, configuration side), shipped `net_proto_counters.py` from #44 (TCP/UDP stack counters), and shipped `nic_ring_audit.py` from #43 (NIC-driver drop counters) — this is the *netfilter-table-pressure* side: how many flows is the conntrack table tracking right now, how close to the wall, are insert-drops happening silently?** | S | 5 | NO shipped module reads conntrack-table state — yet on any homelab box running Docker, Podman, libvirt, or k3s, the netfilter conntrack table is the silent #1 cause of "LAN inference works sometimes". Every docker bridge adds NAT + MASQUERADE rules that create one conntrack entry per outbound flow; default `nf_conntrack_max` on a 32 GB box is ~262 144, but a single misbehaving container that opens 50 K short-lived TCP connections (Prometheus scrape every 1 s × 30 targets × 60 s windows) plus an Ollama instance fanning out HF mirror probes can hit the wall in 4-6 hours; once full, `nf_conntrack_count == nf_conntrack_max` and the kernel emits `nf_conntrack: table full, dropping packet` to dmesg AND `/proc/net/stat/nf_conntrack`'s `insert_failed` + `drop` columns increment — meanwhile the user sees "Home Assistant says LLM is unreachable for 30 seconds then recovers" and nobody connects it to conntrack-table overflow. Format : `/proc/net/nf_conntrack` is one line per tracked flow (`ipv4 2 tcp 6 431999 ESTABLISHED src=192.168.1.42 dst=192.168.1.100 sport=54321 dport=11434 ...`), `/proc/net/stat/nf_conntrack` is per-CPU table (`entries searched found new invalid ignore delete delete_list insert insert_failed drop early_drop icmp_error expect_new expect_create expect_delete search_restart`). Auditor detects: (1) **table-fill rate** — sample `nf_conntrack_count` at 5 s intervals, project time-to-full at current insert rate; flag if <30 min runway, (2) **insert-drop cascade** — sum per-CPU `insert_failed` + `drop`, flag any positive value as critical (means packets dropped silently), (3) **TCP-TIME_WAIT bloat** — count entries in `TIME_WAIT` state; if >50 % of table, recommend dropping `nf_conntrack_tcp_timeout_time_wait` from default 120 s to 30 s, (4) **per-protocol breakdown** — TCP/UDP/ICMP/unknown ratios reveal "300 K UDP entries from a chatty mDNS storm" or "200 K SCTP entries from a misconfigured WebRTC daemon", (5) **bucket-imbalance** — `nf_conntrack_buckets` should be `nf_conntrack_max / 4`; if user bumped `max` without `buckets`, hash collisions slow every packet by 100-300 ns. First module : `nf_conntrack_audit.py`, GET `/api/nf-conntrack-audit?window=30` returns `{count:48312,max:262144,utilization_pct:18.4,buckets:65536,collisions_estimate:"low",insert_failed_24h:0,drop_24h:0,states:{ESTABLISHED:12104,TIME_WAIT:31802,SYN_SENT:42,CLOSE_WAIT:8,FIN_WAIT:12},protocols:{tcp:44218,udp:4012,icmp:82},top_offenders:[{src_cidr:"172.17.0.0/16",entries:31200,reason:"docker-bridge NAT churn"}],verdict:"WARN",issue:"TIME_WAIT 65.8 % of table — bump `net.netfilter.nf_conntrack_tcp_timeout_time_wait=30`",time_to_full_h:null}`. Pairs naturally with shipped `net_proto_counters.py` for end-to-end "LAN inference jitter root cause" ribbon. |
| 45.2 | **`/proc/mdstat` + `/sys/block/md*/md/{array_state,sync_action,sync_speed,sync_completed,resync_start,mismatch_cnt,degraded,raid_disks,bitmap/*}` + per-member `/sys/block/md*/md/dev-*/{state,errors,slot}` software-RAID array health + resync-throttling + member-state auditor — distinct from shipped `disk_health.py` (SMART-level individual-drive health) — this is the *array-level* picture: degraded? resyncing? mismatch count growing? sync hogging PCIe bandwidth during inference?** | S | 5 | NO shipped module reads MD-RAID state — yet many homelab GPU rigs run RAID1 for `/var/lib/{ollama,models}` resilience or RAID10 for HF cache; when one disk fails, the array degrades silently to read-only redundancy and the next failure is total loss; when a scheduled monthly `check` operation kicks off at 3 AM Sunday (default Debian/Ubuntu cron), `md0_resync` saturates the SATA controller for 6-12 hours, and any GGUF mmap fault from `nvme0n1` on the *same* PCH-attached SATA controller stalls 200-500 ms — the user sees "Sunday morning inference is mysteriously slow". Format : `/proc/mdstat` is a multi-line text dump (`md0 : active raid1 sdb1[1] sda1[0]` + bitmap/recovery line), `/sys/block/md*/md/` exposes machine-readable per-array state. Auditor detects: (1) **degraded array** — `array_state ∈ {clean,active}` is OK, `degraded_disks > 0` or `array_state ∈ {readonly,read-auto,clear}` is CRITICAL; surface "md0 degraded — sdb1 FAULTY (errors=142), replace before next failure", (2) **resync hogging bandwidth** — `sync_action ∈ {resync,recover,check,repair}` plus `sync_speed_kb_per_s`; if speed >50 MB/s during inference hours, recommend `echo 1000 > /sys/block/md0/md/sync_speed_min` + `echo 50000 > sync_speed_max` to throttle to 50 MB/s ceiling, (3) **mismatch_cnt growing** — silent bit-rot or controller bug; any nonzero value on RAID1 is suspicious, persist across boots and flag if delta >0, (4) **bitmap-write amplification** — if `bitmap/can_clear == false` for >1 hour, write amplification stays 2× for that period (every write touches the bitmap superblock); surface, (5) **scrub schedule absence** — read `/etc/cron.{d,daily,weekly,monthly}/mdadm` to detect missing scrubs (no shipped module checks cron files but stdlib `os.listdir` is fine). First module : `mdraid_health.py`, GET `/api/mdraid-health` returns `{arrays:[{name:"md0",level:"raid1",state:"clean",degraded:0,raid_disks:2,active:2,sync_action:"idle",sync_speed_kb_per_s:0,mismatch_cnt:0,bitmap:{chunksize:67108864,can_clear:true},members:[{name:"sda1",state:"in_sync",errors:0},{name:"sdb1",state:"in_sync",errors:0}],verdict:"OK"}],last_scrub_age_d:8,verdict_overall:"OK"}`. |
| 45.3 | **`/proc/sysvipc/{shm,sem,msg}` + `/proc/sys/kernel/{shmmax,shmall,shmmni,msgmax,msgmnb,msgmni,sem}` SysV-IPC shared-memory / semaphore / message-queue inventory + leak detector — for CUDA-MPS, PostgreSQL, X11, and stale-Jupyter shm leaks that silently eat host RAM** | S | 5 | NO shipped module reads SysV IPC state. Format : each `/proc/sysvipc/<kind>` is a tab-separated table with `key`, `id`, `perms`, `size`, `cpid`, `lpid`, `nattch`, `uid`, `gid`, `cuid`, `cgid`, `atime`, `dtime`, `ctime` (shm) or similar (sem/msg). Detects: (1) **CUDA-MPS stale shm segments** — when `nvidia-cuda-mps-server` crashes (segfault, OOM-kill, manual SIGKILL), it leaves `/dev/shm/nvidia-mps/*` AND a kernel SysV shm segment with `nattch=0` and `cpid` pointing to a dead PID; these persist until reboot or manual `ipcrm -m <id>`; sizes 100 MB-2 GB per stale segment; on a 24 GB RAM rig that's been up 60 days, can accumulate to 8-12 GB of unreclaimable host RAM, (2) **PostgreSQL shm leftover after crash** — `pg_ctl restart` after immediate-shutdown can leave the previous postmaster's shm segment; new postmaster picks a different key and starts fresh, old segment lingers, (3) **X11 MIT-SHM leak** — long-running X clients (especially Chromium running WebGPU dashboards) leak MIT-SHM pixmap segments on tab-close, (4) **semaphore-set exhaustion** — `kernel.sem` defaults limit total semaphores to 32 000; some Jupyter kernels leak semaphore sets per-cell-execution; once hit, new Python multiprocessing fails with `ENOSPC` and the error message is misleading. Auditor surfaces: total shm bytes, top-N segments by size with PID + `nattch` + age, flag segments where `nattch=0` and `cpid` no longer in `/proc` as LEAKED with paste-ready `ipcrm -m <id>`. First module : `sysvipc_audit.py`, GET `/api/sysvipc-audit` returns `{shm:{segments:42,total_mib:8421,leaked:[{id:1048576,size_mib:2048,cpid_dead:12345,cpid_cmdline:"nvidia-cuda-mps-server (dead)",age_d:14}],leaked_total_mib:2048,verdict:"WARN"},sem:{arrays:128,total_semaphores:892,limit:32000,verdict:"OK"},msg:{queues:0,verdict:"OK"}}`. |
| 45.4 | **`/proc/sys/kernel/random/{entropy_avail,poolsize,uuid,boot_id,write_wakeup_threshold,urandom_min_reseed_secs}` + `/sys/devices/virtual/misc/hw_random/rng_{current,available,quality}` entropy-pool + hwrng-source auditor for boot-time TLS / SSH / VPN-handshake jitter** | XS | 4 | NO shipped module reads entropy state. On modern kernels (5.18+), `getrandom()` blocks until the CRNG is seeded; if the box has no hwrng (`rng_current=none`) and uses systemd's `random-seed.service` lazily, the first 30-90 seconds after boot have all TLS handshakes (HTTPS to HF, OpenWebUI WebSocket, llama-server `--ssl`) stalling on `getrandom()` for 200-2000 ms each — the symptom is "browser takes forever to load the dashboard for the first minute after boot, then it's instant", but most users never connect it to entropy starvation. Auditor detects: (1) **no hwrng configured** — `rng_current=none` while `rng_available` lists `tpm-rng-0` or `intel-rng` → surface `systemctl enable rngd` or `rng-tools` install, (2) **entropy_avail dipping below `write_wakeup_threshold`** in steady-state → flag CRNG-reseed pressure, (3) **`poolsize` mismatch with kernel config** — useful info for `/proc/config.gz` correlation in #45.7. First module : `entropy_audit.py`, GET `/api/entropy-audit` returns `{entropy_avail:3456,poolsize:256,boot_id:"...",hwrng:{current:"tpm-rng-0",available:["tpm-rng-0","intel-rng"],quality:1024},uuid:"...",verdict:"OK"}`. |
| 45.5 | **`/sys/fs/{ext4,btrfs,xfs}/<dev>/{lifetime_write_kbytes,errors_count,first_error_time,last_error_time,mb_groups,session_write_kbytes}` per-filesystem runtime health + write-amplification + error-history auditor — distinct from shipped `fs_mount_audit.py` (mount options only) and shipped `disk_health.py` (SMART) — this is the *filesystem-internal* side: how many bytes has ext4 written over its lifetime, has it logged any errors, how big are the mb_groups for fragmentation?** | S | 4 | NO shipped module reads per-FS sysfs. Each filesystem driver exposes runtime metrics under `/sys/fs/<fstype>/<dev>/`. Ext4 exposes `lifetime_write_kbytes` (total writes since mkfs; useful for NVMe wear-life projection: `lifetime_write_kb × write_amplification ÷ tbw_total = wear_pct`), `errors_count` + `first_error_time` + `last_error_time` (any nonzero errors_count means the FS encountered a corruption it recovered from — invaluable for "is the disk failing or did fsck pre-mount fix it"), `mb_groups` (multi-block-allocator group count, surrogate for fragmentation). Btrfs exposes per-`devid` allocation, scrub state, qgroup quotas. XFS exposes `stats/stats` (long counter line) + `error/fail_at_unmount`. Auditor detects: (1) **NVMe wear via ext4 lifetime_write** — combine with shipped `disk_health.py` SMART `Total_LBAs_Written` for cross-check (catches FS-level over-reporting from sparse writes), (2) **silent FS error history** — `ext4 errors_count > 0` means kernel logged at least one filesystem inconsistency event; the user's `dmesg -T | grep EXT4-fs` may have rolled over, but the sysfs counter persists, (3) **btrfs scrub overdue** — read `/sys/fs/btrfs/<UUID>/devinfo/*/scrub_speed_max` + `last_scrub_time` from `btrfs scrub status` JSON if available, fallback to age-of-`scrub.status` file, (4) **session_write_kbytes vs lifetime ratio** — high session-write on `/var/lib/ollama` ext4 indicates HF-cache churn, useful UI data point. First module : `fs_runtime_audit.py`, GET `/api/fs-runtime-audit` returns `{filesystems:[{fstype:"ext4",dev:"nvme0n1p2",mountpoint:"/",lifetime_write_kib:182394112,session_write_kib:421344,errors_count:0,mb_groups:512,verdict:"OK",nvme_wear_pct_estimate:14.2},{fstype:"btrfs",mountpoint:"/var/lib/ollama",last_scrub_age_d:42,verdict:"WARN",issue:"btrfs scrub >30 days old — bit-rot risk"}]}`. |
| 45.6 | **`/proc/net/{route,ipv6_route,fib_trie,arp,fib_triestat}` + `/sys/class/net/*/{neighbour_pressure,gc_thresh*}` routing-table + ARP-neighbor + FIB-trie depth auditor for "split-horizon LAN inference" misconfig detection** | S | 3 | NO shipped module reads routing/neighbor tables. Format : `/proc/net/route` is the IPv4 main-table FIB (Iface, Destination, Gateway, Flags, RefCnt, Use, Metric, Mask, MTU, Window, IRTT), `/proc/net/arp` is the ARP cache. Detects: (1) **default route ambiguity** — two interfaces both have `0.0.0.0` default with equal metric → asymmetric routing breaks NAT/conntrack mid-flow, classic "VPN + LAN both up" homelab footgun, (2) **stale ARP entries** — `INCOMPLETE` or `FAILED` neighbors that the kernel keeps retrying every probe-interval, (3) **gc_thresh3 saturation** — when ARP table > `/proc/sys/net/ipv4/neigh/default/gc_thresh3` (1024 default), kernel emits `neighbour table overflow` and packets drop silently; common on /16 LANs with mDNS noise. First module : `net_route_audit.py`, GET `/api/net-route-audit`. Lower-fit than 45.1-45.5 because most desktops have simple routing, but homelab boxes with VPN + LAN + docker bridges hit this. |
| 45.7 | **`/proc/config.gz` (when CONFIG_IKCONFIG_PROC=y) or `/boot/config-$(uname -r)` kernel-build-config inventory + per-feature posture auditor (CONFIG_PREEMPT, CONFIG_HZ, CONFIG_NO_HZ_FULL, CONFIG_TRANSPARENT_HUGEPAGE, CONFIG_SLAB_FREELIST_HARDENED, CONFIG_PREEMPT_VOLUNTARY vs CONFIG_PREEMPT_RT)** | M | 3 | NO shipped module reads kernel build config. Decompresses `/proc/config.gz` with stdlib `gzip` or falls back to `/boot/config-$(uname -r)` plaintext. Surfaces: CONFIG_PREEMPT mode (`NONE`/`VOLUNTARY`/`PREEMPT`/`PREEMPT_RT`) — affects inference latency variance by 0.5-3 ms p99, CONFIG_HZ (250/300/1000) — affects timer granularity for fan-curve responsiveness, CONFIG_TRANSPARENT_HUGEPAGE (`always`/`madvise`/`never`) — already shipped `thp_audit.py` reads *runtime* knob but not *kernel-compile* default, CONFIG_NO_HZ_FULL — tickless-CPU support for HPC isolation. Heavier-weight because config parsing is hundreds of relevant symbols, even at top-N curation. First module : `kernel_config_audit.py`. |
| 45.8 | **`/sys/devices/system/machinecheck/machinecheck*/bank*` + `/sys/devices/system/machinecheck/machinecheck*/{tolerant,monarch_timeout,check_interval,trigger}` MCE-bank inventory + per-bank-mask + scrub-interval auditor for silent ECC corrected-error tracking across CPU+RAM+PCIe** | M | 3 | NO shipped module reads MCE banks directly (shipped `edac_ram_ecc.py` reads EDAC subsystem which is one of several MCE consumers; raw MCE banks are the lower layer). MCE bank registers (Bank 0-N depending on CPU vendor: Intel Skylake-X has 25, AMD Zen has 6) accumulate corrected-error counters that EDAC may or may not surface depending on driver coverage; on AMD platforms with `amd64_edac` partial coverage, Bank 4 (NB) errors miss EDAC entirely. Heavier because per-CPU-vendor decoding required. First module : `mce_banks_audit.py`. Lower fit because shipped `edac_ram_ecc.py` covers 80 % of the use case for RTX 3090 + Ryzen reference rig. |

### Top 4 picked for cycle #45

**45.1 nf_conntrack_audit** (S) — `/proc/net/nf_conntrack` + `/proc/net/stat/nf_conntrack` netfilter-table saturation + insert-drop + per-protocol-state auditor (docker/k3s bridges silently filling conntrack table, TIME_WAIT bloat)
**45.2 mdraid_health** (S) — `/proc/mdstat` + `/sys/block/md*/md/*` software-RAID array-level health + resync-throttling auditor (degraded arrays, Sunday-3-AM scrub hogging SATA bandwidth during inference)
**45.3 sysvipc_audit** (S) — `/proc/sysvipc/{shm,sem,msg}` SysV-IPC shared-memory leak detector (stale CUDA-MPS shm segments after MPS crash, PostgreSQL shm leftover, semaphore-set exhaustion)
**45.4 entropy_audit** (XS) — `/proc/sys/kernel/random/*` + `/sys/devices/virtual/misc/hw_random/*` entropy-pool + hwrng-source auditor (boot-time TLS/SSH handshake stalls when no hwrng configured)

## R&D #44 survey (started 2026-05-23)

8 fresh angles, none in shipped list (#6-43 — including just-shipped `irq_rates_audit` / `block_queue_audit` / `zoneinfo_audit` / `nic_ring_audit` from cycle #43), modules tree (156 files), or parked bench (27.2/27.5/27.6/27.8 + 28.2/28.3/28.6/28.8 + 29.2/29.4/29.5/29.6 + 30.4/30.6/30.7/30.8 + 31.5/31.6/31.7/31.8 + 32.2/32.6/32.7/32.8 + 33.3/33.5/33.7/33.8 + 34.5/34.6/34.7/34.8 + 35.5/35.6/35.7/35.8 + 36.5/36.6/36.7/36.8 + 37.5/37.6/37.7/37.8 + 38.5/38.6/38.7/38.8 + 39.5/39.6/39.7/39.8 + 40.5/40.6/40.7/40.8 + 41.5/41.6/41.7/41.8 + 42.5/42.6/42.7/42.8 + 43.5/43.6/43.7/43.8). Stdlib + jsonschema only, single-GPU desktops / homelab focus (RTX 3090 reference), GUI-controllable from Svelte 5 settings.

| # | Feature | Effort | Fit | Why it matters / first module |
|---|---------|--------|-----|-------|
| 44.1 | **`/proc/diskstats` + `/sys/block/*/stat` + `/sys/block/*/queue/io_poll_delay` + `/sys/block/*/inflight` per-block-device I/O-latency-histogram + queue-depth + inflight-request time-series auditor — distinct from shipped `nvme_iosched.py` (which is just the `scheduler` knob), shipped `block_queue_audit.py` from #43 (which is the *static* queue tunables like `nr_requests`/`read_ahead_kb`), and shipped `disk_health.py` (which is SMART-level health) — this is the *runtime workload* side: how deep is the queue right now, how long do requests sit in the device, are GGUF mmap page-faults stalling behind dm-crypt write-back?** | S | 5 | Shipped `block_queue_audit.py` (just shipped #43) reads *static* per-queue knobs from `/sys/block/*/queue/`, shipped `nvme_iosched.py` reads only the `scheduler` knob, shipped `disk_health.py` reads SMART — but none watch *live I/O timing*. The runtime workload picture lives in two pseudo-files no shipped module reads: `/proc/diskstats` (14 columns per block device — reads completed, reads merged, sectors read, ms spent reading, writes completed, writes merged, sectors written, ms spent writing, I/Os currently in progress, ms spent doing I/O, weighted ms spent doing I/O, plus discard counters in modern kernels) and `/sys/block/*/inflight` (current read + write requests in driver queue, useful for queue-depth saturation detection). Sampling at 1-second intervals over 30-60 s and computing deltas reveals: (1) **GGUF mmap thrash latency** — when llama-server mmaps a 40 GB Q4_K_M model from NVMe and the page-cache is cold, the kernel issues 4 KB read-ahead in lockstep with the inference forward pass; if `nvme0n1`'s `await` (ms per I/O) exceeds 5 ms while inflight queue depth caps at 32, prompt-eval t/s drops 40-60 % on first prompt; surface "nvme0n1 await 12 ms (was 0.4 ms 30 s ago), 31 in-flight (queue limit 32) — model loading thrashing page cache, run `vmtouch -t` to pre-fault `mistral-7b.gguf`", (2) **dm-crypt write-back stall** — homelab boxes with LUKS-encrypted `/var/lib/ollama` show `dm-0` await spikes >100 ms during ollama snapshot writes because kcryptd thread is single-CPU-bound on Zen non-EPYC; the symptom is "ollama server freezes for 3 s every 60 s during pull" but nobody connects it to dm-crypt write-back; flag with paste-ready `cryptsetup --perf-no_read_workqueue --perf-no_write_workqueue refresh <name>` fix, (3) **discard storm during model deletion** — `rm -rf ~/.cache/huggingface` triggers fstrim-equivalent discard cascade on `nvme0n1`, await spikes >500 ms, GPU PCIe DMA stalls because PCH NVMe and PCIe x16 share root-complex; surface as time-coordinated event correlation. First module : `disk_io_latency.py`, GET `/api/disk-io-latency?window=60` returns `{devices:[{name:"nvme0n1",await_ms:0.6,svctm_ms:0.4,inflight:2,util_pct:12,reads_per_s:340,writes_per_s:18,read_kib_per_s:21504,write_kib_per_s:128,baseline_await_ms:0.4,baseline_p99_await_ms:0.8,verdict:"OK"}],hot_path:[{ts:1716480000,device:"dm-0",await_ms:142,reason:"dm-crypt-stall",remedy:"`cryptsetup --perf-no_write_workqueue refresh cryptroot`"}]}`. Pairs naturally with `pcie_histogram.py` for end-to-end "where does the prompt-eval-time-budget go" UI ribbon. |
| 44.2 | **`/proc/slabinfo` + `/sys/kernel/slab/*/{slabs,objs_per_slab,object_size,total_objects,objects,partial,cpu_slabs,reclaim_account,sanity_checks,trace,store_user,deactivate_to_tail,deactivate_to_head}` SLUB slab-cache leak + fragmentation + per-cache growth-rate auditor for long-uptime homelab rigs** | S | 5 | NO shipped module reads kernel-slab accounting — yet `/proc/slabinfo` is the *only* stdlib path to detect kernel-memory leaks in dentry/inode/buffer_head/anon_vma caches that silently eat 4-12 GB of host RAM after 60 days of uptime. Format : header `slabinfo - version: 2.1` + columns `name <active_objs> <num_objs> <objsize> <objperslab> <pagesperslab> : tunables <limit> <batchcount> <sharedfactor> : slabdata <active_slabs> <num_slabs> <sharedavail>`. Per-cache `/sys/kernel/slab/<cache>/` exposes finer-grained SLUB-allocator state (per-CPU partial slabs, deactivation counters, sanity-check overhead). Sampling deltas over 24-72 h reveals: (1) **dentry-cache explosion from HF cache scans** — `huggingface_hub` periodically `os.walk()` traversals of `~/.cache/huggingface/hub` create 200 K-2 M dentry entries that the kernel keeps in `dentry` cache even after the walk completes; `slabinfo` shows `dentry` active_objs growing 50 K/day with no corresponding inode-cache release, eventually `vfs_cache_pressure=100` reclaim becomes a hot-path that adds 200-400 ms latency to llama-server mmap, (2) **kmalloc-256 / kmalloc-512 leak from nvidia kernel-module** — nvidia.ko allocates small kernel objects per CUDA context; jupyter notebooks that don't call `cuda.empty_cache()` + restart leak 5-50 MB/h into `kmalloc-256` slab, after 30 days kernel uses 1.5 GB just for stale CUDA descriptors — invisible to `nvidia-smi`, only `slabinfo` shows it, (3) **buffer_head + bio explosion during heavy NVMe writes** — `ollama pull` cascades 100 MB of `buffer_head` allocations into slab cache; if `vm.dirty_ratio` is tuned aggressive (`60`) for inference workloads, buffer_head doesn't reclaim and crowds out anon-mem on 32 GB rigs. Surface top-N caches by 24h growth-rate delta, flag any cache growing >10 MB/day with no objects-freed counterpart. First module : `slab_audit.py`, GET `/api/slab-audit?top=10&window=24h` returns `{baseline:{collected_at:1716393600},top_growers:[{name:"dentry",size_kb_now:842112,size_kb_24h_ago:51200,growth_kb_per_h:32954,objs_active:3201234,objs_total:3812000,verdict:"WARN",remedy:"echo 2 > /proc/sys/vm/drop_caches"},{name:"kmalloc-256",size_kb_now:1502720,growth_kb_per_h:1840,verdict:"INVESTIGATE",remedy:"check nvidia-smi for stale CUDA contexts: `fuser -v /dev/nvidia0`"}],total_slab_kb:6121024}`. |
| 44.3 | **`/sys/class/watchdog/watchdog*/{identity,timeout,pretimeout,status,nowayout,bootstatus,options,fw_version,state,pretimeout_governor,access_cs0}` watchdog-device inventory + heartbeat-coverage auditor for headless rigs — distinct from shipped `hw_watchdog.py` (which just reads enable/disable) and shipped `watchdog_setup.py` (which writes systemd `RuntimeWatchdogSec=`) — this is the *device-side* picture: how many watchdogs does the box expose, which one is iTCO vs ipmi vs softdog vs nvidia-watchdog, are timeouts staggered correctly?** | XS | 4 | Shipped `hw_watchdog.py` reads the high-level kernel-watchdog enable state, shipped `watchdog_setup.py` writes systemd `RuntimeWatchdogSec=` — but neither enumerates the *actual character-device watchdogs* exposed by the platform. Modern desktops + homelab boards expose 2-5 simultaneous watchdogs (Intel `iTCO_wdt`, `ipmi_watchdog`, `softdog`, sometimes `nvidia_watchdog` from open-rm 555+, AMD `sp5100_tco`) — each at `/dev/watchdog0..N` with metadata mirrored under `/sys/class/watchdog/watchdog*/`. Reading `identity` reveals the underlying driver name (e.g. `iTCO_wdt`, `Software Watchdog`, `IPMI Watchdog`), `timeout` is the current timeout in seconds, `pretimeout` is the early-warning notification timeout, `bootstatus` is a bitmap revealing *why* the box last reset (cardrop / fan-fail / over-temp / watchdog-bite — invaluable for "did the box reboot due to GPU lockup or PSU brown-out?"). `nowayout` indicates if the watchdog can be stopped once opened (single-shot vs daemon-restartable). Auditor detects: (1) **systemd grabbing the wrong watchdog** — systemd picks `/dev/watchdog0` (first enumerated); on Ryzen boards `sp5100_tco` may enumerate AFTER `softdog`, so systemd ends up petting `softdog` (purely userspace, won't fire if kernel deadlocks) while the *real* hardware watchdog sits idle, (2) **timeout staggering missing** — if `iTCO_wdt` timeout = `ipmi_watchdog` timeout = 60 s, both bite simultaneously and the IPMI BMC logs `Power Cycle` while iTCO has already issued `Hard Reset`, producing inconsistent boot reasons; surface "stagger iTCO to 60 s + IPMI to 120 s so IPMI is the second-chance failsafe", (3) **bootstatus history** — read `/sys/class/watchdog/watchdog0/bootstatus` and decode bits (`WDIOF_OVERHEAT|WDIOF_FANFAULT|WDIOF_EXTERN1|WDIOF_EXTERN2|WDIOF_POWERUNDER|WDIOF_CARDRESET|WDIOF_POWEROVER`); persist the value across boots into the dashboard so the user can answer "why did my rig reboot at 03:42 last Tuesday — was it watchdog-bite or kernel panic?". First module : `watchdog_inventory.py`, GET `/api/watchdog-inventory` returns `{devices:[{name:"watchdog0",identity:"iTCO_wdt",timeout_s:30,pretimeout_s:0,bootstatus:0,nowayout:false,options:0x83b3,fw_version:0,state:"active",pet_source:"systemd"},{name:"watchdog1",identity:"Software Watchdog",timeout_s:60,nowayout:false,state:"inactive",pet_source:"none"}],coverage_verdict:"WARN",issue:"systemd petting softdog (watchdog1) instead of hw iTCO (watchdog0) — kernel deadlocks will NOT reboot",remedy:"set `RuntimeWatchdogDevice=/dev/watchdog0` in /etc/systemd/system.conf"}`. |
| 44.4 | **`/proc/net/{netstat,snmp,sockstat,protocols,unix,tcp,udp,raw,packet}` deep network-protocol per-counter auditor — distinct from shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` (which is sysctls only — knob configuration side), shipped `nic_health.py` (which is link-layer state), and shipped `nic_ring_audit.py` from #43 (which is per-NIC drop counters) — this is the *protocol-stack-internal* side: TCP retransmits, ListenOverflows, ListenDrops, SYN-cookie events, TCP-MD5 failures, UDP receive-buffer overruns, socket inode count per family** | S | 5 | NO shipped module reads `/proc/net/netstat` or `/proc/net/snmp` — yet these two pseudo-files are the *only* stdlib path to surface the TCP/UDP protocol-stack internal counters that drive LAN-served inference jitter. Format : `/proc/net/snmp` is RFC 1213 SNMPv2-style counters per protocol family (Ip:, Icmp:, Tcp:, Udp:, UdpLite:) with each protocol on 2 lines (header line of counter names + data line of values); `/proc/net/netstat` is the Linux-specific extended counter set (`TcpExt:` for TCP-state-machine internals + `IpExt:` for IP-layer extended counters). Sampling deltas over 30-60 s reveals: (1) **ListenOverflows during fan-out inference burst** — when Home Assistant + OpenWebUI + a Discord bot all simultaneously open SSE streams to llama-server, the listen-backlog (`net.core.somaxconn`) overflows silently; `TcpExt.ListenOverflows` and `TcpExt.ListenDrops` increment while the connecting clients see `ECONNREFUSED` and retry — but no NIC counter, no `dmesg`, no application log reports it; the symptom is "Home Assistant says LLM is unreachable for 8 seconds then recovers" but nobody connects it to listen-backlog overflow; surface "ListenOverflows +12 in 30 s — bump `net.core.somaxconn=4096` and `llama-server --listen-backlog 4096`", (2) **TcpRetransSegs cascade** — when 10GbE NIC has bad SFP+ DAC cable, `TcpRetransSegs` climbs 5-10 % of `TcpOutSegs` while `nic_ring_audit.py` shows zero rx_dropped (because the loss is on the egress side, after the NIC); surface retransmit-rate and correlate with NIC errors; (3) **UDP receive-buffer overrun for mDNS/Avahi** — when `avahi-daemon` and `systemd-resolved` both bind UDP/5353, one of them drops packets; `Udp.RcvbufErrors` counter rises while applications see DNS-resolution stalls 1-3 s; surface "UDP RcvbufErrors +47 in 30 s on UDP/5353 — kill one mDNS resolver", (4) **TCPSynRetrans + TCPTimeouts** during PSU brown-out triggering NIC re-init — useful root-cause smoke gun. First module : `net_proto_counters.py`, GET `/api/net-proto-counters?window=30` returns `{tcp:{InSegs:18421,OutSegs:17993,RetransSegs:142,RetransRate_pct:0.79,ListenOverflows:0,ListenDrops:0,SyncookiesSent:0,verdict:"WARN",issue:"RetransRate 0.79 % (baseline 0.05 %) — check SFP+ DAC cable / NIC errors"},udp:{InDatagrams:892,OutDatagrams:912,RcvbufErrors:0,SndbufErrors:0,InErrors:0,verdict:"OK"},ip:{InReceives:19320,InDelivers:19318,verdict:"OK"},sockets:{tcp_inuse:42,tcp_orphan:0,tcp_tw:3,udp_inuse:12,raw_inuse:0,frag_inuse:0,verdict:"OK"}}`. |
| 44.5 | **`/proc/<pid>/pagemap` + `/proc/kpageflags` + `/proc/kpagecount` + `/sys/kernel/mm/page_idle/bitmap` physical-page-level inventory + VRAM-pinned-host-page detector** | M | 3 | Page-frame-level inspection — answers "is this anon page locked, hugepage-merged, dirty, referenced, swap-backed?" via `/proc/<pid>/pagemap` (8 bytes per virtual page, encoding PFN + flags), and `/proc/kpageflags` (8 bytes per physical page, encoding LRU/dirty/anon/hugepage/buddy/ksm/locked/etc). Useful for surfacing nvidia-`cudaHostAlloc` pinned-host-memory regions that the kernel can't reclaim. Requires CAP_SYS_ADMIN, surfaces only summary stats not raw maps. First module : `pageframe_audit.py`, GET `/api/pageframe-audit?pid=<llama-server>`. Lower-fit than 44.1-44.4 because requires root + niche use case. |
| 44.6 | **`/proc/cgroups` + `/sys/fs/cgroup/cgroup.{controllers,subtree_control,events}` + per-leaf `cgroup.threads` / `cgroup.procs` / `cgroup.type` cgroup-v2 hierarchy + controller-availability auditor — distinct from shipped `cgroup_cpuio.py` (writes `cpu.weight`/`io.weight`) and shipped `cgroup_memcap.py` (writes `memory.high/max`) — this is the *hierarchy + capability* side: which controllers are enabled, where is the cgroup-v2-unified root, is delegation working, can the inference user create child cgroups?** | XS | 4 | Shipped cgroup modules configure individual knobs but never enumerate the hierarchy. `/proc/cgroups` lists v1 controller availability (`name hierarchy num_cgroups enabled`), `/sys/fs/cgroup/cgroup.controllers` lists v2 controllers available at root, `cgroup.subtree_control` lists which are enabled for children, `cgroup.events` reports `populated` + `frozen` state. Auditor detects: (1) **systemd not delegating io controller** — by default systemd masks `io` controller from user.slice; user-launched ollama can't get io.weight isolation; surface "io controller not delegated to user.slice — add `Delegate=io` to user@.service.d/override.conf", (2) **mixed v1+v2 hybrid mode** — Ubuntu LTS sometimes mounts v1 `cpuset` alongside v2 unified; cgroup_cpuio writes go to v2 but cpuset still v1 — silently broken NUMA pinning, (3) **leaf-cgroup-with-children violation** — cgroup-v2 forbids processes on internal cgroups; if user nests `ollama.scope/worker1` under `ollama.scope` while ollama-main is still in `ollama.scope`, all attempts to set controllers fail with `EBUSY`. First module : `cgroup_hierarchy.py`, GET `/api/cgroup-hierarchy`. |
| 44.7 | **`/sys/bus/usb/devices/*/{power/autosuspend_delay_ms,power/control,power/runtime_status,authorized,bConfigurationValue,product,manufacturer,maxchild,bMaxPower,bcdUSB,speed}` USB device + power-management inventory for HID / Stream-Deck / USB-UPS / Elgato-capture auditor** | XS | 3 | Sysfs USB tree exposes every connected device; relevant for homelab rigs that run USB-NUT UPS communication (shipped `ups_nut.py` configures NUT but never verifies the *USB device side* — is the UPS suspended? wrong autosuspend?). Detects: USB-UPS autosuspending (USB poll stops, NUT loses comms after 30-60 s idle), USB Stream Deck/HID device wedged in `runtime_status=suspended`, USB authorization disabled by usbguard blocking inference-control HID. First module : `usb_power_audit.py`. |
| 44.8 | **`/proc/timer_list` + `/sys/kernel/debug/tracing/events/timer/*` hrtimer + clockevent device + per-CPU active-timer dump for cpu-idle vs hrtimer-jitter correlation** | M | 3 | `/proc/timer_list` dumps all queued hrtimers across all CPUs in human-readable form (one block per CPU: clockevent device name, current jiffies, `.expires`, `.function` symbol). Diagnoses: nvidia GPU-clock-keepalive timer firing every 1 ms preventing CPU from entering C6, kernel `watchdog/N` thread overrunning, RCU callback queue depth. Heavier-weight than 44.4 because output format requires symbol-name parsing + per-CPU multiplexing. First module : `timer_list_audit.py`. |

## R&D #43 survey (started 2026-05-23)

8 fresh angles, none in shipped list (#6-42 — including just-shipped `file_locks_audit` / `hybrid_cpu_topo` / `cooling_devices` / `cpu_epb` from cycle #42), modules tree (152 files), or parked bench (27.2/27.5/27.6/27.8 + 28.2/28.3/28.6/28.8 + 29.2/29.4/29.5/29.6 + 30.4/30.6/30.7/30.8 + 31.5/31.6/31.7/31.8 + 32.2/32.6/32.7/32.8 + 33.3/33.5/33.7/33.8 + 34.5/34.6/34.7/34.8 + 35.5/35.6/35.7/35.8 + 36.5/36.6/36.7/36.8 + 37.5/37.6/37.7/37.8 + 38.5/38.6/38.7/38.8 + 39.5/39.6/39.7/39.8 + 40.5/40.6/40.7/40.8 + 41.5/41.6/41.7/41.8 + 42.5/42.6/42.7/42.8). Stdlib + jsonschema only, single-GPU desktops / homelab focus (RTX 3090 reference), GUI-controllable from Svelte 5 settings.

| # | Feature | Effort | Fit | Why it matters / first module |
|---|---------|--------|-----|-------|
| 43.1 | **`/proc/interrupts` + `/proc/softirqs` per-CPU IRQ-rate + softirq-imbalance time-series auditor — distinct from shipped `gpu_irq_affinity.py` (which sets `smp_affinity` masks) and shipped `msi_inventory.py` (which lists MSI vectors) — this is the *rate* and *distribution* side: who's firing how often, on which CPUs, and is one CPU drowning under NET_RX while the other 23 are idle?** | S | 5 | Shipped `gpu_irq_affinity.py` configures *which* CPU should handle the nvidia GPU IRQ (`/proc/irq/<n>/smp_affinity`), shipped `msi_inventory.py` lists *which* MSI vectors are allocated — but neither watches the *runtime rate* or detects *softirq imbalance*. The complete IRQ-firing-rate picture lives in two pseudo-files no shipped module reads: `/proc/interrupts` (one row per IRQ vector × one column per CPU, per-CPU 64-bit counters of hardware interrupt deliveries) and `/proc/softirqs` (one row per softirq type — HI/TIMER/NET_TX/NET_RX/BLOCK/IRQ_POLL/TASKLET/SCHED/HRTIMER/RCU — × one column per CPU, per-CPU softirq dispatch counters). Sampling both at 1-second intervals over 30-60 s reveals: (1) NET_RX softirq imbalance — common pattern on homelab boxes where the RTX 3090 is on PCIe slot 1 (NUMA node 0) but the 10GbE NIC is on PCIe slot 3 (still node 0 on desktop but irq-affinity defaults to cpu0) so `NET_RX` softirq lands on cpu0 only, cpu0 sits at 80 % `%si` while the other 23 cores idle, inference workers running on cpu0 silently lose 20-30 % t/s during heavy HF download bursts; the symptom is "my llama-server gets jittery when ollama is downloading a model" but nobody connects it to NET_RX-pinning, (2) RCU softirq storm — kernel 6.x has occasional `RCU` softirq bursts when the box has many short-lived processes (jupyter kernel restarts, web-search subprocesses spawned by RAG pipelines) which can starve inference threads; surface "RCU softirq cpu0 fired 2.4M times in 10 s, normal is <100K — possible kernel bug or runaway fork loop, paste-ready `pgrep -c .` count + `dmesg | tail -50` recipe to investigate", (3) nvidia IRQ rate — RTX 3090 firing 800-2000 IRQs/s during prefill is healthy, but `/proc/interrupts` row for `nvidia` jumping to 50000/s suggests a stuck completion ring or descriptor-walking pathology (very rare but distinctive — happens after `nvidia-smi -r` reset on driver 570.x), (4) `i8042` keyboard controller IRQ firing 50/s with no user present → known PS/2-emulation bug on some MSI boards, harmless but cosmetic, surface as info, (5) NVMe `nvme0q*` per-queue IRQs all landing on cpu0 → bad NVMe IRQ-spreading, recommend `irqbalance.service` + cross-link shipped `nic_queue_affinity.py` (NIC side equivalent) + shipped `gpu_irq_affinity.py` (GPU side), (6) SCHED softirq imbalance — one cpu doing 10× the scheduler work of others → typically a long taskset-pinned inference worker pulling all scheduler wake-ups, surface for transparency, (7) the TIMER softirq column — should be uniform across CPUs, asymmetry suggests broken `clocksource` or `tsc-deadline` issue (cross-link shipped `clocksource_audit.py`), (8) idle CPUs with non-zero softirq counters — perfectly normal (timer ticks during NOHZ_FULL fail to suppress fully), surface as expected. Read `/proc/interrupts` (parse header line for CPU columns, each row = IRQ vector name + per-CPU counts + IRQ-type descriptor), `/proc/softirqs` (10 fixed rows, per-CPU columns), take two snapshots ~10 s apart, compute per-IRQ per-CPU rate (deltas / interval), compute per-IRQ standard deviation across CPUs (high stddev → imbalance), cross-reference shipped `gpu_irq_affinity.py` (nvidia IRQ id + affinity mask), shipped `msi_inventory.py` (MSI vector → IRQ id), shipped `nic_queue_affinity.py` (NIC multi-queue IRQ ids), shipped `cpu_topology.py` + #42.2 hybrid_cpu_topo (hybrid CPUs — IRQs should prefer P-cluster), shipped `numa_placement.py` (NUMA-local IRQ steering), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) all IRQs balanced (top-5 high-rate IRQs have stddev / mean < 0.5) + softirqs evenly distributed → optimal, congratulate, (b) NET_RX softirq concentrated on cpu0 (one CPU has > 70 % of the total) → inference-jitter risk, paste-ready `echo <mask> > /proc/irq/<nic-irq>/smp_affinity` + recommend `irqbalance.service` + `/etc/sysconfig/irqbalance` template, (c) RCU softirq storm → fork-loop / kernel-bug investigation recipe, (d) nvidia IRQ rate > 30000/s sustained → driver-reset diagnostic + cross-link shipped `nvrm_tail.py` + `xid_decoder.py` for the most-recent NVRM event, (e) NVMe per-queue IRQs all on cpu0 → recommend irqbalance + manual `/proc/irq/<id>/smp_affinity_list` rebalance per-queue. Surface "your `/proc/interrupts` over the last 30 s shows: nvidia (IRQ 173) firing 1247/s avg, evenly across cpus 0-7 (P-cluster, optimal per shipped #38.4); enp4s0 NIC (IRQ 142) firing 3400/s, 89 % on cpu0 (imbalance — paste-ready rebalance below); nvme0q1-q4 (IRQs 145-148) all on cpu0 (irqbalance not running, paste-ready `apt install irqbalance && systemctl enable --now irqbalance`); RCU softirq 84K/s avg across cpus (healthy); NET_RX softirq 28K/s 91 % on cpu0 (correlates with NIC IRQ imbalance — fixed once IRQ rebalanced); SCHED softirq 12K/s evenly distributed. Top imbalance = NIC IRQ — paste-ready 4-line fix below". The cleanest "what's actually firing on my CPUs right now and which one is overloaded" answer that today no OSS Linux tool surfaces with single classified verdict + paste-ready recipe. Pairs with shipped `gpu_irq_affinity.py`, `msi_inventory.py`, `nic_queue_affinity.py`, `numa_placement.py`, `cpu_topology.py`, #42.2 hybrid_cpu_topo. First : `modules/irq_rates_audit.py` + GET `/api/irq-rates-audit`. |
| 43.2 | **`/sys/block/*/queue/{nr_requests,scheduler,read_ahead_kb,nomerges,rq_affinity,write_cache,add_random,iostats,max_sectors_kb,wbt_lat_usec,io_poll,io_poll_delay,nr_zones,zoned}` block-layer per-queue knob auditor — distinct from shipped `nvme_iosched.py` (which is just the `scheduler` knob)** | S | 5 | Shipped `nvme_iosched.py` reads only the `scheduler` file (none/mq-deadline/bfq/kyber) for NVMe devices — but the Linux block layer exposes ~15 *other* per-queue tunables that materially affect inference workload latency + throughput, and that no shipped module reads. The recurring foot-guns: (1) `nr_requests=128` (default on most distros) on a Samsung 990 Pro NVMe rated for 1.5M IOPS → queue depth saturates instantly under heavy GGUF mmap-faulting (llama.cpp's first prefill on a 70B model triggers thousands of page faults per second pulling 4 KiB chunks from the GGUF, the NVMe driver queues each as a block request — at nr_requests=128 the queue is the bottleneck not the device), bumping to `nr_requests=1024` (or 2048 for top-tier NVMe) drops first-prefill latency 20-40 %; the symptom "first prefill after fresh boot is always slow even on a fast NVMe" is almost always this knob, (2) `read_ahead_kb=128` (default) on a sequential-read-heavy GGUF mmap workload → kernel reads only 128 KiB ahead of the user's mmap-fault, can't keep up with the matmul's prefetch rate; bumping to 4096 or 8192 saturates the NVMe queue depth properly during prefill, +5-15 % t/s; surface "your `/sys/block/nvme0n1/queue/read_ahead_kb=128` — for sequential GGUF mmap workloads bump to 4096 with `echo 4096 | sudo tee /sys/block/nvme0n1/queue/read_ahead_kb`, persist via `/etc/udev/rules.d/`", (3) `rq_affinity=1` (kernel default) vs `rq_affinity=2` → request completion can run on any CPU in the requesting CPU's cache group (1) vs the *exact* CPU that submitted (2); for inference workers pinned to specific cores (per shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity), rq_affinity=2 keeps completion-IPI traffic on the same core, reducing cache pollution on the matmul-hot cores — small but measurable +1-3 % win on Zen4/5 multi-CCD setups, (4) `nomerges=0` (default, allow request merging) vs `nomerges=2` (no merging at all) → for NVMe with single-issue command latency well below queue-build-up latency, `nomerges=2` can shave 5-10 µs per IO; usually not worth it for inference (sequential reads benefit from merging), surface as advanced-only, (5) `wbt_lat_usec=75000` (writeback-throttling target, 75 ms by default) → user dumps a 30 GiB GGUF checkpoint mid-inference and the kernel throttles writebacks to "preserve" 75 ms read latency — but then forces a long stutter when the wbt timer expires; on a closet-server doing weights downloads alongside inference, set to 0 (disable wbt) for predictable mixed workload; recommend explicitly, (6) `write_cache=write back` vs `write through` → SSD volatile cache state, write-back is the modern default (battery-backed firmware), write-through halves random-write IOPS — surface for transparency, (7) `add_random=0` (kernel ≥ 4.x default for SSDs, contributes nothing to /dev/random pool) — usually fine, but on FIPS-mode systems some hardening guides force =1 which slows IO ~3 %; check + surface, (8) `iostats=0` (some performance guides set this to "save CPU") → shipped `disk_health.py` + #43.1 (this RnD) IRQ-rate auditor lose data, recommend keeping =1, (9) `io_poll=1` + `io_poll_delay=-1` → kernel polls for NVMe completion instead of IRQ, saves IRQ overhead (~5 µs/op) but consumes CPU on the polling core — winning only on dedicated-NVMe boxes with cores to burn, surface as advanced tuning, (10) `max_sectors_kb=512` (kernel default) → cap on per-request size; bumping to 1024 or 2048 lets large mmap-faulted reads coalesce into fewer requests, +3-8 % sequential bandwidth, recommended for inference, (11) zoned-block-device detection (`zoned=host-managed`/`zoned=host-aware`/`zoned=none`) → SMR HDDs rarely show up on homelab GPU rigs but if `/data/models/` is on a zoned device this is critical to know, (12) `wbt_lat_usec` vs filesystem-level commit interval (shipped `fs_mount_audit.py` covers fs commit/data=writeback) — the two knobs interact, surface the combined picture. Walk `/sys/block/*/queue/` for every block device (filter to those holding `/data/models/` or shipped service-discovery's open-file paths to avoid floating noise from loops/dm devices), read all ~15 knobs, cross-reference shipped `nvme_iosched.py` (scheduler), `disk_health.py` (SMART), `fs_mount_audit.py` (filesystem-level commit), `host_class.py` (skip if /data/models on networked fs), classify into 5 verdicts per device: (a) `nr_requests` low + `read_ahead_kb` low on a high-IOPS NVMe holding GGUF files → paste-ready bump recipe (4096 + 1024), (b) `wbt_lat_usec > 0` on a mixed inference + download box → recommend `wbt_lat_usec=0`, (c) `rq_affinity=1` on a pinned-inference setup → recommend `=2`, (d) `iostats=0` → flag (breaks shipped tooling), (e) all defaults on a high-end NVMe → recommend the inference-tuned profile bundle as a one-shot udev rule. Surface "your nvme0n1 (Samsung 990 Pro 2TB, holds /data/models/ per shipped service-discovery) block-queue audit: scheduler=none (optimal per shipped #36.6), nr_requests=128 (low — bump to 1024), read_ahead_kb=128 (low — bump to 4096 for sequential GGUF), rq_affinity=1 (recommend 2 for pinned inference), nomerges=0 (default-ok), write_cache=write back (default-ok), wbt_lat_usec=75000 (consider 0 for mixed inference+download), io_poll=0 (default-ok for IRQ-driven), max_sectors_kb=512 (default — consider 1024). Apply all 4 recommendations atomically via the paste-ready udev rule below: ATTRS{model}=='Samsung SSD 990 PRO 2TB', ATTR{queue/nr_requests}='1024', ATTR{queue/read_ahead_kb}='4096', ATTR{queue/rq_affinity}='2', ATTR{queue/wbt_lat_usec}='0' — drop in /etc/udev/rules.d/99-nvme-inference.rules". The cleanest "I tuned my NVMe scheduler per shipped #36.6 but didn't know there were 14 more knobs to tune" answer for the I-already-tuned-the-obvious-stuff power-user audience. Pairs with shipped `nvme_iosched.py`, `disk_health.py`, `fs_mount_audit.py`, `nvme_swap.py`, `host_class.py`. First : `modules/block_queue_audit.py` + GET `/api/block-queue-audit`. |
| 43.3 | **`/proc/zoneinfo` + `/proc/vmstat` per-NUMA-zone watermark + reclaim-counter + compaction-counter auditor for kswapd-storm + direct-reclaim-stall detection during large GGUF mmap loads** | S | 4 | The Linux memory subsystem manages physical RAM in *zones* (Normal, DMA32, DMA, Movable, plus per-NUMA-node variants on multi-socket systems) — each with independent watermarks (`min`, `low`, `high`, `boost`) that trigger background kswapd or foreground direct-reclaim. When llama.cpp mmaps a 47 GiB GGUF on a 64 GiB box, the kernel fault-walks the file pulling 4 KiB pages into the Normal zone; if free pages drop below `low` watermark before reclaim can keep up, the *first prefill* triggers `direct reclaim` (the user-space thread blocks while the kernel scans LRU + writes back dirty pages + drops clean caches) which can stall 100-2000 ms with no log entry; the symptom "my llama-server prefill is sometimes super slow on the first model load after I've been using my desktop" is almost always direct reclaim. Shipped `buddyinfo_frag.py` covers buddy-allocator fragmentation (the chunk side), shipped `vm_tuning_deep.py` covers `vm.*` sysctl tunables (the policy side), shipped `vm_sysctl_audit.py` covers more sysctl — but the *zone-level live state* (per-zone free/min/low/high/boost watermarks, per-zone `pages_scanned`, `pages_reclaimed`, kswapd wake counts, compaction success/failure counters) lives in `/proc/zoneinfo` + `/proc/vmstat` and no shipped module reads either. The recurring foot-guns: (1) Normal zone `low` watermark crossed during GGUF mmap-fault → kswapd wakes, scans LRUs, can't reclaim fast enough → direct reclaim activates, foreground inference stall; surface "your zone Normal node 0 hit `low` watermark (4.2M pages free, low=4.8M) at 14:32:08, kswapd0 woke 47 times in 30 s, `pages_direct_reclaim` counter advanced by 18234 — your first llama-server prefill stalled ~340 ms on memory reclaim; prevent by bumping `vm.min_free_kbytes` from 67584 (default) to 524288 (~512 MiB headroom for sudden mmap-fault bursts), paste-ready snippet", (2) `pgscan_direct` >> `pgscan_kswapd` over the last minute → background reclaim is *losing*, every reclaim event is direct (user-blocking) → critical, paste-ready fix, (3) `compact_fail` counter advancing rapidly → kernel trying and failing to defragment for THP (transparent huge pages) allocation, shipped `thp_audit.py` covers the policy but `compact_fail` is the live failure signal, recommend disabling THP for inference processes (cross-link shipped `thp_audit.py`), (4) `numa_foreign` >> `numa_hit` (cross-NUMA allocation rate) on a multi-socket system → memory pressure pushing allocations to remote NUMA node, surface the rate + cross-link shipped `numa_placement.py` recommend `numa_balancing=0` + explicit `numactl --membind=0`, (5) `boost` watermark active (kernel ≥ 5.0 feature, raises low/high when fragmentation high) → kernel is in defensive mode, expect higher reclaim activity, surface for transparency, (6) `oom_kill_count` advanced in the last hour → at least one OOM event happened, cross-link shipped `oom_priority.py` + `oomd_correlator.py` for the post-mortem, (7) `nr_isolated_anon` + `nr_isolated_file` non-zero for extended period → migration stuck (rare but devastating), surface as bug indicator, (8) `pglazyfree` + `pglazyfreed` counters → MADV_FREE'd pages still resident vs released, useful for the curious-about-memory user. Read `/proc/zoneinfo` (multi-zone, each ~30 fields including `pages free / min / low / high / boost`, scan/reclaim counters per LRU list — anon active/inactive, file active/inactive), `/proc/vmstat` (global counters — `pgalloc_*`, `pgreclaim_*`, `compact_*`, `numa_*`, `pgmajfault`, `pgmigrate_*`, `oom_kill`), take two snapshots ~30 s apart, compute rate-of-change for the reclaim counters, cross-reference shipped `buddyinfo_frag.py`, `vm_tuning_deep.py`, `vm_sysctl_audit.py`, `thp_audit.py`, `numa_placement.py`, `oom_priority.py`, `oomd_correlator.py`, `host_class.py` (containers — cgroup-v2 memory.* takes precedence), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) all zones above `low` watermark + zero direct reclaim + compaction success rate > 95 % → healthy, surface free-RAM headroom as info, (b) `low` crossed + direct reclaim activated → flag with paste-ready `vm.min_free_kbytes` bump (size based on detected GGUF mmap pattern), (c) compaction failing → THP-disable recommend, (d) NUMA cross-node allocation rate high → `numa_balancing` + `numactl --membind` recipe, (e) OOM killed recently → cross-link diagnostic. Surface "your `/proc/zoneinfo` shows Normal node 0 zone: 8.4 GiB free, watermarks min=212 MiB / low=265 MiB / high=318 MiB (boost active +50 %, kernel raised them due to fragmentation per shipped buddyinfo_frag); last 30 s: kswapd0 woke 4 times (healthy background), pgscan_kswapd_normal +12K (healthy), pgscan_direct_normal +0 (no direct reclaim, optimal). `compact_fail` advanced 0 in last 30 s, `numa_hit` 4.2M, `numa_foreign` 0 (single-node desktop, expected). `oom_kill` counter unchanged. Your reclaim posture is healthy for current workload but `vm.min_free_kbytes=67584` (default 64 MiB) is low for 47 GiB GGUF mmap workloads — consider bumping to 524288 (~512 MiB) to give kswapd more headroom against burst faulting; paste-ready /etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-vm.conf snippet below". The cleanest "my first prefill is sometimes mysteriously slow even on a 96 GB box" answer for the direct-reclaim-stall pattern that today no OSS Linux tool surfaces with single classified verdict + paste-ready vm.min_free_kbytes recipe. Pairs with shipped `buddyinfo_frag.py`, `vm_tuning_deep.py`, `vm_sysctl_audit.py`, `thp_audit.py`, `numa_placement.py`, `oom_priority.py`, `oomd_correlator.py`. First : `modules/zoneinfo_audit.py` + GET `/api/zoneinfo-audit`. |
| 43.4 | **`/sys/class/net/<iface>/statistics/{rx_dropped,tx_dropped,rx_fifo_errors,rx_missed_errors,rx_over_errors,collisions,rx_crc_errors,rx_length_errors,rx_frame_errors}` NIC ring-buffer drop + FIFO-overrun monitor (distinct from shipped `nic_health.py` which covers link state + speed/duplex)** | XS | 4 | Shipped `nic_health.py` reads link-level NIC state (carrier, speed, duplex, MTU, ethtool driver/version) — but the *ring-buffer-drop* side of the NIC subsystem, where packets disappear *between* the wire and the kernel TCP/IP stack due to undersized ring buffers / overwhelmed IRQ handlers / mis-configured RPS, lives at `/sys/class/net/<iface>/statistics/*` (per-counter file, 64-bit counter, updated by the NIC driver). The recurring foot-guns: (1) `rx_dropped` counter climbing during heavy HF model downloads → NIC ring buffer is undersized (default 256 descriptors on most consumer Intel I225/I226 2.5GbE NICs, 1024 on Realtek 2.5GbE) and kernel-side `softnet_data.dropped` advances; the user sees download retry / TLS reset → slow downloads, surface "your enp4s0 has `rx_dropped` advanced 4823 in last 30 s during ollama pull — your NIC ring is too small; check `ethtool -g enp4s0` then bump with `ethtool -G enp4s0 rx 4096 tx 4096`, paste-ready snippet + persist via `/etc/systemd/network/`", (2) `rx_fifo_errors` advancing → NIC's internal FIFO overflowed (the NIC's hardware ring filled faster than DMA could drain to host RAM), typically because PCIe Gen3 x1 link width (shipped `pcie_width_watcher.py` warns) starves the NIC under bursty 10GbE; cross-link, (3) `rx_missed_errors` → packets the NIC received but couldn't even queue (worse than rx_fifo — the NIC simply discarded), indicates extreme overload, (4) `rx_crc_errors` > 0 → physical layer issue, bad cable / port / wall-plate, surface clearly with cable-replacement recipe, (5) `collisions` > 0 on a modern full-duplex NIC → impossible, indicates the NIC negotiated to half-duplex (firmware bug or switch-port misconfig), cross-link shipped `nic_health.py` for the duplex audit, (6) `tx_dropped` non-zero → unusual on the TX side, indicates qdisc problem or queue-full; surface "your qdisc may be misconfigured, check `tc qdisc show dev enp4s0`", (7) `rx_length_errors` + `rx_frame_errors` → Jumbo frame / MTU mismatch (the user enabled MTU 9000 on the NIC but the switch port hasn't been bumped), shipped `nic_health.py` covers MTU but not the drop counter, surface together. Read `/sys/class/net/<iface>/statistics/{rx_dropped,tx_dropped,rx_fifo_errors,rx_missed_errors,rx_over_errors,collisions,rx_crc_errors,rx_length_errors,rx_frame_errors,rx_packets,tx_packets,rx_bytes,tx_bytes}` for every UP non-loopback interface, take two snapshots ~30 s apart, compute deltas + drop-rate-per-packet-received (sensitive proxy — 0.01 % drop already meaningful for download workloads), cross-reference shipped `nic_health.py` (link-level), shipped `nic_queue_affinity.py` (multi-queue distribution), `pcie_width_watcher.py` (PCIe bandwidth starvation), `net_sysctl_audit.py` (`net.core.netdev_max_backlog` + `net.core.rmem_max` host-side tuning), classify into 5 verdicts per interface: (a) zero drops + zero FIFO over last 30 s → healthy, info-only, (b) `rx_dropped` advancing + low ring size → paste-ready `ethtool -G` ring-bump recipe, (c) `rx_fifo_errors` + `pcie_width_watcher` showing degraded PCIe link → PCIe bandwidth starvation, cross-link recipe, (d) `rx_crc_errors` advancing → physical-layer fault, cable/port recipe, (e) MTU mismatch (length/frame errors) → cross-link `nic_health.py` MTU audit. Surface "your enp4s0 (Intel I225-V 2.5GbE, link UP per shipped nic_health.py) statistics over last 30 s: rx_packets 248912 (8.3 K/s, healthy for normal LAN traffic), rx_dropped +4823 (drop rate 1.9 %, HIGH — happening during your active HF model download), rx_fifo_errors +0 (NIC FIFO ok), rx_missed_errors +0 (no extreme overload), rx_crc_errors +0 (physical ok), tx_dropped +0 (qdisc ok). Your `ethtool -g enp4s0` shows RX ring=256/RX-max=4096 — bump to 4096 with `ethtool -G enp4s0 rx 4096 tx 4096`, persist via `/etc/systemd/network/10-enp4s0.link` paste-ready snippet. Bonus: also bump `net.core.netdev_max_backlog=300000` per shipped #38.3 net_sysctl_audit and `net.core.rmem_max=33554432` for the same workload; paste-ready combined sysctl.d snippet below". Tiniest scope (~12 statistics file reads per interface, ~2 interfaces typical = ~25 reads), highest "I had no idea I was dropping 2 % of my HF download packets at the NIC ring level" eye-opener for the homelab user who's already tuned the link side per shipped `nic_health.py` and net.core sysctls per `net_sysctl_audit.py`. Pairs with shipped `nic_health.py`, `nic_queue_affinity.py`, `pcie_width_watcher.py`, `net_sysctl_audit.py`. First : `modules/nic_ring_audit.py` + GET `/api/nic-ring-audit`. |
| 43.5 | **`/proc/keys` + `/proc/key-users` + `/proc/sys/kernel/keys/*` live kernel-keyring inventory + per-user keyring quota auditor — distinct from parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (IMA-measurement side) — this is the *live keyring usage* side: which keys are loaded, who owns them, are LUKS/dm-crypt session keys leaking, is `kernel.keys.maxkeys` quota saturated?** | XS | 3 | The kernel keyring subsystem (`man 7 keyrings`) holds user/session/process credentials in-kernel: LUKS dm-crypt master keys (`logon` type), DNS resolver caches (`dns_resolver`), eCryptfs / fscrypt file-encryption keys (`fscrypt-provisioning`, `fscrypt`), Kerberos AFS tokens (`rxrpc_s`), session-keyring inherited across exec, plus the `cifs` keyring for SMB mounts. On a homelab inference rig with LUKS on `/data/models/`, fscrypt-encrypted user directories, NFSv4 with Kerberos, or any `cifs` mount to a Synology NAS, the keyring fills up; the kernel imposes per-user quotas (`kernel.keys.maxkeys`=200 default per non-root user, `kernel.keys.maxbytes`=20000 default) — quota saturation produces silent EDQUOT errors when the user tries to add a new key (the symptom is "cryptsetup luksOpen worked yesterday but today says 'Disk encryption key not available'", a known frustration with no obvious diagnostic). The recurring foot-guns: (1) `/proc/sys/kernel/keys/maxkeys=200` + a busy dm-crypt setup with 4 LUKS volumes + fscrypt on user homedir + 2 cifs mounts → quota approaches saturation, next LUKS unlock fails silently, surface "your user UID 1000 has 187/200 keys (94 % quota), next LUKS unlock may fail with EDQUOT — bump `kernel.keys.maxkeys` to 2000 + `kernel.keys.maxbytes` to 200000 via `/etc/sysctl.d/`", (2) keyring leak — a long-running service that calls `add_key()` in a loop without revoking → keyring count grows without bound, surface "your `gpu-dashboard.service` (UID 1000) has 247 active session keys, top types: `dns_resolver` (192 entries), `user` (45 entries) — likely DNS-resolver-cache buildup, no immediate problem but indicates `dns_resolver` keyring isn't being garbage-collected; check kernel parameter `dns.kernel.expiry`", (3) `logon` type keys with extended `Time-to-live` (TTL) → LUKS master keys leaked past their intended session, surface as security note "you have 3 `logon` keys with TTL=permanent — these are likely LUKS/dm-crypt master keys from boot, they remain in kernel memory until reboot; this is normal but worth knowing for high-security setups", (4) `user` type keys with shell-readable description containing "password" or "token" → the user pasted a secret into a `keyctl add user` for a script, surface as info, (5) DNS resolver keyring with `dns_resolver` keys that haven't expired despite their TTL → kernel resolver bug, surface "you have 192 `dns_resolver` keys ranging 12-720 hours old, expected TTL is 60 min — possible kernel keyring-expiry bug, paste-ready `keyctl clear @us` to manually reset", (6) `cifs` keyring keys with stale references after the cifs mount was unmounted → keyring not auto-cleaned, surface, (7) per-user quota mismatch — `kernel.keys.root_maxkeys=200` for root + `kernel.keys.maxkeys=200` for non-root → root is throttled identically, ironic and limiting; recommend bumping root_maxkeys to 4000. Read `/proc/keys` (one line per kernel-keyring entry: serial, flags, usage, timeout, perm, uid, gid, type, description), `/proc/key-users` (per-UID summary: usage, nkeys, nikeys, qnkeys/maxkeys, qnbytes/maxbytes), `/proc/sys/kernel/keys/{maxkeys,maxbytes,root_maxkeys,root_maxbytes,gc_delay,persistent_keyring_expiry}`, cross-reference shipped `service_discovery.py` (which UID owns which inference process), shipped `fs_mount_audit.py` (LUKS/cifs/fscrypt mounts), shipped `host_class.py` (container guests have separate keyring namespace), parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (IMA side), classify into 4 verdicts: (a) keyring usage < 50 % quota + no unexpected types → healthy, info-only, (b) per-user quota > 80 % → paste-ready quota bump (`kernel.keys.maxkeys=2000`), (c) DNS or cifs keyring leak (count growing without expiry advancing) → kernel-bug investigation recipe, (d) old `logon` LUKS keys present → info-only fairness note. Surface "your `/proc/keys` shows 247 active kernel-keys (UID 1000: 184 keys / 200 quota = 92 %, UID 0: 63 keys / 200 quota = 32 %): top types `dns_resolver` (147), `logon` (4 — LUKS master keys, permanent TTL, expected), `user` (38 — likely your shell-script add_key calls), `cifs.spnego` (12 — Synology NAS mount), `fscrypt` (46 — your encrypted homedir). Your UID 1000 quota is 92 % — bump `kernel.keys.maxkeys` to 2000 + `maxbytes` to 200000 via `/etc/sysctl.d/99-keyring.conf` (paste-ready) to avoid next-LUKS-unlock EDQUOT. Bonus: 147 `dns_resolver` keys with average age 4 hours suggests `kernel.keys.gc_delay=300` (5 min) is being honored — healthy. No leaked secrets in `user` key descriptions". Niche audience (LUKS + cifs + fscrypt users — ~10-15 % of homelab) but high-payoff "explains my mysterious LUKS unlock failure" feature for that slice. Pairs with shipped `fs_mount_audit.py`, `service_discovery.py`, `host_class.py`, parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit, parked #41.5 efi_secureboot (Secure Boot interacts with logon-key trust). First : `modules/keyring_live.py` + GET `/api/keyring-live`. |
| 43.6 | **`/sys/devices/system/node/node*/{numastat,meminfo,vmstat,distance,hugepages,scan_unevictable_pages}` per-NUMA-node migration + remote-allocation auditor — distinct from shipped `numa_placement.py` (which sets membind policy) and #43.3 zoneinfo_audit (zone-watermark side) — this is the *per-node migration counter* side: how often is the kernel actually moving pages between nodes, and is `autonuma` causing inference jitter?** | S | 4 | Shipped `numa_placement.py` handles the *policy* side (recommend `numactl --membind=0 --cpunodebind=0` for GPU-local inference), shipped `vm_tuning_deep.py` may set the `numa_balancing` sysctl — but the *runtime migration behavior* side (how many pages got migrated, at what rate, with what success vs failure ratio, per-source-and-destination-node breakdown) lives at `/sys/devices/system/node/node*/numastat` + `/proc/<pid>/numa_maps` and no shipped module reads either. The recurring foot-guns: (1) `autonuma` enabled (`kernel.numa_balancing=1`, default on Ubuntu since 20.04) + an inference workload that touches a 47 GiB GGUF mmap → kernel periodically scans the inference worker's page tables, marks pages with PROT_NONE, traps faults, decides if the touching CPU is "wrong NUMA" and migrates the page — these scans cost 0.5-3 ms each and can stall the inference thread; on a single-socket desktop with 1 NUMA node the migrations are no-ops but the *scanning overhead* still costs 1-2 % t/s; shipped `vm_tuning_deep.py` may already recommend `numa_balancing=0` on single-socket but doesn't surface the *measured* migration cost; surface "your `node0/numastat` shows numa_hit 4.2M / numa_miss 0 / numa_foreign 0 / numa_pages_migrated 0 in last 30 s + `pgmigrate_success=12834 / pgmigrate_fail=84` from /proc/vmstat — autonuma is scanning your inference worker but achieving zero useful migrations (single-node box); paste-ready `sysctl kernel.numa_balancing=0` + persist via `/etc/sysctl.d/99-numa.conf`, frees ~1-2 % t/s", (2) multi-socket Xeon / Threadripper Pro on homelab (rare but seen) with NUMA balancing fighting against manual `numactl --membind=0` pin → kernel keeps trying to migrate pages back to "correct" node, surface conflict, (3) per-node `numastat`: `numa_hit` vs `numa_miss` ratio < 95 % → cross-NUMA-node allocation happening, cross-reference shipped `numa_placement.py` for the fix, (4) `node*/distance` matrix → on a desktop single-node, distance to self is 10, to non-existent nodes shows ENOENT, expected; on multi-socket the matrix shows real numbers (10/21/31/41 etc.), surface the topology for the multi-socket-curious, (5) per-node `node*/meminfo` showing one node almost full + other almost empty → bad NUMA placement, recommend `numactl --interleave=all` or per-process membind, (6) `node*/hugepages/hugepages-2048kB/{nr_hugepages,nr_overcommit_hugepages,free_hugepages,surplus_hugepages}` → if user has reserved hugepages for vllm/cuda but the wrong node, surface "your `node0/hugepages/hugepages-2048kB/free_hugepages=0` but `node1` has 4096 free — your `vm.nr_hugepages` got distributed to the wrong node, paste-ready realloc snippet", (7) per-PID `/proc/<pid>/numa_maps` showing pages on remote node → cross-link to inference PID for a "your llama-server's 23 GiB GGUF mmap is 47 % on node 0 + 53 % on node 1" view, recommend `migratepages` runtime fix, (8) `scan_unevictable_pages` non-zero after kernel boot → some pages locked unevictable (mlock'd by llama.cpp `--mlock`, or kernel-locked for vfio), surface for transparency. Walk `/sys/devices/system/node/node*/`, read `numastat` (text key-value pairs: numa_hit, numa_miss, numa_foreign, interleave_hit, local_node, other_node, numa_pages_migrated), `meminfo` (per-node MemTotal/MemFree/MemUsed/Active/Inactive/AnonPages/FilePages/Slab/HugePages_Total etc.), `vmstat` (per-node counters), `distance` (NUMA distance matrix row), `hugepages/hugepages-*kB/*` (per-page-size hugepage state), per-inference-PID read `/proc/<pid>/numa_maps` (per-VMA placement breakdown), take two snapshots ~30 s apart for migration rate, cross-reference shipped `numa_placement.py`, `vm_tuning_deep.py`, `vm_sysctl_audit.py`, `service_discovery.py`, `gpu_cpu_affinity.py` (PCIe-NUMA-local CPU set), `host_class.py`, classify into 5 verdicts: (a) single-node desktop (node count = 1) + `numa_balancing=0` + zero migrations → optimal, info-only, (b) single-node + `numa_balancing=1` + zero useful migrations → paste-ready disable recipe, (c) multi-socket with cross-node allocation > 5 % → cross-link `numa_placement.py` fix, (d) hugepage placement mismatch → realloc snippet, (e) per-PID GGUF split across nodes → per-PID `migratepages` snippet. Surface "your single-socket 7950X has 1 NUMA node (node0), `kernel.numa_balancing=1` (Ubuntu default) but zero useful migrations possible — autonuma scanning overhead is wasted CPU. /proc/vmstat last 30 s: pgmigrate_success=12834, pgmigrate_fail=84 (16.5K total scans × 0.5-3 ms each = ~30-40 s of accumulated scan time across all workers). Paste-ready `echo 0 | sudo tee /proc/sys/kernel/numa_balancing` (immediate) + `/etc/sysctl.d/99-numa.conf` `kernel.numa_balancing=0` (boot-persist). Your hugepages config: `nr_hugepages=4096` (8 GiB) all on node0 (only node, expected), free=4096 (none in use — your inference doesn't allocate from the hugepage pool, consider dropping to 0 to free the 8 GiB for normal pageable RAM unless you specifically want hugepage-backed CUDA mmap)". The cleanest "autonuma is silently costing my single-socket inference rig 1-2 % t/s" answer that today no OSS Linux tool surfaces with single classified verdict + paste-ready recipe. Pairs with shipped `numa_placement.py`, `vm_tuning_deep.py`, `vm_sysctl_audit.py`, `gpu_cpu_affinity.py`, #43.3 zoneinfo_audit. First : `modules/numa_migration_audit.py` + GET `/api/numa-migration-audit`. |
| 43.7 | **`/proc/mtrr` + `/sys/kernel/debug/x86/pat_memtype_list` (if perf-paranoia / lockdown allow) MTRR (Memory Type Range Register) + PAT (Page Attribute Table) cacheability auditor for GPU BAR + PCIe-mapped-IO write-combining health** | XS | 3 | The x86 CPU's caching strategy for any physical memory range is governed by *two* mechanisms layered together: legacy MTRR (8-16 fixed/variable hardware registers per CPU that mark ranges as Write-Back / Write-Through / Write-Combining / Uncacheable / Write-Protect — the original Pentium-Pro mechanism) and modern PAT (per-page attribute table the kernel manages via `set_memory_wc()` / `ioremap_wc()` — Pentium III+ feature). For GPU PCIe BARs (the RTX 3090's 16 GiB framebuffer mapped into CPU address space, plus its small command-doorbell BAR), the *correct* cacheability is Write-Combining (WC) for the framebuffer (batches small CPU writes into 64-byte cache-line flushes for GPU consumption) and Uncacheable (UC) for the doorbell — anything else degrades GPU command throughput silently. The nvidia driver normally configures these correctly during `nvidia-modeset`'s probe (uses `ioremap_wc()` for the framebuffer mapping) but rare misconfigurations cause silent performance loss. The recurring foot-guns: (1) `/proc/mtrr` shows `base=0xc0000000 size=0x40000000 type=uncachable` over a range overlapping the RTX 3090 BAR → driver-managed PAT for WC is *defeated* because MTRR-UC wins over PAT-WC (legacy MTRR semantics), GPU command throughput drops 30-60 %; the symptom is "my llama-server bench is 30 % slower today than yesterday with no software changes" and is invisible to nvidia-smi; surface "your `/proc/mtrr` entry 3 marks 0xc0000000-0xffffffff as UC, overlapping your RTX 3090 BAR (per shipped #38.5 rebar_audit, BAR is at 0xc0000000-0xfbffffff) — MTRR-UC is overriding driver's PAT-WC, your GPU command throughput is degraded ~30-60 %; paste-ready `mtrr_del` recipe or BIOS-side fix (some Asus boards add a 'UC' BIOS entry for "old PCIe device compatibility" that needs disabling)", (2) MTRR-WC range present but not perfectly aligned with GPU BAR (off by a page) → partial WC coverage, partial PAT fallback, mid-range performance degradation, surface "your MTRR-WC range 0xc0000000-0xffffffff doesn't match BAR alignment exactly, recommend BIOS update or remove MTRR entirely and let nvidia driver manage via PAT", (3) PAT disabled in kernel (`nopat` cmdline option, sometimes recommended in obscure VFIO passthrough guides) → kernel falls back to MTRR-only, no fine-grained WC; surface as "your kernel cmdline has `nopat`, GPU performance limited by MTRR coarseness — remove via shipped `cmdline_audit.py`-recommended path", (4) PAT enabled but `iomem=strict` (some hardening kernels) blocks `/sys/kernel/debug/x86/pat_memtype_list` reads → graceful degradation, only `/proc/mtrr` available, surface as info, (5) more than 8 MTRR variable ranges in use → MTRR slot exhaustion, kernel logs "mtrr: type mismatch ignored" silently in dmesg, surface "you have 9 of 8 MTRR slots in use — slot 8 (your last-added range) is being silently ignored, consolidate via `mtrr_del` recipe", (6) MTRR-WC over RAM region (rare, but happens with creative BIOS settings) → CPU treats normal RAM as WC, severe perf loss, surface immediately, (7) cross-CPU MTRR inconsistency — on a multi-socket box different CPUs see different MTRR ranges → very rare kernel bug, surface for completeness. Read `/proc/mtrr` (text: one line per MTRR entry — `reg<N>: base=0x... (XMB), size=YMB, count=Z: <type>`), optionally `/sys/kernel/debug/x86/pat_memtype_list` (only if `/sys/kernel/debug` mounted + lockdown allows — text key-value per range; if blocked surface graceful degradation), cross-reference shipped `rebar_audit.py` (BAR base + size — match against MTRR ranges), shipped `gpu_pci_bind.py` (PCIe BDF for the GPU), shipped `cmdline_audit.py` (check for `nopat` / `mtrr=force`), shipped `host_class.py` (skip if running in a VM guest — MTRR in guest is virtual), shipped `kernel_taint.py`, parked #41.5 efi_secureboot (Secure Boot affects MTRR write), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) MTRR clean (zero variable entries or only system-RAM WB entries) + PAT enabled + GPU BAR not overlapped by any UC range → optimal, info-only, (b) GPU BAR overlapped by MTRR-UC → flag as silent perf killer, BIOS-side fix recipe + as last resort `mtrr_del` runtime recipe, (c) PAT disabled via `nopat` → recommend remove via `cmdline_audit.py`, (d) MTRR slot exhaustion → consolidate recipe, (e) WC over RAM → immediate flag. Surface "your `/proc/mtrr` shows 3 entries: reg0: base=0x0 size=2048MB type=write-back (system RAM, expected), reg1: base=0x80000000 size=1024MB type=write-back (extended RAM, expected), reg2: base=0x100000000 size=49152MB type=write-back (high RAM, expected). No MTRR entry overlaps your RTX 3090 BAR (0xa0000000-0xbfffffff per shipped #38.5 rebar_audit), PAT is enabled (`/sys/kernel/debug/x86/pat_memtype_list` shows `uncached-minus @ 0xa0000000-0xa00fffff` for the doorbell + `write-combining @ 0xa1000000-0xafffffff` for the framebuffer — both nvidia-driver-managed via ioremap_wc, optimal). Your GPU cacheability posture is healthy, no MTRR/PAT-related performance loss". Tiniest scope (~3-8 file reads), highest "I had no idea legacy MTRR could silently kill 30 % of my GPU command throughput" eye-opener for the GPU-perf-curious who've already exhausted nvidia-smi-side tuning. Niche audience (~5-10 % of users hit this) but extremely-high-payoff "explains my mysterious 30 % perf drop" feature for that slice. Pairs with shipped `rebar_audit.py`, `gpu_pci_bind.py`, `cmdline_audit.py`, `host_class.py`, parked #41.5 efi_secureboot. First : `modules/mtrr_pat_audit.py` + GET `/api/mtrr-pat-audit`. |
| 43.8 | **`/sys/fs/{ext4,xfs,btrfs,f2fs}/<dev>/{features,options,journal,sb,errors,...}` per-filesystem live-tunables + mount-state auditor — distinct from shipped `fs_mount_audit.py` (which reads `/proc/mounts` mount-option strings) — this is the *live filesystem driver state* side: journal mode active, error counters, feature flags, allocator behavior** | M | 3 | Shipped `fs_mount_audit.py` reads `/proc/mounts` for *mount option strings* (the static stuff: data=writeback, noatime, nodiratime, discard) — but each filesystem driver also exposes its *live runtime state* under `/sys/fs/<fstype>/<dev>/` (ext4 at `/sys/fs/ext4/<dev>/`, xfs at `/sys/fs/xfs/<dev>/`, btrfs at `/sys/fs/btrfs/<uuid>/`, f2fs at `/sys/fs/f2fs/<dev>/`) — and no shipped module reads any of these. The state includes per-fs error counters (have we seen filesystem errors since mount?), journal/transaction state (is the journal currently full-flushing?), feature flags (is `bigalloc` enabled? `casefold`? `verity`?), allocator preferences (ext4's `mb_stream_req`, xfs's `mp_xfs_allocator`), and a lot more. The recurring foot-guns: (1) ext4 `errors_count > 0` at `/sys/fs/ext4/<dev>/errors_count` → filesystem has detected internal errors since mount (typically silent corruption — kernel logged once to dmesg and went quiet), shipped `disk_health.py` covers SMART (device side), shipped `dr_bundle.py` covers boot-time fsck logs, but live ext4 error counter is unaudited → surface "your /data/models/ on ext4 has errors_count=4 since last mount (2026-05-21 03:14), first_error_time=2026-05-22 09:47, last_error_inode=12345678 — there's silent corruption, paste-ready `e2fsck -n /dev/nvme0n1p2` (dry run, safe) to investigate without unmount", (2) xfs `agcount` very high on small fs (e.g. 32 agcount on a 2 TB volume = 64 GiB per AG which is a defragmentation pessimization) → suggests filesystem was created with bad mkfs options, surface for transparency + reformat recommend, (3) btrfs `discard_async_*` counters → btrfs's async discard daemon stats (kernel ≥ 5.6); if `discard_async_failed > 0` your TRIM isn't working, cross-link shipped `trim_audit.py`, (4) ext4 `journal_path` → on rare setups the user moved the journal to a separate device (e.g. small Optane stick) for write durability; surface for transparency, (5) btrfs `commit_stats` showing very slow commit average (>500 ms) → CoW pressure / fragmentation, recommend `btrfs balance` recipe + `btrfs filesystem defragment`, (6) xfs `stats_clear` + `xstats` → xfs internal allocator stats (block-group-allocation hits/misses, log-write rate), useful for power-users tuning the xfs allocator, (7) ext4 `lifetime_write_kbytes` → total lifetime writes since mkfs, useful for tracking SSD wear vs SMART's reported wear, surface for transparency, (8) f2fs (rare on homelab but appearing for cheap SD-card-based boot media on some SBCs) — `f2fs.<dev>/{features,segment_info,cp_interval,gc_*}` for the GC pressure, surface if present, (9) `casefold` (case-insensitive filenames, kernel ≥ 5.2 feature) + GGUF filenames with case-variant duplicates (`Llama-70b.gguf` vs `llama-70b.gguf`) → both appear as one file on casefold-enabled directories, can surprise the user; surface fairness note, (10) btrfs `subvolume_id` and snapshot count → if `> 50` snapshots accumulated from autosnap tools, btrfs gets slow on mmap-heavy workloads, recommend snapshot prune. Walk `/sys/fs/{ext4,xfs,btrfs,f2fs}/*/` (one subdir per device/uuid), read each filesystem's exposed tunables (the file set varies by fs type — careful per-fs handling), per-fs map sysfs path → mount point via shipped `fs_mount_audit.py`'s `/proc/mounts` parse, cross-reference shipped `disk_health.py` (device SMART), `nvme_iosched.py` (block-layer scheduler), `trim_audit.py` (discard health), `fs_mount_audit.py` (mount options), `dr_bundle.py` (boot-time fsck), `host_class.py` (container guests use host fs), classify into 5 per-fs verdicts: (a) error counter zero + journal healthy + commit_stats normal → healthy, info-only summary of feature flags, (b) ext4 `errors_count > 0` → flag with `e2fsck -n` recipe, (c) btrfs slow commit / high snapshot count → balance + snapshot-prune recipe, (d) xfs misaligned agcount → reformat recommend (low-confidence, advanced), (e) f2fs gc pressure high → recommend `fsck.f2fs -f`. Surface "your /data/models on /dev/nvme0n1p2 (ext4) audit: features=`has_journal ext_attr resize_inode dir_index filetype needs_recovery extent 64bit flex_bg sparse_super large_file huge_file uninit_bg dir_nlink extra_isize metadata_csum`, journal_path=internal, journal_size=1024MB (default), errors_count=0 (clean since mount 2026-05-19 14:32), lifetime_write_kbytes=1.4 PiB (heavy use — typical for inference rig), mb_stream_req=16 (default), commit_interval=5s (mount-time setting per shipped fs_mount_audit). Your filesystem is healthy. Feature flags suggest a standard Ubuntu 24.04 mkfs.ext4 — nothing exotic. Bonus: `/sys/fs/ext4/<dev>/inode_readahead_blks=32` is default — for large-GGUF mmap workloads bump to 128 for better metadata readahead, paste-ready `echo 128 | sudo tee /sys/fs/ext4/nvme0n1p2/inode_readahead_blks` + persist via udev. Your /home on /dev/nvme0n1p3 (btrfs, separate volume): subvolume_id=5, snapshot_count=8 (Timeshift weekly, healthy), commit_stats.last_commit_ns=234ms (healthy, < 500 ms threshold), discard_async_failed=0 (TRIM working per shipped trim_audit)". Effort is M because each fs type has a different sysfs layout (ext4 ≈ 30 files, xfs ≈ 25, btrfs ≈ 40-60 per fs-uuid, f2fs ≈ 50) and dispatching cleanly + tolerating missing kernel features needs care, but stdlib-only. Niche audience (filesystem-curious power-users — ~5-10 %) but high-payoff "I had no idea my ext4 had errors silently accumulating" feature. Pairs with shipped `fs_mount_audit.py`, `disk_health.py`, `nvme_iosched.py`, `trim_audit.py`, `dr_bundle.py`, `host_class.py`. First : `modules/fs_live_tunables.py` + GET `/api/fs-live-tunables`. |

**Top 4 (fit × urgency)** :
