1. 43.1 `/proc/interrupts` + `/proc/softirqs` per-CPU IRQ-rate + softirq-imbalance auditor — S, fit 5, the cleanest "what's actually firing on my CPUs right now and which one is overloaded" answer for the multi-engine homelab inference rig where NET_RX softirq pinning to cpu0 silently jitters inference whenever ollama is downloading a model. Parses `/proc/interrupts` (per-IRQ-per-CPU counters) + `/proc/softirqs` (NET_RX / TIMER / RCU / SCHED / BLOCK per-CPU dispatch counters) at two snapshots ~10 s apart, computes per-IRQ stddev across CPUs (high stddev = imbalance), classifies into 5 verdicts (balanced / NET_RX-pin / RCU-storm / nvidia-IRQ-storm / NVMe-pin-cpu0), each with paste-ready `smp_affinity` rebalance or `irqbalance.service` recipe. Pairs with shipped `gpu_irq_affinity.py`, `msi_inventory.py`, `nic_queue_affinity.py`, `numa_placement.py`. First : `modules/irq_rates_audit.py`.
2. 43.2 `/sys/block/*/queue/*` block-layer per-queue knob auditor (15 knobs beyond just `scheduler`) — S, fit 5, the cleanest "I tuned my NVMe scheduler per shipped #36.6 but didn't know there were 14 more knobs" answer for the I-already-tuned-the-obvious-stuff power-user. Reads `nr_requests` / `read_ahead_kb` / `rq_affinity` / `nomerges` / `wbt_lat_usec` / `write_cache` / `max_sectors_kb` / `io_poll` / `iostats` / `add_random` / `nr_zones` etc. per block device, filters to those holding `/data/models/` per shipped service-discovery, classifies into 5 verdicts (defaults-on-fast-NVMe / nr_requests-low / wbt-blocking-mixed / rq_affinity-wrong-for-pinned / iostats-breaks-tooling), produces a paste-ready single-udev-rule that applies all recommended bumps atomically. Pairs with shipped `nvme_iosched.py`, `disk_health.py`, `fs_mount_audit.py`, `nvme_swap.py`. First : `modules/block_queue_audit.py`.
3. 43.3 `/proc/zoneinfo` + `/proc/vmstat` per-zone watermark + reclaim auditor — S, fit 4, the cleanest "my first prefill is sometimes mysteriously slow even on a 96 GB box" answer for the direct-reclaim-stall pattern that hits llama.cpp's first GGUF mmap-fault burst when `vm.min_free_kbytes` is too low. Reads per-zone (`Normal` / `DMA32` / `DMA` / `Movable`) `free / min / low / high / boost` watermarks plus `pgscan_kswapd_*` / `pgscan_direct_*` / `compact_fail` / `numa_foreign` / `oom_kill` counters at two snapshots ~30 s apart, classifies into 5 verdicts (healthy / direct-reclaim-active / compaction-failing / NUMA-foreign-allocs / OOM-recent), each with paste-ready `vm.min_free_kbytes` bump sized to detected workload. Pairs with shipped `buddyinfo_frag.py`, `vm_tuning_deep.py`, `thp_audit.py`, `oom_priority.py`. First : `modules/zoneinfo_audit.py`.
4. 43.4 `/sys/class/net/<iface>/statistics/*` NIC ring-buffer drop + FIFO-overrun monitor — XS, fit 4, the cleanest "I had no idea I was dropping 2 % of my HF download packets at the NIC ring level" answer for the user who's already audited link-state per shipped `nic_health.py` and host-side sysctls per `net_sysctl_audit.py` but never looked at per-NIC drop counters. Reads `rx_dropped` / `tx_dropped` / `rx_fifo_errors` / `rx_missed_errors` / `rx_crc_errors` / `rx_length_errors` / `rx_frame_errors` / `collisions` per UP non-loopback interface, two snapshots ~30 s apart for rate computation, classifies into 5 verdicts (zero-drops / ring-too-small / PCIe-starvation / physical-fault / MTU-mismatch), paste-ready `ethtool -G` ring-bump + persist via `/etc/systemd/network/*.link`. Pairs with shipped `nic_health.py`, `nic_queue_affinity.py`, `pcie_width_watcher.py`, `net_sysctl_audit.py`. First : `modules/nic_ring_audit.py`.

## R&D #42 survey (started 2026-05-23)

8 fresh angles, none in shipped list (#6-41 — including just-shipped `zswap_zram_audit` / `edac_ram_ecc` / `panic_policy` / `inotify_audit` from cycle #41), modules tree (148 files), or parked bench (27.2/27.5/27.6/27.8 + 28.2/28.3/28.6/28.8 + 29.2/29.4/29.5/29.6 + 30.4/30.6/30.7/30.8 + 31.5/31.6/31.7/31.8 + 32.2/32.6/32.7/32.8 + 33.3/33.5/33.7/33.8 + 34.5/34.6/34.7/34.8 + 35.5/35.6/35.7/35.8 + 36.5/36.6/36.7/36.8 + 37.5/37.6/37.7/37.8 + 38.5/38.6/38.7/38.8 + 39.5/39.6/39.7/39.8 + 40.5/40.6/40.7/40.8 + 41.5/41.6/41.7/41.8). Stdlib + jsonschema only, single-GPU desktops / homelab focus (RTX 3090 reference), GUI-controllable from Svelte 5 settings.

| # | Feature | Effort | Fit | Why it matters / first module |
|---|---------|--------|-----|-------|
| 42.1 | **`/proc/locks` + per-PID `/proc/<pid>/fdinfo/<fd>` advisory + mandatory file-lock contention auditor — model-file `flock()` / fcntl POSIX-lock / OFD-lock race detector for ollama vs llama-server vs vllm vs koboldcpp racing on the same GGUF mmap** | S | 5 | The single most invisible foot-gun on a busy homelab inference rig running *multiple* engines back-to-back: ollama daemon owns `~/.ollama/models/blobs/sha256-<digest>` via an `flock(LOCK_EX)` taken at model-load and *never released until the daemon exits*, then the user `ollama stop`-s the model but the daemon-level lock persists, then the user fires up llama-server pointing at the same GGUF (a common pattern — ollama for chat, llama-server for benchmark / scripted RAG), llama-server's `mmap()` succeeds *but* its `fcntl(F_SETLK, F_WRLCK)` for index-update races against the residual ollama lock, llama-server retries silently every 100 ms and the first prefill silently stalls 3-30 seconds with no log entry on either side. Even worse: when the user `ollama serve`-s in one terminal and `llama-server --mmap` in another against the same file, the kernel's `/proc/locks` shows the contention but neither tool surfaces it — the user sees "llama-server seems slow today" and never connects it to the still-running ollama daemon they forgot about. The kernel exposes the complete lock table at `/proc/locks` (one line per lock, format: `<id>: <type> <kind> <mode> <pid> <maj>:<min>:<inode> <start> <end>` — type ∈ `POSIX` / `FLOCK` / `OFDLCK` / `LEASE` / `DELEG`, kind ∈ `ADVISORY` / `MANDATORY`, mode ∈ `READ` / `WRITE`) and per-PID `/proc/<pid>/fdinfo/<fd>` shows `lock:` entries for OFD-locks (kernel ≥ 3.15) — but no shipped module reads either. The recurring foot-guns the audit catches: (1) two PIDs hold conflicting EXCL flock on the same `(maj,min,inode)` of a GGUF blob → the second one is blocking, surface "ollama PID 12834 holds FLOCK WRITE on /home/user/.ollama/models/blobs/sha256-abc123, llama-server PID 14091 is waiting for the same lock — kill ollama or `ollama stop --keep-loaded=false` to release", (2) systemd-tmpfiles / systemd-journald holding LEASE locks on `/tmp` files that LM Studio's GGUF download lands in → silent download stall, surface "LM Studio download blocked by systemd-journald LEASE on `/tmp/llm-studio-download-X.gguf`", (3) NFS-mounted model directory (the homelab user with a Synology NAS exporting `/volume1/models/`) → `/proc/locks` shows `POSIX` locks from `lockd` kernel thread, lock recovery on NFS reconnect can take 30-90 s during which inference appears frozen, surface clearly, (4) Btrfs-on-NVMe model directory with a subvolume snapshot in progress → `/proc/locks` shows a transient FLOCK from `btrfs-transacti` kernel thread that briefly blocks model-file reads, surface as a known harmless-but-noisy condition, (5) OFD-lock leak: a Python script using `fcntl.flock()` with `LOCK_EX | LOCK_NB` that crashed before releasing → the kernel cleans up via process-death FD-close, but a process that just *forks-and-stays-alive* without releasing leaves the lock indefinitely; surface "PID 9912 (python3 my-script.py) holds an OFD-lock on /data/models/llama-70b.gguf since 2026-05-20 14:32 — last access via `lsof` was 4 days ago, likely a leaked lock from a crashed flask app, kill the PID to release", (6) mandatory locks (the rare `mount -o mand` case + `chmod g+s,g-x` file mode) → MANDATORY mode in `/proc/locks` means *any* read/write blocks not just `fcntl()` users, ancient feature but devastating when accidentally enabled — flag immediately. Parse `/proc/locks` (whitespace-tokenized lines), join via `(maj:min:inode)` against the shipped service-discovery PIDs' `/proc/<pid>/maps` (mmap'd files) + `/proc/<pid>/fd/*` symlinks (open files), per-PID walk `/proc/<pid>/fdinfo/<fd>` for OFD-lock annotations (lines starting `lock:`), cross-reference the inode against `/proc/mounts` to identify NFS / Btrfs / overlayfs / tmpfs source filesystems (different lock semantics), shipped `service_discovery.py` (inference PIDs), shipped `fs_mount_audit.py` (mount option `mand` / `nosuid` context), shipped `host_class.py` (skip on container guests where lock namespacing may obscure), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) zero conflicting locks across all inference PIDs → optimal, congratulate, (b) two inference engines hold EXCL flock on the same GGUF inode → contention, name both PIDs + paste-ready "stop the holder" recipe, (c) PID-N holds a lock with no shared inode but the file is gone (`/proc/locks` shows the inode, `lsof -i:<pid>` shows nothing) → leaked lock, paste-ready kill recipe, (d) NFS/lockd lock contention → reconnect-stall warning + `/proc/fs/nfsfs/{servers,volumes}` cross-link, (e) mandatory-mode lock present → unconditional flag, `find / -perm /2000 -not -perm /0010` to identify the file + paste-ready `chmod g-s` recipe. Surface "your `/proc/locks` shows 47 locks total, 2 conflicts on inode 1234567 (/home/user/.ollama/models/blobs/sha256-abc123, 24.1 GiB GGUF on `/dev/nvme0n1p2` ext4): ollama daemon PID 12834 holds FLOCK WRITE since boot, llama-server PID 14091 is blocked waiting for FLOCK WRITE since 14:37:12 (8 min 23 s) — your `llama-server` is currently stalled on prefill because of the residual ollama daemon lock; either `ollama stop` (releases on next idle cycle, ~30 s) or `systemctl stop ollama` (immediate) — paste-ready recipe". Single highest-leverage missing diagnostic for the multi-engine homelab pattern (ollama for chat + llama-server for benchmark + vllm for batch + koboldcpp for roleplay = the four-engine cohabitation pattern that dominates power-user setups) that today no OSS tool surfaces. Pairs with shipped `service_discovery.py` (inference PIDs), shipped `fs_mount_audit.py` (mount semantics), shipped `proc_static_audit.py`, shipped `proc_deep_state.py`. First : `modules/file_locks_audit.py` + GET `/api/file-locks-audit`. |
| 42.2 | **`/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/topology/{cluster_id,cluster_cpus,die_id,die_cpus,package_cpus,book_id,drawer_id,ppin}` heterogeneous-CPU cluster-aware topology auditor — P/E cluster + chiplet + Snapdragon X / Lunar Lake compute-tile mapper for surgical pinning beyond `cpu_topology.py`** | S | 5 | Shipped `cpu_topology.py` covers the *classic* Linux CPU-topology trio (physical_package_id, core_id, thread_siblings_list) — sufficient for Zen3 monolithic + Skylake — but the topology landscape since 2022 has *exploded*: Alder/Raptor/Meteor/Arrow Lake split CPUs into P-clusters (4 cores @ 5.7 GHz, hyperthreaded) + E-clusters (4-16 cores @ 4.2 GHz, no SMT, with their own L2-shared cluster boundary), Zen4/Zen5 multi-CCD parts (7950X / 7900X / 9950X / 9900X) place 8 cores per CCD with each CCD a separate L3-shared cluster + separate `die_id`, Snapdragon X Elite (rare on homelab but appearing on the cheap-eBay-Surface-Laptop slice) has Oryon performance cluster + Oryon-M efficiency cluster, Lunar Lake / Panther Lake adds a *third* compute tile (LP-E cores on the SoC tile, separate `cluster_id` from both the P-cluster and E-cluster), Sapphire Rapids server tiles each get their own `die_id`, and the kernel ≥ 5.16 exposes `cluster_id` / `cluster_cpus` (the L2-shared boundary, narrower than die-shared L3) plus `die_id` / `die_cpus` (chiplet boundary) at `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/topology/`. The recurring foot-guns shipped `cpu_topology.py` misses: (1) Alder Lake 12900K: shipped reports `physical_package_id=0` for all 24 logical CPUs (1 package), `thread_siblings_list` for P-core SMT pairs — but cannot distinguish "P-cluster 0 (cores 0-7 logical, 0-1/2-3/4-5/6-7 SMT-paired physical 0-3)" from "E-cluster 0 (cores 16-19)" from "E-cluster 1 (cores 20-23)"; shipped #40.7 cpufreq_domains (parked) covered the cpufreq side, but the *cache-locality* side (which 4 E-cores share an L2?) requires `cluster_id` / `cluster_cpus`, and surgical inference pinning needs "pin to P-cluster 0 (cores 0-7), keep E-clusters for OS background" precisely, (2) 7950X 16C/32T: shipped reports 16 unique core_ids + SMT siblings, but cannot identify "CCD 0 = cores 0-7 + SMT siblings 16-23, CCD 1 = cores 8-15 + SMT siblings 24-31" (the load-bearing fact for keeping all inference threads on one CCD to share L3); `die_id` distinguishes the CCDs cleanly, (3) Snapdragon X Elite: shipped finds 12 cores all reporting `physical_package_id=0` + no SMT + no cache info on some kernels — the *actual* P-vs-M cluster split is exposed via `cluster_id` (P-cluster cores have one cluster_id, M-cluster cores another), without it the inference scheduler treats all 12 cores as equal and runs at average ~75 % of peak, (4) Lunar Lake 258V: 4 P-cores + 4 E-cores + 2 LP-E cores (on SoC tile, *not* the compute tile) — three distinct clusters, `cluster_id` tells them apart; pinning inference onto LP-E by accident costs 60-70 % vs P-cluster, (5) `ppin` (Protected Processor Identification Number, kernel ≥ 5.18 on Intel + AMD recent) → unique 64-bit ID per CPU, useful for the "is this the same CPU I benchmarked yesterday or did the user RMA it" workstation-fleet detector for the homelab user with 2-3 boxes, (6) S/390x mainframe terms `book_id` / `drawer_id` → not relevant for x86 homelab, surface "not applicable" cleanly to avoid noise. Walk `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/topology/{cluster_id,cluster_cpus,cluster_cpus_list,die_id,die_cpus,die_cpus_list,package_cpus,package_cpus_list,thread_siblings_list,core_siblings_list,ppin}` plus `/sys/devices/system/cpu/types/intel_atom/cpus` + `/sys/devices/system/cpu/types/intel_core/cpus` (kernel ≥ 6.5, explicit P/E listing), cross-reference shipped `cpu_topology.py` (classic trio), shipped `cpu_cache_topology.py` (L1/L2/L3 sharing — `cluster_cpus` typically == L2-shared set), shipped `smt_audit.py` (SMT toggle), shipped `gpu_cpu_affinity.py` (PCIe-local cores — pick the cluster *closest* to PCIe), shipped `numa_placement.py`, classify into 5 topology classes: (a) monolithic single-cluster (Zen3 5950X, all cores in one cluster + die) → no cluster-aware advice needed, (b) multi-CCD Zen (7950X / 7900X / 9950X) → recommend "inference on CCD 0 only, taskset -c <cluster_cpus of die 0>", paste-ready snippet, (c) Intel hybrid P+E (Alder/Raptor/Meteor/Arrow) → recommend "P-cluster for inference, E-clusters for OS background — pin llama-server with taskset -c <cluster_cpus where cluster_id matches P-cluster>", (d) Lunar/Panther Lake P+E+LP-E → recommend "P-cluster primary, E-cluster spillover, *avoid LP-E* (SoC-tile, ~60 % perf)", (e) ARM (Snapdragon X, Apple-Silicon-via-Asahi) → recommend "P-cluster only, surface M-cluster as background" + fairness note on absent SMT. Surface "your 14900K topology: 1 package, 2 dies (compute tile + SoC tile), 3 clusters (cluster_id=0 P-cluster cores 0-15 SMT-paired, cluster_id=4 E-cluster-0 cores 16-19, cluster_id=8 E-cluster-1 cores 20-23) — inference should pin to P-cluster only via `taskset -c 0-15`, leaving E-clusters for OS background. Shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity says your RTX 3090 is PCIe-attached to NUMA node 0 = same package, all clusters PCIe-equidistant on a desktop board, so cluster choice = perf-only not PCIe-affinity — paste-ready systemd Drop-In below". The cleanest answer to the "I have a 14900K / 7950X / Snapdragon X and shipped #37.2's pinning advice gave me a flat list of 16/24/12 cores but I have no idea which subset is the perf cluster" complaint that today no OSS Linux tool surfaces with a single classified verdict + paste-ready recipe. Pairs with shipped `cpu_topology.py` (classic trio), `cpu_cache_topology.py` (L1/L2/L3), `smt_audit.py`, `gpu_cpu_affinity.py`, `numa_placement.py`, parked #40.7 cpufreq_domains (cpufreq-domain side). First : `modules/hybrid_cpu_topo.py` + GET `/api/hybrid-cpu-topo`. |
| 42.3 | **`/sys/class/thermal/cooling_device*/{type,cur_state,max_state}` + `/sys/class/thermal/thermal_zone*/cdev*` + `cdev*_trip_point` + `cdev*_weight` cooling-device inventory + thermal-zone-to-fan binding graph auditor — distinct from shipped `thermal_zones.py` (which reads sensors only)** | XS | 5 | Shipped `thermal_zones.py` walks `/sys/class/thermal/thermal_zone*/{temp,type,trip_point_*_temp,trip_point_*_type,policy}` — the *sensor + trip-point* side of the thermal subsystem (what temperatures are read, at what thresholds does the kernel act). But the *actuator* side — *which fans / power-limit-throttles / processor-throttle controllers does the kernel actually drive when a trip fires?* — lives in a separate sysfs branch at `/sys/class/thermal/cooling_device*/` (one entry per cooling actuator: chassis fans, CPU power-limit controllers `intel_powerclamp`/`amd_pstate-cpufreq`, processor-throttle device `Processor`, GPU-side throttle, battery-charge-rate limiter on laptops) and the *binding* — "trip point 2 of thermal_zone3 drives cooling_device7 with weight 100 to limit it between cur_state=0 and max_state=10" — lives in `/sys/class/thermal/thermal_zone*/cdev*` (symlinks to the bound cooling_devices) plus `cdev<N>_trip_point` (which trip ties to this cdev) plus `cdev<N>_weight` (relative aggressiveness vs other zones driving the same cdev). The complete *thermal control graph* is invisible today: which fan responds to which sensor crossing which trip point? when the kernel says "passive throttling at trip 1", which `intel_powerclamp` device is actually being pulsed? on a Threadripper with 5 chassis fans wired through the chipset, which fan is bound to the CPU package thermal zone vs the GPU thermal zone vs the NVMe thermal zone? The recurring foot-guns: (1) `Processor` cooling-device present with `cur_state > 0` for >30 s → CPU is actively being throttled by ACPI `_PSV` passive-cooling logic (independent of intel_pstate's HWP), shipped `throttle_cause.py` knows GPU throttles + shipped `cpu_boost.py` knows turbo gate but neither watches ACPI _PSV passive throttle, surface "your CPU is being held at cur_state=3 of max_state=10 by ACPI passive cooling — your `intel_pstate` and HWP_EPP look optimal but the *firmware* is throttling underneath, check thermal_zone with trip type=`passive` at 95 °C", (2) `Fan` cooling-device with `max_state=255` reporting `cur_state=10` constantly → PWM fan stuck low because trip points are too high (BIOS-default ACPI fan curves on consumer Asus / MSI boards often have passive trip at 95 °C + active trip at 100 °C, so the kernel barely drives the fan above idle until the CPU is already hot), recommend re-tuning trip points via shipped `fan_curve.py` (alternate path), (3) cooling_device of type `intel_powerclamp` bound to thermal_zone of type `x86_pkg_temp` → kernel-side idle injection ("force CPU to halt N % of the time") is the *fallback* throttle Intel uses on consumer boards without proper firmware throttle, on inference workloads `intel_powerclamp` engaging means 5-25 % t/s loss with no log entry, surface clearly, (4) `cdev*_weight` non-uniform across multiple zones binding to one fan → asymmetric drive (the GPU's thermal_zone has weight=100, the NVMe's has weight=10 → fan responds to GPU 10× more than to NVMe overheating), can be intentional or BIOS-default-wrong; surface for transparency, (5) RTX 3090's `nvidia` cooling-device (newer kernels expose GPU fan as a cooling_device alongside the GPU's own thermal_zone) — `cur_state` here is the kernel-driven GPU fan duty cycle override, shipped `fan_curve.py` covers the nvidia-smi side but not the kernel-side cdev binding, (6) NVMe drive's thermal_zone bound to a chassis fan via `cdev*` → laptop / SFF chassis cools NVMe via the same fan as CPU, the user sees "NVMe is 70 °C while CPU is 45 °C and fan is barely spinning" because CPU's thermal_zone has higher weight than NVMe's, recommend bumping NVMe weight, (7) cooling_device present but unbound to any zone (`cdev<N>` symlinks don't include it from any zone) → orphaned actuator the kernel never drives, the BIOS exposes it but no ACPI _PSL/_AC0/_AC1 list ties it → user can drive it manually via `cur_state` write, surface as advanced opportunity. Walk `/sys/class/thermal/cooling_device*/` (read `type` — string identifier like `Processor`, `intel_powerclamp`, `Fan`, `nvidia`, `iwlwifi_1` for WiFi cards' thermal throttle, etc.; read `cur_state` + `max_state` numeric), walk `/sys/class/thermal/thermal_zone*/` (already mostly handled by shipped `thermal_zones.py` — augment with `cdev<N>` symlinks + `cdev<N>_trip_point` + `cdev<N>_weight` reads), build the bipartite binding graph "zone → trip point → weight → cdev", classify into 5 verdicts: (a) all cdevs bound + cur_state mostly 0 + clear binding graph → healthy, surface the graph as info, (b) `Processor` or `intel_powerclamp` cdev with sustained `cur_state > 0` → active passive throttling, cross-link shipped `throttle_cause.py` + `cpu_boost.py` + suggest trip-point adjustment, (c) `Fan` cdev with `cur_state` low while bound zone reports temp > trip_point → fan curve stuck, recommend shipped `fan_curve.py` re-tune, (d) one cdev driven by N zones with asymmetric weights → flag for transparency + recommend rebalance if NVMe-undercooled pattern detected, (e) orphan cdev (no zone references it) → manual-only actuator, surface as "you have a `<cdev type>` exposed by BIOS but no thermal zone drives it — you can write 0-N to `cur_state` manually if needed". Surface "your box has 5 cooling_devices: Processor (cur=0/max=10), intel_powerclamp (cur=0/max=50), Fan (cur=3/max=255, drives system chassis fan), nvidia (cur=0/max=100, RTX 3090 GPU fan kernel override unused — nvidia-smi owns it per shipped fan_curve.py), iwlwifi_1 (cur=0/max=5, WiFi card thermal cap). Thermal_zone bindings: x86_pkg_temp passes trip 1 (90 °C passive) → Processor (weight 100) + intel_powerclamp (weight 50); acpitz passes trip 2 (75 °C active) → Fan (weight 100). Last hour cur_state log: Processor 0 throughout, intel_powerclamp 0 throughout, Fan oscillated 2-7 — your cooling graph is healthy, no passive throttle engaged. If you want more aggressive fan response below 75 °C, lower `acpitz/trip_point_2_temp` to 65 °C — paste-ready snippet (requires reboot to apply via `/etc/tmpfiles.d/` because trip_point_*_temp is RW only on some boards)". Tiniest scope (~5 cdev reads × ~5 zones × bindings ≈ 50 file reads), biggest "I finally understand what fan the kernel is actually controlling and why" eye-opener for the cooling-curious audience that has already read shipped `thermal_zones.py` + `fan_curve.py` and asked the next question. Pairs with shipped `thermal_zones.py` (sensors + trips), `fan_curve.py` (nvidia-smi fan curve side), `throttle_cause.py` + `cpu_boost.py` (CPU-side throttle correlate), `thermal_slowdown_kind.py` (GPU thermal slowdown bits). First : `modules/cooling_devices.py` + GET `/api/cooling-devices`. |
| 42.4 | **`/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/power/energy_perf_bias` legacy EPB knob auditor — Intel x86_energy_perf_policy + AMD EEMR Energy-Perf-Bias-vs-HWP-EPP-vs-EPP-string interplay diagnostic (the third axis shipped `hwp_epp.py` doesn't cover)** | XS | 4 | Modern Intel/AMD x86 CPUs expose *three independent* energy-vs-performance policy knobs that interact in non-obvious ways and frequently contradict each other: (1) the *legacy* EPB (Energy-Performance-Bias) MSR knob exposed at `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/power/energy_perf_bias` as a 0-15 integer (0=performance, 6=balance, 15=powersave — the original Nehalem-era knob driven by the deprecated `x86_energy_perf_policy` userspace tool), (2) the *current* HWP-EPP (Hardware-P-state Energy-Perf-Preference) knob exposed at `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/cpufreq/energy_performance_preference` as a string (`performance` / `balance_performance` / `default` / `balance_power` / `power` — the Skylake-onward knob that shipped `hwp_epp.py` already audits), (3) the `cpufreq` governor itself at `scaling_governor` (`performance` / `powersave` for intel_pstate active-mode, richer set for acpi_cpufreq passive-mode — shipped `pstate_audit.py` covers this). Shipped `hwp_epp.py` covers knob #2, shipped `pstate_audit.py` covers knob #3 — but *knob #1* (legacy EPB) is unaudited and frequently *contradicts* knobs #2/#3. The recurring foot-guns: (1) `energy_perf_bias=15` (powersave, often a BIOS-default-conservative on consumer laptops + some prebuilt OEM desktops like Dell XPS / HP Omen) + `energy_performance_preference=performance` (user set via `cpupower` after reading a tuning guide) + `scaling_governor=performance` → the EPB knob *wins on some microarchitectures* (specifically older Skylake-X + Haswell-EP, which still honor EPB over HWP-EPP), so the user's "performance" CPU is silently throttled by an MSR they didn't know existed, ~5-15 % t/s loss on prefill, (2) `energy_perf_bias=0` (performance) + idle box → +5-10 W idle power because EPB=0 disables some C-state-entry hints; on a desktop fine, on a closet-server that's $20/year extra electricity, surface with "you have EPB=0 (performance) on a 24/7 box, +8 W idle cost; if your inference workload is bursty (< 30 % duty cycle) consider EPB=6 (balance) for parity perf + lower idle", (3) `energy_perf_bias` value changes after suspend/resume (a known kernel-resume-path bug on some Lenovo ThinkPad models — EPB reset to firmware default after resume from S3), surface "your EPB was 0 at boot but is now 7 after the last resume — known kernel bug on ThinkPad X1 Gen 11, paste-ready `/etc/systemd/system/restore-epb.service` post-resume hook", (4) AMD-side: `amd_pstate_epp` driver (kernel ≥ 6.3) exposes EPP-string at the same `energy_performance_preference` path *and* honors EEMR (Energy-Efficient Mode Register, AMD's EPB-equivalent) at the same `energy_perf_bias` path on Zen3+ — same triple-knob contradiction risk as Intel; shipped `hwp_epp.py` may or may not handle the AMD path cleanly (worth surfacing the AMD pathway distinctly), (5) per-CPU EPB inconsistency (cpu0 has EPB=0, cpu8 has EPB=6) → typically a `cpupower frequency-set -r` partial-run; surface "47 of 48 CPUs have EPB=0 but cpu23 has EPB=6 — your EPB is inconsistent, write 0 to all with the snippet below", (6) `energy_perf_bias` absent (module `msr` not loaded, or `/dev/cpu/*/msr` access blocked by Secure Boot lockdown) → can't read or write, surface "your kernel has lockdown=integrity active per shipped #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (parked), MSR access blocked — EPB cannot be tuned at runtime, set via BIOS only or boot with `lockdown=none`". Read `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/power/energy_perf_bias` per CPU (single integer 0-15 if present, ENOENT on AMD pre-Zen3 or Intel pre-Sandy Bridge), cross-reference shipped `hwp_epp.py` (knob #2, EPP string per CPU), `pstate_audit.py` (knob #3, scaling_driver + governor), `cpu_microcode.py` (microarch detection — Skylake-X / Haswell-EP need the "EPB-wins" warning, modern Zen5 / Lunar Lake don't), `host_class.py` (laptop vs desktop matters for the "powersave default = OEM trap" detection), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) EPB unanimous + consistent with HWP-EPP + governor → optimal, congratulate ("EPB=0 + EPP=performance + governor=performance, your three energy-perf knobs agree, no hidden contradiction"), (b) EPB contradicts HWP-EPP on a microarch where EPB wins (Haswell-EP / Skylake-X / Broadwell-EP) → flag silent throttle, paste-ready `cpupower set-perf-bias -p 0` snippet, (c) EPB unanimous=0 on a 24/7 idle box → idle-power cost note + EPB=6 recommendation, (d) per-CPU EPB inconsistent → flag + uniform-set snippet, (e) EPB absent (MSR access blocked or pre-Sandy-Bridge / pre-Zen3) → "not applicable" fairness note. Surface "your 7950X cpu0-31 EPB readback: all 0 (performance, kernel-default for amd_pstate_epp + EEMR), HWP-EPP=performance (shipped #36.4), governor=performance (shipped #pstate_audit) → three knobs agree, optimal for prefill-bound inference; cost is ~12 W extra idle vs EPB=6 — if your duty cycle <30 % consider downshift" or "your 14900K cpu0-23 EPB readback: cpu0-21 EPB=0, cpu22-23 EPB=6 (E-cluster-1) — inconsistent, partial cpupower run; bring all to EPB=0 with `for c in /sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/power/energy_perf_bias; do echo 0 | sudo tee $c; done` (paste-ready) and persist via `/etc/systemd/system/set-epb.service` Drop-In so it survives reboot + suspend/resume". Tiniest scope (~24-48 file reads on a typical box + 5 classification branches), highest "I had no idea there was a *third* energy-perf knob silently overriding my carefully-tuned HWP-EPP" eye-opener for the audience that already read shipped `hwp_epp.py` + `pstate_audit.py` + `cpu_boost.py`. Pairs with shipped `hwp_epp.py` (knob #2), `pstate_audit.py` (knob #3), `cpu_boost.py` (turbo gate, knob #4), `cpu_microcode.py` (microarch determines EPB-vs-EPP precedence), `host_class.py` (laptop OEM trap detection), parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (lockdown blocks MSR). First : `modules/cpu_epb.py` + GET `/api/cpu-epb`. |
| 42.5 | **`/proc/sys/kernel/random/{entropy_avail,poolsize,urandom_min_reseed_secs,write_wakeup_threshold,read_wakeup_threshold}` + `/sys/class/hwrng/*/{rng_current,rng_available,rng_quality}` + `/sys/class/tpm/tpm*/{tpm_version_major,active_pcr_banks}` kernel RNG + hwrng + TPM entropy-source health auditor for getrandom-blocking + LUKS-unlock + TLS-to-HF stalls** | XS | 3 | The Linux kernel's `getrandom(2)` syscall (used by *every* TLS handshake the dashboard makes to `huggingface.co` to download models, every `cryptsetup luksOpen` on a LUKS-encrypted `/data/models/` partition at boot, every `ollama pull` that establishes HTTPS with manifest registries, every Python `secrets` / `uuid.uuid4` call in user RAG pipelines) *blocks until the kernel CRNG is initialized* — typically a few seconds after boot on a hardware-RNG-equipped box (RDRAND/RDSEED on x86, TPM-RNG, dedicated hwrng on some homelab boards) but potentially *minutes* on a headless server with no hardware RNG, no jitter source, and a minimal-IO boot profile. The boot-time symptom: SSH listening but `cryptsetup` hangs for 30-90 s waiting for entropy, the dashboard's first HF download stalls for 10 minutes on first boot after a fresh install, the user blames "Hugging Face is slow today" while actually their kernel CRNG hadn't been seeded yet because `haveged` wasn't installed and no hwrng was loaded. The runtime symptom: a worst-case kernel can throttle `getrandom()` if the CRNG entropy estimate drops (since kernel ≥ 5.18 this is much less common — the kernel uses a unified BCRYPT-style CSPRNG that doesn't "drain" — but on kernels 4.19 - 5.17 still seen). The recurring foot-guns: (1) `/proc/sys/kernel/random/entropy_avail` < 1000 + active LUKS device mounted → next `luksResume` after suspend will hang briefly; surface as a known boot-after-resume issue, (2) `/sys/class/hwrng/` empty (no hardware RNG loaded) on a box that has `tpm_crb` or `tpm_tis` driver in dmesg + an active /dev/tpm0 → TPM-RNG available but `tpm-rng.ko` not loaded → recommend `modprobe tpm-rng` (+ `/etc/modules-load.d/tpm-rng.conf` for boot persistence), (3) `rng_current=virtio_rng` on a VM guest + no host-side entropy provisioning (Proxmox VE host without `rng-tools` running) → guest sees `entropy_avail` always around the wakeup threshold, all `getrandom()` calls slow, recommend Proxmox host-side `apt install rng-tools` + `virtio-rng-pci` device configured for the guest, (4) RDRAND/RDSEED disabled in BIOS (rare but seen on Ryzen with `Power-Down Enable=Disabled` + "core_perf_boost=disabled" combo that some power-supply-stability guides recommend) → CPU-RNG instruction not available, `/sys/class/hwrng/` may be empty, surface for transparency, (5) headless server + no TPM + no hwrng + `haveged.service` not installed → CRNG seed comes from jitter only, first boot after install takes 30-90 s of "kernel: random: crng init done" delay; recommend either `apt install haveged` or installing a `rng-tools`-driven TPM-RNG path, (6) TPM present + `active_pcr_banks` empty (TPM in disabled state from BIOS) → `tpm-rng` registered but produces no entropy, surface, (7) FIPS-mode kernel (`/proc/sys/crypto/fips_enabled=1`, rare on homelab but possible if the user pasted a hardening config) blocks userspace RDRAND seed, forces CRNG-only path → slower seeding, surface for transparency. Read `/proc/sys/kernel/random/{entropy_avail,poolsize,urandom_min_reseed_secs,write_wakeup_threshold,read_wakeup_threshold,uuid,boot_id}`, walk `/sys/class/hwrng/` (zero-or-more entries, each with `name`, `quality` (kernel ≥ 5.11), `current`), read `/sys/devices/virtual/misc/hw_random/{rng_current,rng_available,rng_quality}` (the multiplexer side), enumerate `/sys/class/tpm/tpm*/` for TPM version (1.2 vs 2.0) + active PCR banks (sha256, sha384), check `/proc/sys/crypto/fips_enabled` for FIPS-mode override, cross-reference shipped `dmi_bios.py` (board may not have TPM header populated), shipped `kernel_taint.py` (taints affect RNG trust), shipped `kmod_params.py` (nvidia.ko + tpm_crb module-param state), classify into 4 verdicts: (a) hwrng active + `entropy_avail > 2000` + CRNG init done at boot in <2 s → healthy, surface as info, (b) no hwrng + `haveged` not installed + headless → boot-entropy starvation risk, paste-ready `apt install haveged` or `apt install rng-tools && systemctl enable rngd` recipe, (c) TPM present but `tpm-rng` not loaded → recommend `modprobe tpm-rng` + persistence, (d) VM guest with virtio_rng + host-side rng-tools absent → guest-entropy starvation, recommend host fix. Surface "your AM5 7950X has RDRAND/RDSEED active (CPU-RNG hwrng entry `intel-rng` or `amd-rng`, quality=1000), TPM 2.0 detected at /sys/class/tpm/tpm0 but `tpm-rng.ko` not loaded — registering TPM as a second entropy source would diversify your CRNG seed (recommended for boxes that LUKS-mount /data/models at boot + serve HTTPS to LAN clients); `modprobe tpm-rng && echo 'tpm-rng' | sudo tee /etc/modules-load.d/tpm-rng.conf` — paste-ready" or "your headless Proxmox guest has only `virtio_rng` hwrng (quality=1000) but `entropy_avail=900` consistently — your Proxmox host needs `apt install rng-tools && systemctl enable rngd` to drive the virtio_rng device, today the guest sees only what the host happens to leak through; paste-ready recipe (run on host)". Niche audience (security-aware + LUKS-using + headless homelabbers — ~15-25 % of audience), but extremely-high-payoff "explains my 90 s LUKS unlock + 10-minute first HF download" answer that today no OSS Linux tool surfaces with a single classified verdict + paste-ready fix. Pairs with shipped `dmi_bios.py` (TPM hardware presence), `kernel_taint.py` (RNG trust), `kmod_params.py` (module load state), parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (TPM PCR + IMA correlate). First : `modules/rng_entropy.py` + GET `/api/rng-entropy`. |
| 42.6 | **`/proc/sysvipc/{shm,sem,msg}` + `/proc/<pid>/fd/*` symlink scan + `/dev/shm/*` POSIX-shm inventory — SysV shared memory + POSIX shm leak/exhaustion auditor for llama.cpp `--mlock` arenas + vllm KV-cache shm pools + jupyter IPC fragments** | S | 3 | Modern inference engines lean heavily on shared-memory APIs that the user rarely sees: llama.cpp with `--mlock --no-mmap` allocates a giant SysV shm segment to hold dequantized tensors so two `llama-server` workers can share weights, vllm's tensor-parallel implementation uses POSIX shm at `/dev/shm/` for inter-rank gradient broadcast, jupyter's `ipykernel` uses SysV shm for the comm-pipe between kernel and notebook, koboldcpp's `--horde-worker` mode uses both — and *all* of these silently leak shm segments when the worker SIGKILLs (very common on a busy homelab where users `pkill -9 llama-server` instead of clean SIGTERM). Today no shipped module reads `/proc/sysvipc/shm` (the kernel's authoritative SysV shm inventory: id, perms, size, owner_uid, last_attach_pid, last_detach_pid, attach_count, ctime, atime, dtime). Shipped `proc_smaps.py` walks per-PID mappings (would catch live attached shm) but not orphaned (zero-attach) segments that survive PID death. The recurring foot-guns: (1) `/proc/sysvipc/shm` shows 8-12 entries totalling 40+ GiB after a week of uptime, attach_count=0 for most → leaked from crashed llama.cpp `--mlock` workers, `ipcrm -m <id>` recipe per orphan, (2) `/dev/shm/llama-*-arena-0` files totalling 24 GiB present after `pkill llama-server` (POSIX shm leaks don't auto-cleanup like SysV ones do on the last detach), recommend `find /dev/shm -name 'llama-*' -mtime +1 -delete` cron, (3) SHMMAX kernel parameter (legacy, default unlimited on kernel ≥ 3.16 but some Proxmox VE templates ship with `kernel.shmmax=64M`) → next llama.cpp `--mlock` for a 24 GB GGUF fails with ENOMEM despite plentiful RAM, the user blames "llama.cpp bug" while actually `sysctl kernel.shmmax` is the culprit, (4) SHMMNI (max segments, default 4096) approaching limit after a leaky busy session, (5) `kernel.shmall` (max total shm pages) cap that some hardening guides set artificially low (32 MiB) blocking inference workloads, (6) vllm with tensor-parallel=4 on a single GPU (rare misconfiguration but seen — user copy-pastes a multi-GPU recipe) leaves 4 ranks' worth of POSIX shm at `/dev/shm/vllm-rank-{0..3}` after exit, ~40 GiB orphaned, (7) jupyter kernel crashes (`Kernel died, restarting`) leak `/dev/shm/JupyterKernelMemory-*` entries that grow unbounded across the user's workday. Parse `/proc/sysvipc/{shm,sem,msg}` (tabular: header line + N rows), walk `/dev/shm/` for POSIX-shm files (each is an mmap'd backing file), per-SHM resolve the `last_attach_pid` / `last_detach_pid` against shipped service-discovery's known-PID list (alive vs dead) + against `/proc/<pid>/status` to confirm liveness, scan `/proc/<pid>/fd/*` symlinks for `/dev/shm/*` targets to identify which POSIX shm files are actively held, cross-reference `kernel.shm{max,mni,all}` from `/proc/sys/kernel/` against current utilization, shipped `proc_smaps.py` (per-PID mapping side), `service_discovery.py` (PID identity), `host_class.py` (skip on locked-down container guests where sysvipc namespaced), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) shm inventory tidy + attach_count consistent with live PIDs + `/dev/shm` cleanly empty when no inference running → healthy, congratulate, (b) orphan SysV shm segments (`attach_count=0` + `last_detach_pid` is a dead PID) → leak, paste-ready `ipcrm -m <id>` per-orphan recipe + warning "this frees X GiB RAM", (c) orphan POSIX shm in `/dev/shm/*` larger than 100 MiB + not opened by any current PID → leak, paste-ready `rm /dev/shm/<file>` recipe, (d) `kernel.shmmax` artificially low + current llama.cpp pattern would exceed → flag with paste-ready `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-shm.conf` raise, (e) shm-count approaching SHMMNI → warning. Surface "your `/proc/sysvipc/shm` shows 14 segments totalling 47.3 GiB: 4 live (vllm rank shm, all attach_count ≥ 1, owner PID 12834 alive), 8 orphan (llama.cpp --mlock arenas from PIDs 11234/11567/.../12001 — all dead per `/proc/<pid>/status`, last_detach 4-18 hours ago), 2 sem arrays from a crashed jupyter kernel. `/dev/shm` shows 6 files totalling 31.2 GiB: 2 active (vllm rank-0/1), 4 orphan (llama-arena-0/1/2/3, no PID has them open). Free 78.5 GiB RAM by running: `ipcrm -m 65538 65539 65540 65541 65542 65543 65544 65545 && rm /dev/shm/llama-arena-{0..3}` (paste-ready). Then prevent recurrence: add `kernel.shmmax=68719476736` + a `find /dev/shm -name 'llama-*' -mtime +1 -delete` cron — paste-ready snippet". Niche-but-extremely-common-once-you-look feature for the multi-engine homelab pattern (combined ollama / llama-server / vllm / jupyter cohabitation that 50-60 % of users run). Pairs with shipped `proc_smaps.py` (live mapping), `service_discovery.py` (PID identity), `proc_static_audit.py` (sysctl shm-limits), `host_class.py`. First : `modules/sysvipc_audit.py` + GET `/api/sysvipc-audit`. |
| 42.7 | **`/sys/kernel/security/{lockdown,apparmor,landlock,tomoyo,smack,selinux}/*` + `/proc/<pid>/attr/{current,prev,exec}` LSM (Linux Security Module) stack + lockdown-level + per-process security-domain auditor for "why does my dashboard get EPERM reading /sys/kernel/debug/zswap"** | M | 3 | The Linux Security Module (LSM) stack — AppArmor (Ubuntu/SUSE default), SELinux (Fedora/RHEL default), Landlock (kernel ≥ 5.13 unprivileged sandboxing API), Tomoyo (rare), Smack (rare), plus the `lockdown` LSM (kernel ≥ 5.4 that gates `/dev/mem`, MSR access, kexec, certain `/sys/kernel/debug/*` reads) — collectively decide which paths a process *can* read. The dashboard already encounters silent EPERM cases (shipped `cgroup_memcap.py` notes "skipping `/sys/fs/cgroup/memory.events.local` on AppArmor confined run", shipped `airgap.py` notes lockdown-blocks-network-debug paths) but no shipped module *audits the LSM stack itself* — which LSMs are active, in what mode (enforce / complain / permissive), what lockdown level is set (none / integrity / confidentiality), and which inference workers run under which security domain. The recurring foot-guns: (1) Ubuntu 24.04 default ships with AppArmor in *enforce* mode + an `ollama.service` AppArmor profile that blocks reads to `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpufreq/policy*/scaling_governor` → shipped #40.7 cpufreq_domains advice "echo performance >..." silently EPERM-fails when run from within ollama's `ExecStartPost=`, the user has no visibility, (2) `lockdown=integrity` (Ubuntu HWE default when Secure Boot is on per shipped parked #41.5 efi_secureboot) blocks MSR access → shipped #42.4 cpu_epb advice "write to /sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/power/energy_perf_bias" silently EPERM-fails because MSR-write is lockdown-blocked, surface "your kernel is in lockdown=integrity per `/sys/kernel/security/lockdown` (Secure Boot active per parked #41.5), MSR writes blocked, paste-ready cpu_epb recipe will fail with EPERM — either disable Secure Boot or use BIOS-level EPB setting", (3) SELinux in *enforcing* mode on Fedora with the dashboard's `gpu-dashboard.service` running under `unconfined_t` (typical) but llama-server under a `container_t` (because user ran `podman run ollama/ollama`) → dashboard can't read `/proc/<llama-server-pid>/maps` for shipped `proc_smaps.py` due to SELinux MLS step, surface clearly, (4) Landlock (kernel ≥ 5.13) self-imposed sandboxing — a Python script using `landlock-rs`-equivalent stdlib hooks to drop file-tree access — leaves traces in `/proc/<pid>/attr/current`, useful for the security-aware user wanting to verify their sandbox is active, (5) Tomoyo / Smack on niche distros (Tomoyo on TOMOYO-Live, Smack on Tizen-derived) → just surface presence for fairness, no advice, (6) `/sys/kernel/security/apparmor/profiles` shows enforced profiles — count + name top 10 by PID for visibility, (7) `kernel.unprivileged_bpf_disabled=1` (Ubuntu 22.04+ default for hardening) → blocks `bpftrace`/eBPF perf tools the user might want to debug shipped #42.1 file-lock contention with, surface as transparency, (8) `kernel.kptr_restrict=2` (Fedora hardening default) → blocks `/proc/kallsyms` symbol reads, breaks kernel-symbol resolution for shipped `nvrm_tail.py` stack traces, surface as transparency. Walk `/sys/kernel/security/lockdown` (read line containing `[none]` `integrity` `confidentiality` with `[X]` indicating active), `/sys/kernel/security/apparmor/{profiles,enabled}` (text + count), `/sys/kernel/security/landlock/abi_version` (presence), `/sys/fs/selinux/{enforce,policyvers,mls}` (SELinux active level), enumerate active LSMs via `/sys/kernel/security/lsm` (kernel ≥ 5.1 comma-separated list), per-PID for shipped service-discovery inference workers read `/proc/<pid>/attr/{current,prev,exec}` (returns "unconfined" or the AppArmor profile name or SELinux context like `system_u:system_r:container_t:s0`), read `/proc/sys/kernel/{unprivileged_bpf_disabled,kptr_restrict,dmesg_restrict,perf_event_paranoid,yama/ptrace_scope}` for hardening-knob context, cross-reference shipped `kernel_taint.py`, parked #41.5 efi_secureboot, parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit, shipped `host_class.py` (skip on container guest where LSM may be the *container's* not the host's), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) standard Ubuntu HWE (AppArmor enforce + lockdown=integrity from Secure Boot) → expected, surface the impact on shipped EPB/MSR/debug advice + paste-ready "disable Secure Boot if you need MSR writes" caveat, (b) Fedora SELinux enforcing + dashboard unconfined_t + inference confined_t → cross-domain access blocked, surface, (c) all LSMs disabled (`/sys/kernel/security/` empty or `selinux=0 apparmor=0` kernel-cmdline overrides) → flag as "no MAC layer, every shipped recipe will work but your inference rig has no isolation from a compromised model file", (d) Landlock-using inference worker (rare but seen on advanced setups) → surface as info, (e) lockdown=confidentiality (rare, requires explicit cmdline) → most aggressive, many shipped paths blocked, surface complete impact matrix. Surface "your Ubuntu 24.04 has AppArmor enforce (148 profiles loaded, ollama / llama-server / vllm under `unconfined`; only system daemons confined), lockdown=integrity (per parked #41.5 Secure Boot active), `kernel.unprivileged_bpf_disabled=1`, `kernel.kptr_restrict=2`, `kernel.dmesg_restrict=1`, `kernel.yama/ptrace_scope=1` → standard hardened-Ubuntu posture; shipped #42.4 cpu_epb writes WILL EPERM (lockdown blocks MSR), shipped `nvrm_tail.py` kernel-symbol resolution will silently degrade (kptr_restrict=2 hides symbols), shipped #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity writes to `/proc/irq/<n>/smp_affinity` work fine (CAP_SYS_NICE / root). Impact summary table + paste-ready 'disable Secure Boot to unlock MSR writes' caveat below. If your shipped recipes silently fail, this audit is the first place to look". Effort is M because LSM-stack walking + per-PID attr reading + cross-correlating against ~20 shipped modules' EPERM-risk paths requires care, but stdlib-only (file reads + simple parsing). Pairs with shipped `kernel_taint.py`, parked #41.5 efi_secureboot (Secure Boot drives lockdown), parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (IMA correlates with LSM), shipped `host_class.py` (container vs host). First : `modules/lsm_lockdown.py` + GET `/api/lsm-lockdown`. |
| 42.8 | **`/sys/bus/event_source/devices/*/{type,format,events,cpus,cpumask}` + `/proc/sys/kernel/{perf_event_paranoid,perf_event_max_sample_rate,perf_cpu_time_max_percent,perf_event_max_stack,perf_event_mlock_kb}` PMU (Performance Monitoring Unit) + perf-subsystem event-source inventory + LBR/PEBS availability auditor for `perf record` / `perf stat` inference profiling** | M | 3 | Power-user homelabbers profiling their inference workloads with `perf record -e cpu/event=0xc0/ -e cache-misses` (to find LLC-miss hotspots in llama.cpp's matmul) or `perf stat -e branch-misses,LLC-load-misses,dTLB-load-misses ./llama-server ...` (to compare quant performance across builds) need to know *which* PMU events the kernel exposes for *this specific* CPU microarch — Skylake-X exposes a different set of LBR (Last Branch Record) + PEBS (Precise Event-Based Sampling) events than Zen5, Lunar Lake's compute tile has different PMU coverage than its SoC tile (LP-E cores), and many cloud-VM / Proxmox-guest scenarios show *no* hardware PMU (kvm exposes only software events). The complete event-source inventory lives at `/sys/bus/event_source/devices/*/` — one directory per PMU domain (`cpu`, `cpu_core` (P-cluster on hybrid), `cpu_atom` (E-cluster on hybrid), `intel_pt` (Intel Processor Trace), `intel_bts`, `amd_iommu_0`, `power`, `uncore_imc_*` (memory controller IMC PMU per channel), `uncore_cha_*` (Cache/Home Agent), `msr`, `ibs_op` / `ibs_fetch` on AMD (Instruction-Based Sampling), `kprobe`/`uprobe`/`tracepoint`) — with sub-files: `type` (numeric PMU type), `format` (event-encoding spec), `events/<name>` (named-event encodings, e.g. `instructions` → `event=0xc0`), `cpus` / `cpumask` (which CPUs this PMU covers — load-bearing on hybrid where `cpu_core` only covers P-cores and `cpu_atom` only E-cores). No shipped module reads any of this. The recurring foot-guns: (1) `perf_event_paranoid=2` (Ubuntu default) blocks user-mode `perf record` without `CAP_PERFMON` → user fights "permission denied" trying to profile their own llama-server, surface "your `kernel.perf_event_paranoid=2` blocks all non-root perf use; set to 1 (allow user CPU-PMU, block kernel) for profiling user-space inference: `sudo sysctl kernel.perf_event_paranoid=1` + persist via `/etc/sysctl.d/`", (2) `perf_event_paranoid=4` (some hardening guides) → no perf at all, surface, (3) hybrid CPU (Alder Lake etc.) + user runs `perf record -a` without specifying PMU domain → samples *only* `cpu` (default) which on hybrid is actually `cpu_core` (P-cluster), missing E-cluster events entirely; surface "your hybrid CPU has `cpu_core` (P-cluster cpus 0-15) and `cpu_atom` (E-cluster cpus 16-23) as separate PMU domains — `perf record -e cycles -a` only samples one; use `perf record -e cpu_core/cycles/ -e cpu_atom/cycles/ -a` for full coverage" + paste-ready snippet, (4) `intel_pt` PMU absent → Intel Processor Trace not available (older Skylake-S, all AMD, virt), surface for transparency since some advanced profiling guides assume it, (5) `intel_pt` PMU present + `kernel.perf_event_paranoid > 1` → PT is *available* but can't be used due to paranoia level, surface the unlock recipe, (6) uncore_imc PMUs absent (`/sys/bus/event_source/devices/uncore_imc_*/` empty) → can't measure DRAM bandwidth via perf, recommend `pcm-memory` (Intel PCM tool) as fallback or surface the limitation, (7) `perf_event_max_sample_rate=100000` (default 100 KHz) + user's profiling script asks for 1 MHz → silent throttle, surface bump recipe, (8) `perf_event_mlock_kb=516` (kernel default, conservative) → user's `perf record -m 8M` fails with "permission denied" because mlock budget too small, bump to `perf_event_mlock_kb=8192`, (9) AMD IBS PMU (`ibs_op`/`ibs_fetch`) absent on AMD Zen5 + new kernel → microcode-dependent, surface fairness note, (10) kvm guest with no host-side `--enable-pmu` → entire `/sys/bus/event_source/devices/cpu` may be empty, surface "your kvm guest has no hardware PMU exposed; host must launch qemu with `-cpu host,+pmu` for perf to work", paste-ready Proxmox VE `args:` snippet. Walk `/sys/bus/event_source/devices/*/{type,cpus,cpumask,nr_addr_filters}` + `events/` subdirectory (each file = one named event), enumerate available PMU types + named events per type, read `/proc/sys/kernel/{perf_event_paranoid,perf_event_max_sample_rate,perf_cpu_time_max_percent,perf_event_max_stack,perf_event_mlock_kb}`, cross-reference shipped `cpu_topology.py` + #42.2 hybrid_cpu_topo (P-vs-E cluster maps to `cpu_core`/`cpu_atom`), shipped `cpu_microcode.py` (microarch determines PMU revision), shipped `host_class.py` (kvm guest detection), shipped `kernel_taint.py` (taints affect perf trust), parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (lockdown vs perf), classify into 5 verdicts: (a) full PMU coverage + `perf_event_paranoid=1` + hybrid PMUs split correctly → optimal profiling-ready, congratulate + surface the per-PMU event count, (b) `perf_event_paranoid >= 2` blocking user-mode perf → paste-ready unlock recipe, (c) hybrid CPU + user-facing recipe needs both PMUs → paste-ready `-e cpu_core/cycles/ -e cpu_atom/cycles/` template, (d) kvm guest with empty PMU → host-side fix recipe (Proxmox `args:` snippet), (e) `intel_pt` available but locked behind paranoia → surface unlock for power-user. Surface "your 14900K has 8 PMU domains: cpu_core (cpus 0-15, 47 named events including LBR + PEBS), cpu_atom (cpus 16-23, 38 named events, no LBR on E-cluster), intel_pt (Intel Processor Trace, available), msr, uncore_imc_0/1 (DRAM channel A/B PMU), uncore_cha_0/1 (LLC slice PMU), ibs_op (absent — Intel, not AMD), kprobe/uprobe/tracepoint (always). `kernel.perf_event_paranoid=2` (Ubuntu default) blocks your non-root profiling; sudo sysctl kernel.perf_event_paranoid=1 then `perf record -e cpu_core/cycles/ -e cpu_atom/cycles/ -F 999 -a -g -- ./llama-server ...` for full-cluster profiling — paste-ready ready-to-go". Effort is M because event-source walking + per-PMU events directory enumeration + cross-referencing PMU `cpumask` against hybrid topology requires care, but stdlib + struct (numeric event format encoding) suffice. Very niche audience (the slice of power-user homelabbers who actually run `perf record` on their inference workloads — ~3-5 % of users), but the cleanest "your PMU and perf-paranoia knob and hybrid-PMU-split + uncore IMC bandwidth-counter availability are all mapped" feature in the entire stack for that slice. Pairs with shipped `cpu_topology.py`, #42.2 hybrid_cpu_topo, `cpu_microcode.py`, `host_class.py`, `kernel_taint.py`. First : `modules/pmu_perf_audit.py` + GET `/api/pmu-perf-audit`. |

**Top 4 (fit × urgency)** :
1. 42.1 `/proc/locks` file-lock contention auditor — S, fit 5, the single most invisible foot-gun on a busy multi-engine homelab inference rig: ollama daemon holds an `flock(LOCK_EX)` on `~/.ollama/models/blobs/sha256-<digest>` from model-load until daemon exit, then the user fires up llama-server pointing at the same GGUF (a common pattern — ollama for chat, llama-server for benchmark / scripted RAG), and the second tool stalls 3-30 s silently on first prefill with no log entry on either side. Parses `/proc/locks` (one line per lock: type ∈ POSIX/FLOCK/OFDLCK/LEASE, kind ∈ ADVISORY/MANDATORY, mode, pid, maj:min:inode), joins via `(maj:min:inode)` against shipped service-discovery PIDs' `/proc/<pid>/maps` + `/proc/<pid>/fd/*`, per-PID walks `/proc/<pid>/fdinfo/<fd>` for OFD-lock annotations, cross-references against `/proc/mounts` for NFS/Btrfs/overlayfs source-fs detection (different lock semantics). Classifies into 5 verdicts (no-conflicts / two-engines-on-same-GGUF / leaked-lock-from-dead-PID / NFS-lockd-recovery / mandatory-lock-from-`mount -o mand`), each with a paste-ready release recipe. Catches the four-engine cohabitation pattern (ollama + llama-server + vllm + koboldcpp) that dominates power-user setups and that today no OSS tool surfaces. Pairs with shipped `service_discovery.py`, `fs_mount_audit.py`, `proc_static_audit.py`, `proc_deep_state.py`. First : `modules/file_locks_audit.py`.
2. 42.2 heterogeneous-CPU `cluster_id` / `die_id` topology auditor — S, fit 5, the missing piece that shipped `cpu_topology.py` (classic trio: physical_package_id + core_id + thread_siblings_list) leaves out: Alder/Raptor/Meteor/Arrow Lake split cores into P-clusters + E-clusters with their own L2-shared boundary, Zen4/Zen5 multi-CCD parts (7950X / 9950X) place CCDs on separate `die_id`s, Snapdragon X Elite has Oryon-P + Oryon-M, Lunar Lake adds a *third* LP-E cluster on the SoC tile that's catastrophically slow for inference if pinned to by accident. Walks `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/topology/{cluster_id,cluster_cpus,die_id,die_cpus,package_cpus,ppin}` + `/sys/devices/system/cpu/types/{intel_atom,intel_core}/cpus` (kernel ≥ 6.5), cross-references shipped `cpu_topology.py` + `cpu_cache_topology.py` (`cluster_cpus` typically == L2-shared set) + `gpu_cpu_affinity.py` (PCIe-local cluster pick) + `numa_placement.py`, classifies into 5 topology classes (monolithic / multi-CCD Zen / Intel hybrid P+E / Lunar/Panther P+E+LP-E / ARM-Snapdragon-Apple), each with a paste-ready "pin to perf cluster only" taskset recipe + systemd Drop-In. Single highest-value answer to the "I have a 14900K / 7950X / Snapdragon X and shipped advice gave me a flat list of cores but I don't know which subset is the perf cluster" complaint that today no OSS Linux tool surfaces with a single classified verdict. First : `modules/hybrid_cpu_topo.py`.
3. 42.3 `/sys/class/thermal/cooling_device*` cooling-device inventory + thermal-zone-to-fan binding graph — XS, fit 5, the missing actuator-side of the thermal subsystem that shipped `thermal_zones.py` (sensor + trip-point side) leaves uncovered. Walks `/sys/class/thermal/cooling_device*/{type,cur_state,max_state}` + the binding annotations at `/sys/class/thermal/thermal_zone*/cdev<N>` + `cdev<N>_trip_point` + `cdev<N>_weight` to build the complete bipartite graph "zone → trip point → weight → cdev", classifies into 5 verdicts (healthy-binding / `Processor` or `intel_powerclamp` actively pulsing → passive throttle engaged / `Fan` cur_state stuck low while bound zone is hot / asymmetric weight rebalance needed / orphan-cdev opportunity). Catches the previously-invisible "your CPU is being held at cur_state=3/10 by ACPI _PSV passive cooling — your intel_pstate + HWP_EPP look optimal but the *firmware* is throttling underneath" diagnostic that explains a slice of inexplicable t/s loss on consumer Asus / MSI boards with conservative-default trip points. Tiniest scope (~50 file reads, 5 branches), biggest "I finally understand what fan the kernel is actually controlling" payoff for the cooling-curious audience that already read shipped `thermal_zones.py` + `fan_curve.py`. Pairs with shipped `thermal_zones.py`, `fan_curve.py`, `throttle_cause.py`, `cpu_boost.py`, `thermal_slowdown_kind.py`. First : `modules/cooling_devices.py`.
4. 42.4 `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/power/energy_perf_bias` legacy EPB knob auditor — XS, fit 4, the *third* energy-vs-performance axis shipped `hwp_epp.py` (HWP-EPP string knob) and `pstate_audit.py` (governor knob) don't cover: the legacy EPB MSR-driven knob at `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/power/energy_perf_bias` (0-15 integer, the original Nehalem-era `x86_energy_perf_policy` knob) that *still wins on Haswell-EP / Skylake-X / Broadwell-EP* (silently throttling 5-15 % even when HWP-EPP and governor both say `performance`) and that mirrors to AMD's EEMR (Energy-Efficient Mode Register, Zen3+ via `amd_pstate_epp` driver kernel ≥ 6.3). Reads per-CPU EPB (single integer or ENOENT on unsupported microarchs), cross-references shipped `hwp_epp.py` + `pstate_audit.py` + `cpu_microcode.py` (microarch determines which knob wins) + `host_class.py` (laptop OEM-default-conservative trap detection) + parked #41.8 keyring_ima_audit (lockdown blocks MSR write → EPB can't be tuned), classifies into 5 verdicts (unanimous-agreement / contradiction-on-EPB-wins-microarch / EPB=0-on-24/7-idle-box / per-CPU-inconsistent / MSR-blocked-by-lockdown). Surfaces "your 14900K cpu0-21 EPB=0, cpu22-23 EPB=6 — partial cpupower run left inconsistency, uniform-set with the snippet below + persist via systemd Drop-In so it survives reboot + suspend/resume". Tiniest scope of the cycle (~24-48 file reads + 5 branches), biggest "there was a *third* energy-perf knob silently overriding my carefully-tuned HWP-EPP" eye-opener for the audience that already read shipped `hwp_epp.py` + `pstate_audit.py` + `cpu_boost.py`. Pairs with shipped `hwp_epp.py`, `pstate_audit.py`, `cpu_boost.py`, `cpu_microcode.py`, `host_class.py`. First : `modules/cpu_epb.py`.

Bench (42.5 RNG/entropy + hwrng + TPM-RNG health auditor / 42.6 SysV-shm + POSIX-shm `/dev/shm` leak auditor / 42.7 LSM stack + lockdown-level + per-PID-attr auditor / 42.8 PMU event-source + perf-paranoia + hybrid-PMU-split auditor) for cycles after top 4 lands.

## R&D #41 survey (started 2026-05-23)

8 fresh angles, none in shipped list (#6-40 — including just-shipped `ksm_advisor` / `vm_tuning_deep` / `gpu_pci_bind` / `nic_queue_affinity` from cycle #40), modules tree (144 files), or parked bench (27.2/27.5/27.6/27.8 + 28.2/28.3/28.6/28.8 + 29.2/29.4/29.5/29.6 + 30.4/30.6/30.7/30.8 + 31.5/31.6/31.7/31.8 + 32.2/32.6/32.7/32.8 + 33.3/33.5/33.7/33.8 + 34.5/34.6/34.7/34.8 + 35.5/35.6/35.7/35.8 + 36.5/36.6/36.7/36.8 + 37.5/37.6/37.7/37.8 + 38.5/38.6/38.7/38.8 + 39.5/39.6/39.7/39.8 + 40.5/40.6/40.7/40.8).
