| # | Feature | Effort | Fit | Why it matters / first module |
|---|---------|--------|-----|-------|
| 40.1 | **`/sys/bus/pci/devices/<gpu>/{enable,driver,driver_override,remove,rescan}` + `/sys/bus/pci/drivers/{nvidia,nouveau,vfio-pci}/{bind,unbind,new_id,remove_id}` GPU PCIe driver-binding inventory + paste-ready vfio-passthrough / driver-rebind orchestrator** | S | 5 | When a homelab user wants to hot-swap a GPU between the host (for llama-server inference) and a Proxmox / qemu VM (for a passthrough Windows gaming guest or a sandboxed CUDA fuzzing VM), or wants to recover a stuck GPU after a CUDA error 999 without rebooting, the *correct* answer is to write a few specific paths under `/sys/bus/pci/`: `echo 0000:01:00.0 > /sys/bus/pci/drivers/nvidia/unbind` to release nvidia.ko's grip, `echo 10de 2204 > /sys/bus/pci/drivers/vfio-pci/new_id` to bind vfio-pci for passthrough, `echo 0000:01:00.0 > /sys/bus/pci/devices/0000:01:00.0/driver/unbind && echo 1 > /sys/bus/pci/devices/0000:01:00.0/remove && echo 1 > /sys/bus/pci/rescan` to fully re-probe a wedged GPU. Today shipped `vfio_sentinel.py` watches *whether* vfio-pci is bound to a GPU it shouldn't be, and shipped `gpu_reset.py` handles the nvidia-side reset paths — but neither *enumerates* the full driver-binding state per GPU (which driver is currently bound? what does `driver_override` say for the next boot? is `enable` 1 (PCI bus master enabled) or 0 (orphaned)?) nor surfaces the *paste-ready transition recipes* (host→VM, VM→host, stuck→reset) with the right sequence of writes. Walk `/sys/bus/pci/devices/` for VID 0x10de + 0x1b4b (audio function alongside GPUs) + 0x10de USB-C function (Turing+ has these), read per-device `enable` (1=BME on, 0=orphaned), `driver` (symlink → `/sys/bus/pci/drivers/nvidia` or `nouveau` or `vfio-pci` or empty), `driver_override` (persisted across re-probes), `power/control` (auto/on, the `d3cold_policy` overlap), enumerate `/sys/bus/pci/drivers/{nvidia,nouveau,vfio-pci}/` for which IDs each driver claims (`new_id` / `remove_id` files surface the PCI ID allowlist), classify into 6 binding states: (a) nvidia bound + enable=1 + persistence_mode on → optimal for inference, (b) nouveau bound → silent fallback, suggest `blacklist nouveau` + initramfs rebuild, (c) vfio-pci bound + no VM running → orphaned passthrough config, GPU sitting idle, (d) driver=empty + enable=0 → orphaned GPU (drive was unbind'd manually, recover with bind), (e) driver_override=vfio-pci pinned across reboots → user has set up passthrough, surface VM-handoff recipes, (f) Turing+ GPU but only the 3D-function bound (audio + USB-C functions still nvidia-owned in stale state) → multi-function partial-bind landmine. Emit paste-ready *forward* recipes (host→VM: full 3-function unbind+vfio-pci bind script), *reverse* recipes (VM→host: vfio-pci unbind + nvidia bind + persistence_mode on + nvidia-smi -pm 1), and *recovery* recipes (stuck GPU: PCI remove + rescan, with a clear "this WILL drop all running CUDA contexts, save your work" warning). Pairs with shipped `vfio_sentinel.py`, `gpu_reset.py`, `d3cold_policy.py`, `persistence_mode.py`, `iommu_groups.py` for the complete "what does this box do with its GPU's PCIe binding" picture. Single highest-leverage feature for the dual-use homelab user (gaming-VM-by-night, inference-rig-by-day pattern that drives a top-3 GitHub-issue category in every vfio / Proxmox / single-GPU-passthrough tracker) that today nothing in the OSS Linux ecosystem surfaces cleanly with one-click recipes. First : `modules/gpu_pci_bind.py` + GET `/api/gpu-pci-bind`. |
| 40.2 | **`/sys/kernel/mm/ksm/{run,pages_shared,pages_sharing,pages_to_scan,sleep_millisecs,merge_across_nodes}` + per-PID `/proc/<pid>/ksm_*` Kernel Same-page Merging detector + impact-on-LLM advisor (does NOT help LLM, but actively HURTS it — turn off)** | XS | 5 | KSM (Kernel Same-page Merging) is a Linux feature that scans anonymous memory for identical pages and dedups them — wonderful for KVM hosting 12 identical Windows VMs sharing the same Windows 10 install pages, *catastrophic* for LLM inference workloads. Why catastrophic: (1) GGUF / safetensors weight tensors have effectively zero inter-page duplication (random-looking quantized blobs), so the `khugepaged`-like `ksmd` kernel thread scans them futilely at CPU cost while never finding merges, (2) the scan triggers TLB flushes that invalidate the inference worker's hot working set, (3) when KSM *does* merge a page (rare, but happens with all-zero padding in token-buffer arenas), the next write triggers a copy-on-write fault that stalls the inference worker, (4) `merge_across_nodes=1` (default) causes KSM to merge pages across NUMA nodes, defeating shipped #35.3 numa_placement. Yet KSM is enabled by default on Fedora 37+ (KSM `Auto` mode via `ksmtuned.service`), on openSUSE / Tumbleweed, on Proxmox VE hosts (always on for VM density), and increasingly on Ubuntu Server LTS (since 23.04 via `systemd-ksm.service` for memory-constrained server use). The user has no idea — the symptom is "kernel CPU time inexplicably 3-8 % on `ksmd` even when no VMs are running, llama-server t/s is 4-6 % below benchmark and varies with `ksmd`'s scan duty cycle". Today nothing in the shipped stack surfaces KSM. Read `/sys/kernel/mm/ksm/run` (0=off, 1=on, 2=unmerge-all-and-stop), `pages_shared` (number of dedup-anchor pages), `pages_sharing` (number of pages mapped to those anchors), `pages_to_scan` (per-cycle scan budget), `sleep_millisecs` (between cycles), `merge_across_nodes` (NUMA-crossing toggle), `general_profit` (kernel ≥ 6.1, signed bytes saved minus scan cost), enumerate shipped service-discovery inference PIDs and read per-PID `/proc/<pid>/ksm_stat` (kernel ≥ 6.1: `ksm_rmap_items`, `ksm_zero_pages`, `ksm_merging_pages`) + `/proc/<pid>/ksm_merging_pages` legacy, compute "is `ksmd` actively touching the inference worker's pages right now", classify into 4 verdicts: (a) `run=0` → not running, OK, (b) `run=1` but `pages_sharing=0` on this host (no VMs, no duplication candidates) → pure cost, no benefit, recommend disable, (c) `run=1` + inference worker has nonzero `ksm_merging_pages` → KSM is futilely scanning your tensors, definite slowdown, (d) `run=1` + Proxmox detected (shipped host_class form-factor=KVM-host) → KSM is justified for guest density, leave on but maybe set `merge_across_nodes=0` for inference guests. Surface "KSM enabled by `ksmtuned.service` (Fedora default) but `pages_sharing=0` after 4 h uptime → ksmd is burning 3-6 % CPU scanning your llama-server's tensors with zero benefit, disable: `systemctl disable --now ksmtuned ksm` (Fedora) or `echo 2 > /sys/kernel/mm/ksm/run && systemctl mask ksm.service` (Ubuntu/Debian) — paste-ready snippet". Niche audience (Fedora / openSUSE / Tumbleweed homelab users + anyone running Proxmox VE bare-metal for LLM workloads — combined ~20-25 % of audience), but a clean answer to a previously-invisible 4-6 % t/s tax with a one-line systemd fix. Cross-references shipped #31.1 thp_audit (also a memory-scanning kernel thread), shipped #35.3 numa_placement (NUMA boundary), shipped host_class (just shipped, classifies Proxmox/KVM-host context). First : `modules/ksm_advisor.py` + GET `/api/ksm-advisor`. |
| 40.3 | **`/proc/sys/vm/{drop_caches,page-cluster,watermark_scale_factor,vfs_cache_pressure,min_free_kbytes,zone_reclaim_mode}` deeper VM tuning auditor — page-cache eviction + reclaim aggressiveness for 32-GB-host + 70B-model rigs** (extends shipped `vm_sysctl_audit`) | S | 5 | Shipped `vm_sysctl_audit.py` audits the 4-5 most commonly-touched `/proc/sys/vm/*` knobs (`swappiness`, `dirty_ratio`, `dirty_background_ratio`, `overcommit_memory`, `overcommit_ratio`) — but stops short of the *less-common-but-higher-impact-for-LLM* knobs that dominate the 32-GB-host-running-70B-quant pattern: (1) `vm.page-cluster` (default 3 = 8-page swap-readahead bursts; on NVMe swap with 70B-quant model paging this is *wrong*, optimal is 0 — single-page reads, no readahead wasted on random tensor blobs), (2) `vm.watermark_scale_factor` (default 10 = wake `kswapd` when free memory drops below 0.1 % of zone; on a 32-GB box loading a 24-GB quant + 4-GB context + 2-GB OS, `kswapd` wakes too late and direct-reclaim stalls inference for 50-200 ms hiccups — bump to 100-500 for early-wake), (3) `vm.vfs_cache_pressure` (default 100 = balanced inode/dentry vs page cache; for a model-loading workload that mmaps gigabytes of GGUF and never touches the filesystem otherwise, drop to 50 to keep file metadata in cache longer and avoid the "first prompt after idle is slow because dentry cache evicted the GGUF inode" microstall), (4) `vm.min_free_kbytes` (the watermark floor below which allocations fail; default scales with RAM but on a memory-tight 32-GB box loading a barely-fitting quant, the default sometimes triggers OOM-kill on a transient spike, bump to ~256-512 MB for safety), (5) `vm.zone_reclaim_mode` (default 0 on most boxes, but BIOS-default-1 on some Dell PowerEdge and HPE ProLiant servers reactivates aggressive same-node reclaim that conflicts with shipped #35.3 numa_placement's cross-node intent), (6) `vm.drop_caches` (write-only, but the dashboard should surface that it exists as a manual eviction tool with a "click to drop pagecache for benchmark repeatability" button, paired with the *warning* that it stalls every disk operation for 5-30 s on a busy box). Read all six knobs, cross-reference shipped `vm_sysctl_audit.py` (which already handles swappiness + dirty ratios), shipped #35.3 numa_placement, shipped `host_class` (RAM-tight detection), classify into 4 verdicts: (a) defaults across the board on a 32-GB-host + ≥24 GB quant detected → flag all 4 tuning candidates with paste-ready `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-vm-tuning.conf` snippet, (b) `page-cluster=3` + NVMe swap with active `/proc/swaps` entries → flag the readahead-waste, (c) `vm.zone_reclaim_mode=1` + multi-NUMA box → flag the conflict with shipped numa_placement, (d) `watermark_scale_factor=10` + RAM utilization > 80 % → bump recommendation. Surface "your 32-GB box has a 24-GB GGUF loaded + page-cluster=3 (default) + NVMe swap with `vm.swappiness=10` → every swap-in pulls 8 pages of random tensor blob (useless readahead), drop to `vm.page-cluster=0`; also bump `watermark_scale_factor` from 10 to 200 to wake kswapd at 2 % free instead of 0.1 % — prevents direct-reclaim stalls during context-window growth". Paste-ready snippet writes to `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-vm-tuning.conf` so it survives reboot + interacts cleanly with just-shipped sysctl_d_audit. Niche but high-leverage memory-pressure tuning for the audience that pushes RAM hardest. First : `modules/vm_tuning_deep.py` + GET `/api/vm-tuning-deep`. |
| 40.4 | **`/sys/class/net/<dev>/queues/{rx-N,tx-N}/{rps_cpus,xps_cpus,rps_flow_cnt,byte_queue_limits}` + `/sys/class/net/<dev>/{tx_queue_len,mtu,gro_flush_timeout,napi_defer_hard_irqs}` per-NIC RX/TX queue + RPS/XPS affinity auditor for LAN-served inference (Home Assistant + OpenWebUI + Discord bots fan-out)** | S | 5 | When a homelab inference rig serves 5-50 LAN clients (Home Assistant integration polling ollama every 30 s, OpenWebUI streaming SSE to 3 browsers, 2 Discord bots running RAG queries, a Mac on the same VLAN running Continue.dev for code-completion at high QPS), every TCP/HTTP packet that arrives on `eth0` / `enp4s0` triggers an interrupt + softirq + socket-layer demux on whichever CPU the NIC's RX queue is mapped to. On a busy multi-socket inference rig, three independent placement layers must align for low jitter: (1) the NIC's MSI-X IRQs must land on a CPU that's *not* a pinned-llama-server worker (avoid IRQ-vs-inference contention; defeats shipped #37.2's pinning if NIC IRQ lands on cores 8-15 while llama-server is `taskset -c 8-15`), (2) RPS (Receive Packet Steering, software RX queue spreading) at `/sys/class/net/<dev>/queues/rx-N/rps_cpus` decides which CPUs do the *socket-layer* processing after the NIC IRQ — Ubuntu default is empty mask (RPS disabled, all RX processing on the IRQ CPU), which silently single-threads SSE-streaming throughput at the IRQ-CPU's capacity, (3) XPS (Transmit Packet Steering) at `/sys/class/net/<dev>/queues/tx-N/xps_cpus` decides which CPU writes the TX packet — when llama-server worker on CPU 12 writes a 200-byte SSE chunk to a socket, XPS decides which TX queue (and thus which NIC IRQ on reply ACK) the packet lands on. Today shipped `nic_health.py` reads NIC link state + speed + driver, but doesn't surface RPS/XPS/queue topology at all. Walk `/sys/class/net/*/queues/{rx-N,tx-N}/` for every non-loopback NIC, read per-queue `rps_cpus` (hex bitmask), `xps_cpus`, `rps_flow_cnt` (flow-tracking depth), `byte_queue_limits/limit` (TX byte cap for bufferbloat avoidance), per-NIC `tx_queue_len` (default 1000 — too low for 1 Gbps + 50 streams), `mtu` (1500 default, 9000 jumbo if your LAN switch supports it for higher t/s on local-network model downloads from a NAS), `gro_flush_timeout` (Generic Receive Offload coalesce window, default 0 = aggressive flush, costs CPU; 50000 ns = small batching for inference-server's tiny-packet SSE pattern), `napi_defer_hard_irqs` (kernel ≥ 5.16, defers IRQ to NAPI-poll for IRQ-coalescing — defaults to 0, but 1-2 reduces IRQ rate on a busy LAN-served box by 30-50 %), cross-reference shipped #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity (the GPU's IRQ placement), shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity (PCIe-local CPU list), shipped `nic_health.py`, classify into 5 verdicts: (a) RPS empty + single RX queue on a multi-core box + multi-stream serving → RPS disabled, flag with `echo <hex-of-non-inference-cores> > /sys/class/net/eth0/queues/rx-0/rps_cpus` snippet, (b) NIC IRQs + inference-pinned cores overlap → cross-contention, suggest IRQ moveset, (c) multi-queue NIC (typical of Intel I225-V / Realtek RTL8125 2.5G / Aquantia 10G) with all queues on one CPU → unbalanced, suggest spread, (d) `tx_queue_len=1000` + 2.5G/10G NIC + multi-stream → bump to 10000, (e) `gro_flush_timeout=0` on a SSE-streaming box → small batching win available. Surface "your 2.5G Realtek RTL8125 has 8 RX queues but RPS is empty on all of them, all 8 IRQs land on CPU 0 (NUMA-local to your GPU per shipped #37.2's `local_cpulist=0-7`) — *and* your llama-server is `taskset -c 0-7` per shipped #31.3's pin advice, so every LAN packet steals time from inference workers; either move RPS to cores 8-15 (non-inference) with the snippet below, or unpin llama-server from CPU 0 specifically". Paste-ready systemd Drop-In oneshot that applies on boot + integrates with just-shipped host_class (skip on container guests where the host owns the NIC). Pairs with shipped `nic_health.py` (link status), shipped #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity (parallel IRQ-side story for GPU), shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity (PCIe-local cores) for the complete CPU-affinity-for-everything-that-fires-IRQs picture. The single highest-leverage feature for the LAN-served multi-client inference rig (a top-3 homelab pattern) that today nothing in the OSS Linux ecosystem surfaces cleanly. First : `modules/nic_queue_affinity.py` + GET `/api/nic-queue-affinity`. |
| 40.5 | **`/proc/sys/net/ipv4/tcp_{congestion_control,available_congestion_control,notsent_lowat,rmem,wmem}` + `/proc/sys/net/core/{rmem_default,wmem_default,netdev_max_backlog}` TCP congestion-control + buffer auditor for LAN/WAN SSE-streamed inference responses (BBR vs cubic vs reno)** | XS | 4 | When llama-server / ollama / vllm streams a 4000-token response back over a TCP socket via SSE (Server-Sent Events) to a remote client (OpenWebUI on the user's laptop across the LAN, or a phone via Tailscale at coffee-shop latency, or a Discord bot serving a remote user), the *kernel's* choice of TCP congestion-control algorithm dictates how quickly the SSE stream's many small (50-200 B per token) packets ramp up — and how quickly the connection recovers from a packet drop. The default on most Linux distros is `cubic` (BIC-derived, ramp-up tuned for bulk transfer, *terrible* for many-small-packets SSE streams: a 50-token-per-second inference response on a 5 ms RTT LAN sees ~3-8 % throughput hit vs `bbr`; on a 60 ms RTT Tailscale link the same response sees 15-25 % throughput hit and *visible* token-stutter to the user). Linux ships `bbr` (Google's bottleneck-bandwidth-and-RTT algorithm, optimal for low-RTT streaming + WAN) in `/proc/sys/net/ipv4/tcp_available_congestion_control` as a module (`modprobe tcp_bbr`) but doesn't enable it by default. Shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` audits net.core / net.ipv4 buffer-size knobs (`rmem_max`, `wmem_max`, `tcp_rmem`, `tcp_wmem`) — but stops short of the *congestion-control choice*, which is the single highest-leverage TCP tuning knob for SSE-streaming inference. Read `/proc/sys/net/ipv4/tcp_congestion_control` (current default for new sockets — `cubic` / `bbr` / `reno` / `vegas` / `dctcp` / `htcp`), `tcp_available_congestion_control` (loaded algos — `bbr` may or may not be in there depending on `lsmod | grep tcp_bbr`), `tcp_notsent_lowat` (default 4 GB ≈ unbounded — too high for low-latency SSE; setting to 16384 = 16 KB caps how much un-ACKed data the kernel buffers, reducing token-arrival jitter), `tcp_low_latency` (deprecated since 4.14 but some old guides still recommend it), `net.core.rmem_default` / `wmem_default` (per-socket starting buffer, default 212992 = 208 KB — fine for SSE), `netdev_max_backlog` (1000 default, bump to 5000 on multi-NIC boxes), classify into 4 verdicts: (a) `bbr` already default → optimal, congratulate, (b) `cubic` default + `bbr` module not loaded → suggest `modprobe tcp_bbr` + `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-tcp.conf` snippet to set `net.ipv4.tcp_congestion_control=bbr` + `net.core.default_qdisc=fq` (BBR's required pacing qdisc), (c) `reno` (legacy fallback, rare but seen on minimal Alpine setups) → flag with "you're losing 10-30 % SSE throughput, switch to bbr", (d) `bbr` module loaded but default still `cubic` → user partially set up, finish the migration. Surface "tcp_congestion_control=cubic (Ubuntu default), bbr module not loaded → enable BBR for ~10-30 % faster SSE token streaming on WAN / Tailscale clients: `modprobe tcp_bbr && echo 'tcp_bbr' | sudo tee /etc/modules-load.d/bbr.conf` then drop `net.ipv4.tcp_congestion_control=bbr` + `net.core.default_qdisc=fq` into `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-tcp.conf` — paste-ready snippet". Pairs with shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` (buffer sizes), just-shipped `sysctl_d_audit.py` (drift protection for the new sysctl.d file), and angle 40.4 (NIC-queue side) for the complete "what is the TCP path from your llama-server to your phone" story. Tiniest scope, biggest "explains the recurring 'why does streaming feel jittery on cellular' phone-user complaint" payoff with a one-line `modprobe` fix. First : `modules/tcp_bbr_advisor.py` + GET `/api/tcp-bbr-advisor`. |
| 40.6 | **`/proc/<pid>/ns/{mnt,uts,ipc,net,pid,user,cgroup,time}` + per-process namespace inventory + container-vs-host disambiguator for inference workers (Docker / Podman / LXC / systemd-nspawn / Bubblewrap detection)** | S | 4 | The recurring "is my llama-server *really* running in a container or did `docker compose down` leave it on the host" diagnostic confusion: on a modern homelab box, ollama may be running natively under systemd, *or* in a Docker container (the official `ollama/ollama` image), *or* in a Podman rootless container, *or* in a LXC container on Proxmox VE, *or* in a systemd-nspawn ephemeral container, *or* in a Bubblewrap sandbox (flatpak'd LM Studio). Each of these has different implications for: (1) which `/proc/<pid>/limits` apply (the container's, not the host's), (2) which `/proc/sys/*` knobs are effective (kernel namespacing for net/ipc/uts/time-ns but NOT for most `vm.*` and `kernel.*` knobs — they're host-global despite the container illusion), (3) which `/sys/class/*` paths the process sees (often shadowed by container runtime overlays), (4) whether shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity's `taskset` advice will actually work (depends on cgroup cpuset.cpus + namespace isolation level). Today shipped `container_audit.py` enumerates *host-visible* container runtimes (docker daemon running, podman socket open, lxc-ls output) — but doesn't walk *per-process namespace membership* to answer "for *this specific* PID, what namespaces does it live in, and is it isolated from the host"? Each `/proc/<pid>/ns/<nsname>` is a symlink to `<nsname>:[<inode>]` — comparing the inode against PID 1's namespace inode tells you "is this process in the same namespace as init, or in its own?". Walk shipped service-discovery inference PIDs (llama-server, ollama, vllm, jupyter, koboldcpp), for each PID list all 8 namespace inodes (mnt, uts, ipc, net, pid, user, cgroup, time), compare each against PID 1's inode, count "namespaces-different-from-host" (0=fully native, 8=fully isolated container, 1-7=partially-namespaced like systemd-nspawn or Bubblewrap), cross-reference with shipped `container_audit.py` + just-shipped `host_class.py` (which already classifies the host as containerized or not), classify each inference worker into 6 buckets: (a) fully native (all 8 nsinodes match PID 1) → host-level tuning applies, (b) Docker/Podman full-container (all 8 differ, user-ns matches Docker's pattern) → use container-level limits, (c) LXC partial (cgroup/pid/mnt differ, net+ipc may match host) → check host-vs-container intent, (d) systemd-nspawn (boot=1, all 8 differ, user-ns shows the +100000 offset typical of nspawn) → ephemeral-container intent, (e) Bubblewrap sandbox (mnt+user differ only) → flatpak app, (f) WSL2 hostside passthrough (time-ns differs, all others match) → quirky. Surface "your ollama PID 12834 is in 3 namespaces different from host (mnt + net + cgroup) → Docker container; shipped #37.2's taskset advice writes against /sys/devices outside the container — apply *inside* with `docker exec ollama taskset -c <cores>` instead, paste-ready snippet" + "your llama-server PID 9912 is fully native (0 namespaces differ from PID 1) → host-level tuning applies directly, shipped #31.3's `taskset` advice works as-is". Cleans up the "did my tuning recipe even apply to the inference worker" confusion for the meaningful slice of homelab users running ollama/vllm in containers. Cross-references shipped `container_audit.py` (host-side runtime inventory), just-shipped `host_class.py` (form-factor classifier), shipped service-discovery, shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity (pinning). First : `modules/proc_ns_inventory.py` + GET `/api/proc-ns-inventory`. |
| 40.7 | **`/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpufreq/policy*/{related_cpus,affected_cpus,scaling_driver,scaling_available_governors}` per-policy cpufreq-domain map for surgical per-domain governor switching on multi-CCD Zen + heterogenous Intel** | XS | 4 | On Zen3/4/5 with multiple CCDs (7950X / 7900X / 9950X / EPYC), and on Alder/Raptor/Meteor Lake P+E hybrids, the kernel exposes one `cpufreq/policy*` directory *per frequency domain* — and the relationship "logical CPU → policy" is non-trivial: on a Zen4 7950X both CCDs typically share one cpufreq policy (one DVFS domain across both CCDs), but on Sapphire Rapids each L3 tile is its own policy, and on Alder Lake the P-cluster + E-cluster are typically separate policies. The `related_cpus` file lists "the set of CPUs whose frequency is coupled by hardware to this policy" (i.e. if you change one, all change), `affected_cpus` lists "the subset of related_cpus that are currently online and using this policy" — together they tell you "if I set `scaling_governor=performance` on policy 0, which physical cores will actually be locked at max?". Shipped #31.3 cpu_topology shows SMT siblings + governor *globally*, shipped #36.4 hwp_epp covers the EPP string-mode value *per policy*, and shipped #37.4 cpu_cache_topology shows L3 islands — but none of them surfaces the cpufreq-domain map per se, which is the missing piece for the "I want to set `performance` on the cores I'm pinning to inference and leave `powersave` everywhere else for ~30 W idle savings" hybrid-tuning use case. Walk `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpufreq/policy*/`, read `related_cpus` + `affected_cpus` + `scaling_driver` (`intel_pstate` / `amd_pstate_epp` / `acpi_cpufreq`) + `scaling_available_governors` (typically `performance` `powersave` for intel_pstate, much richer set `conservative` `ondemand` `userspace` `powersave` `performance` `schedutil` for acpi_cpufreq), build a graph "policy → {related cores} → driver → governor choices", cross-reference shipped #37.4 cpu_cache_topology (L3 island map) + shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity (PCIe-local cores) + shipped #31.3, classify into 4 patterns: (a) single policy spanning all cores (Zen3 monolithic 5950X, Ryzen 5xx0 family) → governor switch is global, no per-domain advice, (b) two policies (Zen4 multi-CCD with one policy per CCD, rare but seen; Sapphire Rapids 2-tile) → recipe "set policy 0 (inference cores) to performance, policy 1 (background) to powersave", (c) P+E split (Alder/Raptor) → recipe "set P-cluster to performance, E-cluster to powersave", (d) `intel_pstate` HWP-active mode where governor choice is limited to {`performance`, `powersave`} but EPP string adds granularity → cross-reference shipped #36.4 hwp_epp for the second axis. Surface "your 14900K has 3 cpufreq policies: P-cores 0-15 on policy 0, E-cluster 0 (cores 16-19) on policy 1, E-cluster 1 (cores 20-23) on policy 2; set policy 0 governor=performance for inference + keep policies 1+2 on powersave for ~25 W idle savings + 0 % perf cost — paste-ready `for p in 0; do echo performance > /sys/devices/system/cpu/cpufreq/policy$p/scaling_governor; done` snippet + systemd Drop-In". Pairs with shipped #35.1 cpu_boost (turbo gate), #36.4 hwp_epp (string-mode preference), #37.4 cpu_cache_topology (L3 islands), #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity (PCIe-local cores), and shipped #31.3 cpu_topology to deliver the surgical "set inference cores to performance, background cores to powersave" recipe that the audience reading shipped #31.3 + #37.4 has been asking for. Niche but very-high-satisfaction-per-feature for the multi-CCD / hybrid-Intel slice. First : `modules/cpufreq_domains.py` + GET `/api/cpufreq-domains`. |
| 40.8 | **`/sys/firmware/acpi/tables/{DSDT,SSDT*,MCFG,APIC,MADT,SRAT,SLIT,HMAT}` + `/sys/firmware/acpi/tables/data/HMAT-*` ACPI table inventory + memory-distance / heterogeneous-memory-attribute decoder for surfacing BIOS-level NUMA-distance / memory-tier mistakes** | M | 3 | Linux exposes raw ACPI tables (binary blobs, but with parseable headers) at `/sys/firmware/acpi/tables/` — including some that are *load-bearing* for inference performance on multi-socket / multi-CCD / CXL-attached-memory rigs and that no OSS tool surfaces cleanly today: (1) SRAT (System Resource Affinity Table) declares which CPUs and memory ranges belong to which NUMA node, which the kernel uses to build `/sys/devices/system/node/node*/` — if SRAT lies (some BIOSes do, especially on Bergamo + EPYC's NPS4 setting + on Intel SPR with NUMA-on-CXL features misconfigured), the kernel's NUMA view is wrong and shipped #35.3 numa_placement advice is wrong-by-extension, (2) SLIT (System Locality Information Table) is a NUMA-distance matrix — `numactl --hardware` shows distances like `node 0 to node 1 = 21` (close, same socket) vs `node 0 to node 2 = 41` (cross-socket); when SLIT distances are unset / all-equal, the kernel can't tier memory and shipped numa_placement's cross-NUMA advice is meaningless, (3) HMAT (Heterogeneous Memory Attribute Table, kernel ≥ 5.6) declares CXL.mem / Optane / persistent-memory bandwidth + latency per memory range — only fully-populated on Sapphire Rapids + Granite Rapids with CXL.mem, but increasingly relevant for homelab as cheap CXL memory expanders appear, (4) MADT (Multiple APIC Description Table) lists the box's IO-APIC / LAPIC configuration that gates which IRQs the kernel can route — relevant when shipped #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity advice fails because MADT only declares 1 IO-APIC on a multi-socket box. The first 36 bytes of each ACPI table follow a standard header (signature, length, revision, checksum, OEM ID, table ID, creator ID, creator revision) — stdlib `struct.unpack` parses cleanly. Walk `/sys/firmware/acpi/tables/`, parse each table header (no need to decode the full AML for DSDT/SSDT — header alone tells you "this BIOS has 12 SSDTs, total ACPI binary footprint 89 KB"), specifically read + decode SRAT, SLIT, HMAT into structured Python: SRAT's proximity-domain-to-CPU and proximity-domain-to-memory-range entries, SLIT's NxN distance matrix, HMAT's bandwidth/latency per (initiator, target) pair, cross-reference with shipped #35.3 numa_placement, classify into 4 verdicts: (a) SRAT + SLIT both present, distances reasonable (cross-socket ~21, cross-CCD ~12, same-CCD ~10), HMAT absent (no CXL) → standard multi-socket box, kernel NUMA view is correct, (b) SLIT all-equal (10/10/10 across all nodes) → BIOS lies, kernel can't tier, NUMA-aware tuning is futile, suggest BIOS check, (c) SRAT declares 4 nodes but `/sys/devices/system/node/` shows only 2 → kernel didn't honor SRAT (rare, usually a kernel-cmdline `numa=off` left over), check `/proc/cmdline`, (d) HMAT present with CXL.mem → tiered-memory rig, surface the bandwidth/latency tiers as info ("you have 64 GB DRAM @ 80 GB/s + 256 GB CXL.mem @ 32 GB/s — your llama-server should use `numactl --membind=0,1` to stay on DRAM tier, NOT the CXL tier which is for cold-storage of weights only"). Niche audience (multi-socket / multi-CCD / CXL-curious homelabbers — overlaps with the shipped #35.3 + #37.2 + #37.4 audience plus the small "I have a Sapphire Rapids workstation with CXL.mem because I picked one up cheap on eBay" slice). Effort is M because ACPI table parsing requires careful binary handling, but stdlib + struct + jsonschema suffice — no extra deps. Pairs with shipped numa_placement, just-shipped host_class (skips parsing on container guests where ACPI is hidden), shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity. The cleanest "the kernel is lying to you because the BIOS is lying to it" advanced diagnostic for the audience that has already exhausted shipped #35.3 + #37.2 + #37.4 advice. First : `modules/acpi_tables.py` + GET `/api/acpi-tables`. |

**Top 4 (fit × urgency)** :
1. 40.1 GPU PCIe driver-binding inventory + vfio-passthrough / driver-rebind orchestrator — S, fit 5, the single highest-leverage feature for the dual-use homelab user (gaming-VM-by-night, inference-rig-by-day pattern that drives a top-3 GitHub-issue category in every vfio / Proxmox / single-GPU-passthrough tracker) that today nothing in the OSS Linux ecosystem surfaces cleanly with one-click recipes. Walks `/sys/bus/pci/devices/<gpu>/{enable,driver,driver_override}` per nvidia VID + audio + USB-C functions, walks `/sys/bus/pci/drivers/{nvidia,nouveau,vfio-pci}/`, classifies into 6 binding states with paste-ready forward (host→VM full 3-function unbind+vfio-pci bind), reverse (VM→host nvidia bind + persistence_mode on), and recovery (stuck-GPU PCI remove + rescan) scripts — each gated behind a clear "this WILL drop all running CUDA contexts" warning. Pairs with shipped `vfio_sentinel.py` (watches *whether* vfio is bound where it shouldn't be), shipped `gpu_reset.py` (nvidia-side reset paths), shipped `d3cold_policy.py` + `persistence_mode.py`, shipped `iommu_groups.py` for the complete "what does this box do with its GPU's PCIe binding" picture. Biggest "this feature alone justifies the cycle for the dual-use audience" payoff. First : `modules/gpu_pci_bind.py`.
2. 40.2 KSM (Kernel Same-page Merging) detector + LLM-hurts-not-helps advisor — XS, fit 5, the lowest-effort + highest-leverage pick of the cycle: ~6 file reads under `/sys/kernel/mm/ksm/` + per-PID `/proc/<pid>/ksm_stat` walk catches the previously-invisible 3-6 % CPU tax that `ksmd` burns futilely scanning LLM tensor pages on Fedora 37+ / openSUSE / Tumbleweed / Proxmox VE / Ubuntu Server 23.04+ where KSM is enabled by default but `pages_sharing=0` because LLM weight blobs have effectively zero inter-page duplication. Surfaces "KSM enabled but `pages_sharing=0` after 4 h uptime → ksmd is burning CPU scanning your tensors with zero benefit, disable: `systemctl disable --now ksmtuned ksm` (Fedora) or `echo 2 > /sys/kernel/mm/ksm/run` (Ubuntu) — paste-ready snippet" with a clean classification (off / on-with-benefit / on-with-pure-cost / on-because-Proxmox-VM-host). Cross-references shipped #31.1 thp_audit (sibling scanning kthread), shipped #35.3 numa_placement (NUMA boundary that KSM violates by default), just-shipped `host_class.py` (Proxmox host context). Tiniest effort, biggest "I had no idea ksmd was eating 4 % of my CPU for nothing" eye-opener. First : `modules/ksm_advisor.py`.
3. 40.3 deeper VM tuning auditor (page-cluster + watermark_scale_factor + vfs_cache_pressure + min_free_kbytes + zone_reclaim_mode) — S, fit 5, the missing "your 32-GB host running a 24-GB quant is *just* barely fitting, and the kernel's reclaim policy is one transient memory spike away from a direct-reclaim stall or OOM-kill" diagnostic that today shipped `vm_sysctl_audit.py` (only swappiness + dirty ratios) stops short of. Catches the silent NVMe-swap-readahead-waste (`page-cluster=3` reads 8 random tensor blob pages per swap-in, optimal is 0), the late-kswapd-wake direct-reclaim stalls (`watermark_scale_factor=10` wakes too late on RAM-tight boxes), and the rare-but-load-bearing BIOS-default `zone_reclaim_mode=1` on Dell/HPE servers that conflicts with shipped #35.3 numa_placement. Emits a `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-vm-tuning.conf` snippet that interacts cleanly with just-shipped `sysctl_d_audit.py` for drift-protection. Highest-payoff "I now know how to actually fit 70B-quant on 32 GB without surprise stalls" memory-pressure-tuning win for the RAM-tight-but-VRAM-rich audience. First : `modules/vm_tuning_deep.py`.
4. 40.4 NIC RX/TX queue + RPS/XPS affinity auditor — S, fit 5, the missing "your LAN-served inference rig is silently single-threading SSE through one CPU because RPS is disabled by default on Ubuntu, and that CPU is also where you pinned llama-server per shipped #31.3 advice" diagnostic that today nothing in the OSS Linux ecosystem surfaces cleanly for the LAN-server-multi-client homelab pattern (Home Assistant + OpenWebUI + Discord bots + Continue.dev fan-out). Walks `/sys/class/net/<dev>/queues/{rx-N,tx-N}/{rps_cpus,xps_cpus,rps_flow_cnt,byte_queue_limits}` + per-NIC `{tx_queue_len,mtu,gro_flush_timeout,napi_defer_hard_irqs}`, cross-references shipped #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity (parallel GPU-IRQ story), shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity (PCIe-local cores that may overlap with NIC IRQ cores), shipped `nic_health.py` (link state + speed), classifies into 5 verdicts with a paste-ready RPS-mask + systemd Drop-In oneshot. Completes the "every IRQ source in the box (GPU per #38.4, NIC per 40.4) is placed on a non-inference-pinned core" picture for the most common busy-LAN inference rig. First : `modules/nic_queue_affinity.py`.

Bench (40.5 TCP BBR congestion-control + buffer advisor / 40.6 per-process namespace inventory / 40.7 cpufreq-domain map for surgical per-domain governor / 40.8 ACPI tables SRAT/SLIT/HMAT decoder) for cycles after top 4 lands.

## R&D #39 survey (started 2026-05-23)

8 fresh angles, none in shipped list (#6-38), modules tree, or parked bench (27.2/27.5/27.6/27.8 + 28.2/28.3/28.6/28.8 + 29.2/29.4/29.5/29.6 + 30.4/30.6/30.7/30.8 + 31.5/31.6/31.7/31.8 + 32.2/32.6/32.7/32.8 + 33.3/33.5/33.7/33.8 + 34.5/34.6/34.7/34.8 + 35.5/35.6/35.7/35.8 + 36.5/36.6/36.7/36.8 + 37.5/37.6/37.7/37.8 + 38.5/38.6/38.7/38.8). Stdlib + jsonschema only, single-GPU desktops / homelab focus, GUI-controllable from Svelte 5 settings.

| # | Feature | Effort | Fit | Why it matters / first module |
|---|---------|--------|-----|-------|
| 39.1 | **`/proc/cmdline` unusual kernel boot-parameter parser + LLM-rig "should be there but isn't" + "shouldn't be there but is" advisor** | XS | 5 | The single line at `/proc/cmdline` (e.g. `BOOT_IMAGE=/boot/vmlinuz-6.8.0-49-generic root=UUID=… ro quiet splash nvidia-drm.modeset=1 nvidia.NVreg_PreserveVideoMemoryAllocations=1`) is the highest-leverage "what is this box actually doing on every boot" string in all of Linux — and today nothing in the shipped stack parses it. The recurring homelab foot-guns it surfaces: (1) `mitigations=off` *missing* on a single-user LAN-only inference rig (shipped #37.1 cpu_vulns flags the cost, this flags the missing fix), (2) `nvidia-drm.modeset=1` *missing* on Wayland + nvidia 555+ setups → Sway/Hyprland tearing, (3) `nvidia.NVreg_PreserveVideoMemoryAllocations=1` *missing* → suspend/resume blanks VRAM on shipped #suspend_guard's watchlist, (4) `iommu=pt` + `intel_iommu=on` / `amd_iommu=on` *missing* on a vfio-passthrough rig (shipped #vfio_sentinel can't help if IOMMU is off at boot), (5) `pcie_aspm=off` *present* on a homelab box (defeats shipped #28.6 pcie_aspm wake-error mitigations and wastes 5-10 W idle), (6) `isolcpus=` / `nohz_full=` / `rcu_nocbs=` *misconfigured* (intended for low-latency inference, often left as a copy-paste residue from a guide that didn't match the user's core layout — silently strands cores from the scheduler), (7) `transparent_hugepage=never` *present* (defeats shipped #31.1 thp_audit's khugepaged story), (8) `numa_balancing=disable` *present* on a multi-socket box → cross-NUMA penalty bypasses shipped #35.3 numa_placement advice, (9) `acpi=off` / `noapic` / `pci=noaer` *present* → emergency-mode boot leftover that disables IRQ MSI-X (defeats shipped #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity), AER (defeats shipped #28.5 + #38.1 pcie_aer_trend), and modern ACPI thermal (defeats shipped #thermal_zones). Parse `/proc/cmdline` once (one read), tokenise into key=value pairs (handles quoted values, escaped chars), build a curated allowlist of ~40 "expected on a tuned inference rig" params + a denylist of ~25 "you probably didn't mean to leave this on" params, classify each token into 4 verdicts: (a) expected-and-present = OK, (b) expected-but-missing = recommend addition with paste-ready GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT snippet, (c) unexpected-but-harmless = info, (d) unexpected-and-degrading = flag with explanation + removal recipe. Cross-references shipped #29.1 kmod_params (`nvidia.*` cmdline params override modprobe.d), shipped #37.1 cpu_vulns (mitigations interaction), shipped #38.2 modprobe_audit (where the `nvidia.NVreg_*` lives long-term), shipped #28.6 pcie_aspm, shipped #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity, shipped #31.1 thp_audit, shipped #35.3 numa_placement, shipped #suspend_guard. Emit a paste-ready `sudo sed -i 's|GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT=".*"|GRUB_CMDLINE_LINUX_DEFAULT="… mitigations=off nvidia-drm.modeset=1 nvidia.NVreg_PreserveVideoMemoryAllocations=1 …"|' /etc/default/grub && sudo update-grub` snippet for the union of fixes. The most "explains a dozen forum threads in one diff view" feature per kB of code possible — one read, ~40 lookup-table entries, 4 output cards. First : `modules/cmdline_audit.py` + GET `/api/cmdline-audit`. |
| 39.2 | **`/etc/sysctl.d/*.conf` + `/usr/lib/sysctl.d/*.conf` + `/run/sysctl.d/*.conf` on-disk vs runtime sysctl drift detector** (parallel to shipped #38.2 modprobe_audit, for sysctl knobs) | S | 5 | Shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` + `vm_sysctl_audit.py` audit the *runtime* values of `/proc/sys/net/*` and `/proc/sys/vm/*` and emit `sysctl.conf` snippets — but neither walks the on-disk `sysctl.d/*.conf` config-merge tree to detect "I pasted the snippet 3 weeks ago, will it actually survive a reboot, and is something else silently overriding it?" Linux's sysctl-merge order is (lowest to highest precedence): `/usr/lib/sysctl.d/` < `/run/sysctl.d/` < `/etc/sysctl.d/` < `/etc/sysctl.conf`, alphabetical within each dir, applied by `systemd-sysctl.service` on boot. The recurring foot-guns: (1) user pastes `vm.swappiness=10` into `/etc/sysctl.conf` but `/etc/sysctl.d/10-cloudimg-settings.conf` (Ubuntu cloud-init residue) re-sets it to 60 at boot because last-applied wins, (2) two snippets in `/etc/sysctl.d/` (`50-default.conf` + `99-llm.conf`) both touch `net.core.rmem_max` with conflicting values, alphabetical-last `99-` wins but the user thinks `50-` is authoritative, (3) `procps`/`systemd-sysctl` package upgrade rewrites `/usr/lib/sysctl.d/99-protect-links.conf` and silently re-enables `fs.protected_hardlinks=1` that defeats a user's container-data-symlink workflow, (4) a sysctl in `/etc/sysctl.d/` syntactically valid but the parameter no longer exists in the running kernel (renamed/removed between 5.x and 6.x) — applied silently with no error, the user thinks it's working, (5) `/run/sysctl.d/` overrides everything in `/etc/` and is recreated on every boot from a tmpfiles snippet the user forgot about. Walk `/usr/lib/sysctl.d/*.conf` + `/run/sysctl.d/*.conf` + `/etc/sysctl.d/*.conf` + `/etc/sysctl.conf` in merge order (alphabetical within each tier), parse `key = value` directives (handle comments, line continuations, `-key` ignore-error syntax), compute the effective "if I rebooted right now" union per key, snapshot the *current* runtime `/proc/sys/<key>` value for every key found, classify into 5 drift verdicts: (a) on-disk = runtime = OK, (b) on-disk says X but runtime says Y → `sysctl --system` not applied since edit, run it, (c) two `.conf` files set the same key with conflicting values, last-alphabetical wins → flag the precedence trap with the resolved value + the silently-overridden ones, (d) `.conf` references a key that doesn't exist in `/proc/sys/` → typo or kernel removed the knob, flag with the `find /proc/sys -name "<basename>"` resolver hint, (e) `/run/sysctl.d/` content present but no corresponding tmpfiles `.conf` in `/etc/tmpfiles.d/` → mystery override, flag for investigation. Cross-references shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` + `vm_sysctl_audit.py` (runtime view per subtree) for a complete "what sysctl values will this box use on every boot, and are they future-proofed against package upgrades?" answer — the sysctl-side equivalent of shipped #38.2's modprobe-side answer. Emit "your `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm.conf` sets `vm.swappiness=10` but `/etc/sysctl.d/10-cloudimg-settings.conf` re-applies `vm.swappiness=60` last (alphabetical-last wins, but `99-` should win — check that the file actually exists with `ls -la /etc/sysctl.d/`), runtime is 60". First : `modules/sysctl_d_audit.py` + GET `/api/sysctl-d-audit`. |
| 39.3 | **`/proc/<pid>/coredump_filter` + `/proc/sys/kernel/core_pattern` + `/proc/sys/kernel/core_uses_pid` coredump-readiness auditor for crash-debugging inference workers** | XS | 4 | When a llama-server / ollama / vllm process segfaults on a CUDA error 700 (illegal memory access) or hits a GGML assertion deep in a quantised matmul, the user's *only* post-mortem signal is a core dump — and on every modern distro it silently doesn't get written. The pipeline has six independent gates the user must pass: (1) `ulimit -c unlimited` (shipped #rlimit_audit catches this), (2) `/proc/sys/kernel/core_pattern` must not be `|/usr/share/apport/apport …` (Ubuntu) or `|/usr/lib/systemd/systemd-coredump …` with a `Storage=none` in `/etc/systemd/coredump.conf` (Fedora minimal), or `|/dev/null` (some servers), (3) `/proc/sys/fs/suid_dumpable=0` (default) blocks dumps for setuid binaries — usually irrelevant, but llama-server with `CAP_SYS_NICE` ambient capabilities counts, (4) the per-PID `/proc/<pid>/coredump_filter` bitmask (default `0x33`) must include the *anonymous private* + *anonymous shared* + *huge private* bits for a useful dump of an inference worker whose tensors live in private anon mappings (default 0x33 happens to cover this, but containers and `prctl(PR_SET_DUMPABLE)` calls override it), (5) destination filesystem must have free space (a 64 GB VRAM model with a full host RAM dump is ~150 GB, and `systemd-coredump` truncates by default at `ProcessSizeMax=2G`), (6) compression / format / journal storage policy in `/etc/systemd/coredump.conf` (`Storage=external`, `Compress=yes`, `ProcessSizeMax=`, `ExternalSizeMax=`). Read `/proc/sys/kernel/core_pattern`, `/proc/sys/kernel/core_uses_pid`, `/proc/sys/fs/suid_dumpable`, walk shipped service-discovery PIDs and read each `/proc/<pid>/coredump_filter`, decode the bitmask into named flags (anon_private/anon_shared/file_private/file_shared/elf_headers/huge_private/huge_shared/dax_private/dax_shared), parse `/etc/systemd/coredump.conf` + `/etc/systemd/coredump.conf.d/*.conf` for `Storage`/`Compress`/`ProcessSizeMax`/`ExternalSizeMax`, classify into 4 readiness states: (a) `systemd-coredump` active + `Storage=external` + filter includes anon bits + ulimit unlimited → ready, expect dumps in `/var/lib/systemd/coredump/`, (b) `apport` pattern but apport service disabled or unconfigured for non-apt processes → silent black hole, paste-ready disable+`systemd-coredump` install recipe, (c) `core_pattern=core` (legacy) + cwd at `/` (systemd unit) → dumps land in `/` and silently fail, (d) `Storage=none` Fedora-minimal style → flag, emit `Storage=external` Drop-In. Surface "core_pattern=|/usr/share/apport/apport but apport.service is disabled → your llama-server crashes will leave no trace; either re-enable apport or switch to systemd-coredump with the Drop-In below + bump `ProcessSizeMax=10G` because your model's RSS is 8.2 GB". Niche but high-payoff "I have no idea why ollama died last Tuesday" diagnostic; pairs with shipped `nvrm_tail` (XID / nvrm dmesg logs) + shipped #xid_decoder (CUDA error decode) + shipped #rlimit_audit (RLIMIT_CORE) for a complete post-mortem-readiness story. First : `modules/coredump_ready.py` + GET `/api/coredump-ready`. |
| 39.4 | **`/sys/devices/virtual/dmi/id/chassis_type` + `/sys/firmware/qemu_fw_cfg/` + `/sys/class/dmi/id/{sys_vendor,product_name,board_name,bios_vendor}` virtualization + form-factor classifier for adaptive Settings UI gating** | XS | 4 | The dashboard today shows the *same* Settings → Integrations panel to every user regardless of whether they're running on bare-metal Threadripper desktop, a Proxmox VM with vfio-passthrough GPU, a LXC container on a Synology NAS, a WSL2 environment with `nvidia-container-toolkit`, or a laptop with an eGPU dock — and many cards are irrelevant or actively misleading in some contexts (BIOS update card on a VM, fan-curve card on a passthrough GPU where the host owns the fan, suspend/resume card on a server with no S3 path, UPS card on a laptop with a battery, watchdog card on a container that can't access `/dev/watchdog0`). Six sysfs reads classify the host with high confidence: `/sys/devices/virtual/dmi/id/chassis_type` (3=Desktop, 4=LowProfileDesktop, 6=MiniTower, 7=Tower, 9=Laptop, 10=Notebook, 14=SubNotebook, 17=MainServerChassis, 23=RackMount, 28=BladeEnclosure, 31=Convertible, 32=Detachable), `/sys/firmware/qemu_fw_cfg/` (present-or-not = QEMU guest, can read `/etc/system-status` etc. for richer detection), `/sys/class/dmi/id/sys_vendor` (`QEMU` / `Microsoft Corporation` for Hyper-V / WSL / `VMware, Inc.` / `innotek GmbH` for VirtualBox / `Xen` / `Parallels` / `Bochs` / actual mobo OEM), `/sys/class/dmi/id/product_name` (Synology / TrueNAS / Proxmox VE leaves fingerprints), `/sys/class/dmi/id/bios_vendor` (`SeaBIOS` / `EDK II` / `Phoenix` / `AMI`), `/proc/cpuinfo` `hypervisor` flag (kvm/vmware/microsoft/xen/qemu), plus `/proc/1/cgroup` (containerd / docker / lxc / systemd-nspawn signatures). Classify into 6 form-factors: (a) bare-metal desktop, (b) bare-metal laptop (eGPU likely), (c) bare-metal server / rack, (d) KVM/QEMU guest with passthrough GPU (read `/sys/firmware/qemu_fw_cfg/` to confirm), (e) WSL2 / Hyper-V guest (sys_vendor=Microsoft + chassis_type=0 + `WSL_INTEROP` env), (f) LXC/Docker container (cgroup signatures, `/proc/1/sched` shows non-init pid), each with a *gating profile* that maps to {hide, show, show-with-warning} per Settings card. Push the classification once at startup into shipped Settings → Integrations registry, auto-hide irrelevant cards (BIOS update on VMs, fan-curve on containers, suspend on rack servers, watchdog on containers), auto-warn on lossy cards (e.g. "you're in WSL2, `/proc/<pid>/stat` field 7 (TTY) is 0 for everything, shipped #37.5 sched_autogroup advice may be inaccurate"). Pairs with parked-bench 38.8 (chassis_type + battery, narrower scope) by widening it to a full virtualization-and-form-factor classifier that gates the *entire* UI surface. Niche but very-high-satisfaction-per-feature: cleans up the new-user "what is half this stuff and why is it shown to me on my Proxmox VM" complaint. First : `modules/host_class.py` + GET `/api/host-class`. |
| 39.5 | **`/sys/class/block/*/queue/{discard_max_bytes,discard_granularity,discard_zeroes_data}` + `/etc/fstab` discard / `fstrim.timer` TRIM-pipeline auditor for NVMe model storage + swap longevity** | S | 4 | Modern homelab inference rigs store 200-2000 GB of GGUF / safetensors weights on a single consumer-grade NVMe (Samsung 980 Pro, WD SN850X, Crucial T705) — and the SSD's sustained read performance + endurance both degrade catastrophically without working TRIM, which has *four* independent moving parts that all must align: (1) the SSD itself must report `discard_max_bytes > 0` (some cheap PCIe Gen3 NVMe and almost all USB-NVMe enclosures with cheap JMS583/RTL9210 bridges advertise 0 = no TRIM), (2) the mount option chain must propagate discard intent (`discard` in `/etc/fstab` for inline-TRIM, or `noatime,nodiratime` + nothing for batched-TRIM-via-`fstrim`), (3) `fstrim.timer` must be enabled in systemd (Debian/Ubuntu default ON since 19.04, Fedora ON, but Arch + many minimal/headless setups OFF), (4) for swap on NVMe (a homelab pattern when running 70B-quant models with a 32 GB RAM mobo), `/etc/fstab` swap line must include `discard=once` or `discard=pages` or the SSD's swap-area sectors silently never get TRIMmed and the SSD's FTL endurance burns 3-5× faster. Foot-guns: (a) `/etc/fstab` has `discard` mount option on a kernel ≥ 5.4 where inline `discard` is *worse* than `fstrim.timer` (every file delete triggers a TRIM, stalls the queue) — shipped `nvme_iosched` doesn't surface this, (b) `fstrim.timer` enabled but the SSD reports `discard_max_bytes=0` (USB-NVMe enclosure or fake-NVMe DRAM-less drive) → timer fires weekly with zero effect, user thinks they're protected, (c) ZFS / btrfs RAID on top of NVMe with discard not propagated through the filesystem layer (btrfs needs `discard=async` on kernel ≥ 5.6, ZFS needs `zpool autotrim=on`), (d) swap on NVMe with no `discard=` flag — most-common silent foot-gun. Walk `/sys/class/block/nvme*n*/queue/{discard_max_bytes,discard_granularity,discard_zeroes_data,rotational}` for every NVMe namespace + `/sys/class/block/sd*/queue/discard_max_bytes` for SATA SSDs, parse `/etc/fstab` for mount-option + swap-line discard intent, parse `/proc/swaps` for active swap devices, query systemd for `fstrim.timer` state + `OnCalendar` schedule + last activation (`systemctl show fstrim.timer --property=LastTriggerUSec`), cross-reference shipped `nvme_iosched` (I/O scheduler choice impacts TRIM coalescing), `nvme_swap` (the swap-on-NVMe sentinel), `disk_health` (SMART endurance % left), classify into 4 audit states: (a) NVMe with `discard_max_bytes>0` + `fstrim.timer enabled` + swap-line has `discard=once` → optimal, (b) USB-NVMe with `discard_max_bytes=0` → flag, advise SATA-style mount instead, (c) inline `discard` in `/etc/fstab` on kernel ≥ 5.4 → recommend switch to `fstrim.timer`, (d) swap on NVMe with no discard flag → flag with paste-ready `/etc/fstab` swap-line patch. Emit "your `/etc/fstab` swap line `UUID=… none swap sw 0 0` is missing `discard=once` — every page swapped out and freed leaves dead blocks the SSD never reclaims, ~3-5× endurance penalty over time; change to `UUID=… none swap sw,discard=once 0 0`". Niche but high-leverage longevity audit for the 70B-on-32-GB-host pattern. First : `modules/trim_pipeline.py` + GET `/api/trim-pipeline`. |
| 39.6 | **`/sys/kernel/debug/tracing/available_tracers` + `/sys/kernel/tracing/current_tracer` + `/sys/kernel/debug/tracing/events/` ftrace / eBPF tracing readiness auditor for CUDA / NVRM / GPU-IRQ kernel-side debugging** | S | 3 | When the user's GPU mysteriously XIDs once a week, the *correct* answer is to leave an ftrace `irq:irq_handler_entry` + `nvidia:*` events filter armed in the background and inspect after the fact — but every distro ships with a maze of ftrace toggles in 4 different parent directories (`/sys/kernel/debug/tracing/`, `/sys/kernel/tracing/`, `/sys/kernel/debug/dynamic_debug/`, `/proc/dynamic_debug/`), some of which require `CONFIG_FTRACE=y`, `CONFIG_FUNCTION_TRACER=y`, `CONFIG_KPROBE_EVENTS=y`, `CONFIG_UPROBE_EVENTS=y`, `CONFIG_BPF_SYSCALL=y` in the running kernel — and `/proc/sys/kernel/perf_event_paranoid` (parked #37.7) ALSO gates some of them. Today shipped #nvrm_tail tails the kernel ring buffer for nvidia XID messages after-the-fact, shipped #xid_decoder decodes the codes — but neither tells the user "you have ftrace available right now, here's the one-liner that captures the next XID's preceding kernel events so you can actually root-cause it". Read `/sys/kernel/debug/tracing/available_tracers` (lists `function`, `function_graph`, `nop`, `mmiotrace`, `irqsoff`, `preemptoff`, `wakeup`, `wakeup_rt` — presence tells you which CONFIG_* features the running kernel supports), `current_tracer` (is anything tracing right now? — high CPU cost if accidentally left on), enumerate `/sys/kernel/debug/tracing/events/` subdirs (irq/, sched/, nvidia/, pci/, power/, mce/) + per-event `enable` files (1=enabled), classify into 4 readiness verdicts: (a) full ftrace + nvidia events available + nothing currently tracing → ready, emit ready-to-arm one-liner for XID capture, (b) ftrace available but `nvidia/` events subdir missing → nvidia.ko built without TRACE_EVENT hooks (older driver), suggest 580+ upgrade, (c) `current_tracer != nop` → something is tracing in the background (forgotten? high CPU? — surface what), (d) `tracefs` not mounted or debugfs locked-down → CONFIG_DEBUG_FS=n or `lockdown=integrity` kernel cmdline (parked #37.6 secureboot_tpm angle), flag with workaround. Cross-references shipped `nvrm_tail` (where the after-the-fact trace lands), shipped `xid_decoder` (decoder for what the trace captured), and parked #37.7 perf_event_paranoid (sibling toggle). Surface "your kernel has 47 nvidia/ trace events available, none currently armed; the next time GPU 0 XID-69's, you'll get only the dmesg line — arm `echo 1 > /sys/kernel/debug/tracing/events/nvidia/enable && echo 1 > /sys/kernel/debug/tracing/tracing_on` now to capture context for the next XID, output rotates into `/sys/kernel/debug/tracing/trace_pipe` — here's a paste-ready 5-line systemd-service that captures + rotates daily for the next ftrace-curious investigator". Niche audience (~5 % of homelab — ftrace-curious users with recurring XIDs) but the *single* highest-payoff "next time it breaks I'll have data" reliability feature for that slice. First : `modules/ftrace_ready.py` + GET `/api/ftrace-ready`. |
| 39.7 | **`/proc/sys/kernel/kexec_load_disabled` + `/proc/sys/kernel/dmesg_restrict` + `/sys/kernel/security/lockdown` + `/proc/sys/kernel/kptr_restrict` kernel-hardening posture auditor for "what does this user actually have access to debug with"** | XS | 3 | A recurring frustration on Ubuntu 22.04+ / Fedora 39+ / Pop!_OS 22.04+: the user tries to read `dmesg` to investigate a GPU XID, hits `dmesg: read kernel buffer failed: Operation not permitted` because `/proc/sys/kernel/dmesg_restrict=1` (default since Ubuntu 12.04 but more aggressively enforced since 22.04), tries `cat /proc/kallsyms` for a stack trace, gets all-zero addresses because `kptr_restrict=1`, tries to `kexec -l` a debug kernel to capture a crash dump, fails silently because `kexec_load_disabled=1` (set automatically when Secure Boot is on or `lockdown=integrity` is in `/proc/cmdline`), tries to load a debug module, fails because `lockdown=confidentiality` mode forbids `init_module` syscall entirely. Shipped #30.8 reads `/sys/kernel/security/lockdown` and emits a one-line "lockdown is `<value>`" — but doesn't surface the *consequences* (what specifically can the user no longer do?) or the related four sibling toggles. The dashboard itself runs as a regular user via systemd-system or systemd-user, and a clear-eyed "here's what debugging surface this user has access to right now" card pre-empts the "the dashboard says XID 69 happened but `dmesg` gave me nothing" complaint. Read `/proc/sys/kernel/dmesg_restrict` (0=anyone can read kernel ringbuffer, 1=root-only), `/proc/sys/kernel/kexec_load_disabled` (1=irreversibly disabled this boot — once on, only a reboot clears it), `/proc/sys/kernel/kptr_restrict` (0=show ptrs to everyone, 1=root-only, 2=hide always), `/sys/kernel/security/lockdown` (`none` / `integrity` / `confidentiality`), `/proc/sys/kernel/yama/ptrace_scope` (0=any process, 1=child-only Ubuntu default, 2=admin-only, 3=disabled — blocks `gdb -p` + `py-spy` + `strace -p`), `/proc/sys/kernel/modules_disabled` (1=no more module loading this boot), classify into 5 posture levels: (a) Wide Open (all 0, no lockdown) = single-user dev box, full debug access, (b) Ubuntu Desktop Default (dmesg_restrict=1, ptrace_scope=1, kptr_restrict=1, no lockdown) = `sudo dmesg` works, `gdb -p` only on child processes, kallsyms shows 0s, (c) Secure Boot On (lockdown=integrity, kexec_load_disabled=1, modules_disabled=0) = no kexec, signed modules only, (d) Hardened (lockdown=confidentiality, kptr_restrict=2) = no kallsyms at all, (e) Locked Tight (modules_disabled=1 + lockdown=confidentiality) = no new modules until reboot. Cross-references shipped #30.8 lockdown (one-axis), parked #37.7 perf_event_paranoid (profiling subset), shipped #36.3 kernel_taint (taint history). Surface "your dashboard runs as user `gpud`, posture is Ubuntu Desktop Default: `dmesg -k` will fail with EPERM, kallsyms is masked, `gdb -p $(pidof ollama)` will only work for processes you launched yourself; if this is a single-user trusted box, drop `kernel.dmesg_restrict=0` + `kernel.kptr_restrict=0` + `kernel.yama.ptrace_scope=0` into `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm-debug.conf` — paste-ready snippet". Niche but high-payoff "I now know what tools work on this box" eye-opener. First : `modules/kernel_posture.py` + GET `/api/kernel-posture`. |
| 39.8 | **`/sys/devices/system/cpu/*/topology/thread_siblings_list` + `/sys/devices/system/cpu/*/online` per-pair hyper-thread siblings map for surgical SMT disable on inference cores** (extends shipped #35.4 smt_audit with per-pair granularity) | S | 3 | Shipped `smt_audit.py` covers the *global* SMT state (`/sys/devices/system/cpu/smt/control` = `on`/`off`/`forceoff`/`notsupported`, `/sys/devices/system/cpu/smt/active` = 0/1) and emits binary "disable SMT for inference" advice — but on multi-CCD Zen4 (7950X/7900X), multi-tile Sapphire-Rapids, Threadripper, and EPYC, the *right* answer is rarely "all-or-nothing SMT off". The optimal pattern is: keep SMT on globally for the OS / background workers / E-cores, but *surgically* offline the hyper-thread sibling of every P-core that's pinned to llama-server via shipped #31.3's `taskset` advice, so the inference cores get *exclusive* L1/L2 + ALU bandwidth without the 12-15 % SMT-contention penalty, while the rest of the box keeps its parallelism. Today nothing in the OSS Linux ecosystem surfaces *per-core-pair* SMT disable advice (every existing tool is global-only). Walk `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/topology/thread_siblings_list` (each file lists the small set of logical CPUs that share a physical core, e.g. `0,16` for cores 0 and 16 on a 7950X = same physical core) + `/sys/devices/system/cpu/cpu*/online` (1=enabled, 0=offlined via `echo 0 >`), build a graph "physical-core-id → {sibling_lcpus} → online_state", cross-reference shipped #31.3 cpu_topology (which cores does the user's `taskset` recipe pin?) + shipped #37.4 cpu_cache_topology (which cores share L3 with the pinned set?) + shipped #37.2 gpu_cpu_affinity (which cores are PCIe-local?), emit a per-pair "disable lcpu 16-31 only (the high-numbered siblings of P-cores 0-15) for inference workloads, keep them offline while llama-server runs, re-enable after with the paired `echo 1 >` snippet" recipe + a systemd Drop-In oneshot that offlines the sibling set on `llama-server.service` start and re-enables on stop (`ExecStartPre=/usr/local/bin/llm-smt-pair off`, `ExecStopPost=/usr/local/bin/llm-smt-pair on`). Classify into 4 verdicts: (a) SMT globally off (shipped #35.4 already handled) → no advice, (b) SMT on + user is *not* taskset-pinning → suggest #31.3's pin advice first, (c) SMT on + user IS taskset-pinning to some core range + the sibling range is online → here's the surgical offline recipe, (d) SMT on + user has *already* offlined the sibling range → optimal, congratulate. Pairs with shipped #35.4 (the global view), #37.4 (cache topology), #37.2 (GPU↔CPU placement) for the *fifth dimension* (per-core SMT) of the CPU placement multidimensional cube. Niche audience (the SMT-curious slice of multi-CCD homelab — overlaps heavily with shipped #34/35/37 audience), but the cleanest "+12-15 % inference t/s for 20 lines of systemd" hyper-tactical win in the cycle. First : `modules/smt_pair_pin.py` + GET `/api/smt-pair-pin`. |

**Top 4 (fit × urgency)** :
1. 39.1 `/proc/cmdline` unusual kernel boot-parameter parser — XS, fit 5, the lowest-effort + highest-leverage pick of the cycle: one read of `/proc/cmdline` + a curated ~40-entry "expected on a tuned inference rig" allowlist + ~25-entry "you probably didn't mean this" denylist surfaces a dozen recurring homelab foot-guns at once (`mitigations=off` missing, `nvidia-drm.modeset=1` missing on Wayland, `nvidia.NVreg_PreserveVideoMemoryAllocations=1` missing on suspend-prone setups, `pcie_aspm=off` accidentally on, leftover `isolcpus=` from a guide that didn't match the user's core layout, `transparent_hugepage=never` defeating shipped #thp_audit, `numa_balancing=disable` defeating shipped #numa_placement, emergency `acpi=off`/`pci=noaer` residue) — each one a forum thread that today nothing in the OSS Linux ecosystem cross-references against the shipped audit suite in one place. Cross-references shipped #29.1 kmod_params, #37.1 cpu_vulns, #38.2 modprobe_audit, #28.6 pcie_aspm, #38.4 gpu_irq_affinity, #31.1 thp_audit, #35.3 numa_placement, #suspend_guard for a complete "what is this box actually doing on every boot vs what it should be doing" verdict, with a paste-ready `sed -i … && update-grub` snippet for the union of fixes. Biggest "explains a dozen forum threads in one diff view" feature per kB of code possible. First : `modules/cmdline_audit.py`.
2. 39.2 `/etc/sysctl.d/*.conf` on-disk vs runtime drift detector — S, fit 5, the sysctl-side equivalent of shipped #38.2 modprobe_audit, the missing "I pasted `vm.swappiness=10` into `/etc/sysctl.d/99-llm.conf` three weeks ago, will it survive my next `apt upgrade procps`, and is `/etc/sysctl.d/10-cloudimg-settings.conf` silently re-overriding it on every boot?" diagnostic that today shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` + `vm_sysctl_audit.py` (runtime view only) cannot answer. Walks the four-tier sysctl-config-merge order (`/usr/lib` < `/run` < `/etc/sysctl.d/` < `/etc/sysctl.conf`) in alphabetical precedence, parses `key = value` directives (handles comments, line continuations, `-key` ignore-error syntax), computes the effective union per key, diffs against the runtime `/proc/sys/<key>` value, and catches the five recurring foot-guns (cloud-init residue overrides, two-snippets-conflicting-precedence, package-upgrade rewrites, kernel-removed keys silently no-op, `/run/sysctl.d/` mystery overrides). Pairs naturally with shipped `net_sysctl_audit.py` + `vm_sysctl_audit.py` (runtime view per subtree) and shipped #38.2 modprobe_audit (parallel modprobe-side persistence story) for the complete "what sysctl + nvidia.ko parameter values will this box actually use on every boot, and are they future-proofed against package upgrades" answer. Highest-payoff "set it once and forget it" persistence-correctness win for sysctl tuning. First : `modules/sysctl_d_audit.py`.
3. 39.3 `/proc/<pid>/coredump_filter` + `core_pattern` coredump-readiness auditor — XS, fit 4, the missing post-mortem-readiness leg on top of shipped `nvrm_tail` (XID / nvrm log capture), shipped `xid_decoder` (CUDA error decode), and shipped `rlimit_audit` (RLIMIT_CORE PAM-side) — catches the silent "your llama-server segfaulted last Tuesday and you have nothing to inspect" failure mode that hits every Ubuntu user whose `core_pattern=|/usr/share/apport/apport …` pipe silently sends crashes into a disabled apport service, and every Fedora-minimal user whose `Storage=none` in `/etc/systemd/coredump.conf` makes `systemd-coredump` a black hole. Reads six independent gates (`core_pattern`, `core_uses_pid`, `suid_dumpable`, per-PID `coredump_filter` bitmask decoded into named flags, `coredump.conf` Storage/Compress/ProcessSizeMax limits, destination FS free space), classifies into 4 readiness states with paste-ready Drop-In recipes (switch from apport to systemd-coredump, bump `ProcessSizeMax=10G` for 8.2 GB-RSS inference workers). Niche but high-payoff "next time it crashes I'll actually have data" reliability feature; cross-references shipped #nvrm_tail + #xid_decoder + #rlimit_audit for the complete post-mortem story. First : `modules/coredump_ready.py`.
4. 39.4 virtualization + form-factor classifier for adaptive Settings UI gating — XS, fit 4, six sysfs/dmi reads (`chassis_type`, `qemu_fw_cfg/`, `sys_vendor`, `product_name`, `bios_vendor`, `/proc/cpuinfo` hypervisor flag + `/proc/1/cgroup` container signatures) classify the host into 6 form-factors (bare-metal desktop / laptop / server, KVM-QEMU guest with passthrough GPU, WSL2 / Hyper-V, LXC/Docker container) at startup and push the classification into shipped Settings → Integrations registry to auto-hide/show/warn-on cards per host context. Cleans up the new-user "what is half this stuff and why is it shown to me on my Proxmox VM with passthrough GPU" complaint, gates the BIOS / fan-curve / suspend / watchdog / UPS cards on form-factor reality. Wider scope than parked-bench 38.8 (chassis_type + battery only) by extending into virt detection. Pairs with shipped Settings → Integrations registry to deliver the cleanest "the dashboard now knows where it's running" win in the cycle. First : `modules/host_class.py`.

Bench (39.5 NVMe TRIM-pipeline + fstab discard audit / 39.6 ftrace + nvidia tracing readiness / 39.7 kernel-hardening posture auditor / 39.8 per-pair hyper-thread siblings surgical SMT disable) for cycles after top 4 lands.
